**ASSISTANT**:
**Introductory Post**

Hey everyone! My name is George, and I’m excited to dive into soil science with all of you this semester. I’ll be joining you from the heart of Texas, in the vibrant city of Austin. When I’m not out enjoying the rolling hills and unique ecosystems of the Texas Hill Country, I spend my days engaged in environmental work, focusing on soil and tree health. My current projects include assessing heritage live oaks and, more recently, exploring septic system preservation as a way to protect our precious groundwater resources. You could say I’ve got my hands deep in the dirt, both figuratively and literally.

I’m pursuing a degree in Environmental Science, and this course is a perfect fit for my interests. Soil is at the center of everything—ecosystem health, water quality, and even how we interact with our environment as humans. I’m hoping to gain a deeper understanding of soil processes and management to better serve my local community and, hopefully, expand my work to influence larger regenerative projects. Outside of work and studies, I love spending time outdoors with my family and exploring regenerative farming techniques that mimic nature&#x27;s processes.

One thing that stood out to me after reading the syllabus is the focus on soil classifications and their practical implications. I’m particularly interested in soil-water interactions and how soil health impacts water infiltration and storage, especially in our water-scarce region of Texas.

---

**PART 2: Texas Official State Soil: Houston Black Clay**

Texas is a massive state, and our soils are as diverse as our landscapes. Our official soil is the Houston Black series, a Vertisol that covers more than 1.5 million acres across the state, primarily in the Blackland Prairie region. Houston Black Clay is characterized by its rich, dark color and high shrink-swell capacity, meaning it expands when wet and contracts when dry. This unique property can be both a blessing and a curse, contributing to fertile farmland while also causing foundation issues for buildings and roads.

The major human uses of Houston Black Clay include agriculture, where it supports crops like cotton, corn, and sorghum. However, managing this soil requires careful attention to its drainage and erosion potential. Urban development in Texas has faced challenges due to this soil&#x27;s movement, leading to innovative engineering solutions. Here’s a tip for those interested: never underestimate the power of soil structure!

I’ll be sure to include images of the soil profile and a distribution map to bring this discussion to life. 

Looking forward to hearing everyone’s perspectives and learning together this semester! 

#SoilScience #TexasPride

---

Amanda Kerrick posted Nov 4, 2024 7:05 PM
Part 1:

Good evening everyone! My name is Amanda and I am in the Navy currently stationed in Washington State. I am enrolled in the Environmental Science program here and this class is part of my degree program! I am excited for this one because it&#x27;s one of the first ones I am taking that specifically correlates to my degree program and what I want to do when I graduate. When I graduate and separate from Naval service (in 3 years). I have been in the Navy for 8 years and have deployed around the world, mostly Europe and Fifth Fleet, I will be at my 11 year mark when I separate, and would like to do something with my states Department of Energy or Agriculture. It is similar to the job that I currently have in the Navy, which is a meteorologist and oceanographer, still dealing with the environment and the physics behind weather and how the atmosphere (and ocean) work. I have two cats, Leo and Luca. I got Leo when he was a kitten in 2018 (he&#x27;s 6 years old) and I just got my baby Luca this year, they are the best of friends.



Part Two: 

The state soil for Washington is called Tokul Soil. Tokul soil is composed mostly of volcanic ash and loess over glacial till.  It has an organic layer at the surface, due to the coniferous and forest debris. Below that, a dark-brown, organic rich, gravelly loam creates a fertile and well-drained environment for roots to penetrate and thrive. This sub-layer is strongly influenced by volcanic ash, which makes this soil a volcanic soil! Below the beautiful brown-reddish layer of soil (extending about 1-2 feet) you’ll uncover a somewhat cemented layer of soil, of a complete different shade, more of a grey color. This is because this soil formed from a different ‘parent’ material, a dense glacial till, which then was overlaid with volcanic ash and loess (wind-blown sediment).  A cross section of this soil is pictured below.

 Image

A zoomed in map of the location of the soil, highlighted in green, West of the Cascades:

Image

This type of soil has many human uses to include agriculture, crops such as berries, flowers, leafy greens. Livestock and stables, baseball fields, wetlands and wildlife habitats. Overall I was pretty excited about this type of soil in the area where I live, its cool to know it comes from volcanos and glaciers!

-Amanda

Citation: 

Cerise, L. (2013, February 27). The soil that makes Washington oh so Evergreen. WSU Master Gardener Program of Thurston County. https://wsumgtc.wordpress.com/2013/02/21/evergreen-soil-makes-the-evergreen-state/

The soil that makes Washington oh so Evergreen. WSU Master Gardener Program of Thurston County. (2013, February 27). https://wsumgtc.wordpress.com/2013/02/21/evergreen-soil-makes-the-evergreen-state/

Angel Delgado-leach posted Nov 4, 2024 1:09 PM
Good Morning everyone,


I&#x27;m Angel, and I&#x27;m excited to be back in school after a year and a half away due to some mental health challenges. This is actually my second time taking this course; last term, my hectic work schedule made it difficult to keep up, and I felt too embarrassed to reach out to the professor to ask for the ability to turn in late work.


A bit about me: I’m from Spanaway, Washington, a small town about 45 minutes from Seattle. Right now, I&#x27;m stationed in Italy with NATO, serving in the Air Force for over 17 years as an E-7. I’m majoring in Environmental Science, focusing on Fish and Wildlife Management, and I&#x27;m in my senior year with 99 out of 120 credits completed. I hope to finish my degree early next year.
I’m married to Amber, and we have an 8-year-old son named Raiden. In my free time, I love playing video games and traveling. I decided to take this course to ease back into academics, and completing my degree is really important to me, especially as a role model for my son. He’s seen how much I value education, and I want him to pursue his goals as well. One of my proudest moments was completing my degree through the CCAF, and I’m looking forward to continuing that journey.


For my post, instead of discussing the soils of Washington like last time, I’ll focus on the soils of Sicily, where I’m currently living. The soil types vary depending on the region of the island:

Sicily

- Zone E: Soils of the Apennines of Calabria and Sicily on igneous and metamorphic rocks
- Zone F: Soils of Mount Etna
- Zone G: Soils of the hills of Calabria and Sicily on Tertiary calcareous rocks and sediments, including alluvial and coastal plains
- Zone H: Soils of the hills and mountains on limestone and igneous rocks of Sicily
- Zone I: Soils of the hills of Calabria and Sicily on Tertiary clayey flysch, limestone, sandstone, gypsum, and coastal plains
- Zone L: Soils of the alluvial and coastal plains of central and southern Italy (where I’m stationed) 

Sicily is home to a diverse range of soil types. In the northern region, the soil primarily consists of calcareous rocks and sediments, blended with silt and sand. The western, southwestern, and central areas feature sandy loam, calcareous clay, sandstone, and limestone. In northeast Sicily, the volcanic soil from Mount Etna includes pumice, basalt, pebbles, and black ash. The southeastern part of the island also has volcanic soil, along with loam, limestone, and igneous rocks. 
Looking forward to learning with you all.

- Angel


References

Fantappiè, M. (2014, September). Soil regions of Sicily, according to the “soil map of Italy”... | download scientific diagram. https://www.researchgate.net/figure/Soil-regions-of-Sicily-according-to-the-Soil-map-of-Italy-Costantini-et-al-2013a_fig11_265731824

Class,

 

PART 1

 

My name is Brad Little and I am currently located in Guam, where I am stationed with the Air Force. I am originally from North Carolina and, if all goes well, will be returning there after this assignment. As I mentioned I am in the Air Force and have been for the past 17 years. On returning to North Carolina I aspire to work in range management with the US Forestry Service. Prior to living in Guam I lived in Alaska for 4 years. Between the two locations I have become an avid fisherman and enjoy being outdoors in general. I stay fairly busy with my job but I can usually be found on the water anytime I get the chance. I am married and have a 5-year-old female German Shepherd named Rudy. She has been an excellent, and very resilient, companion for my wife and I as we hop around the world from assignment to assignment.

 

Like most, I am taking this course as a requirement for my program. I have been taking classes through AMU for several years now so nothing to surprising jumped out at me on the syllabus but considering the question I feel like I better go back and look again! Circling back to my degree plan, I am working towards a bachelors in environmental science with a focus on fish and wildlife management. I am not the strongest in chemistry so I have a dash of anxiety reading the course description but I am glad to be here and look forward to working with you all.

 

PART 2

 

North Carolina’s official soil series is Cecil. A photo of the soil profile and state distribution can be seen in the embedded images below. The Cecil Series has a wide variety of land uses. According to the North Carolina State soil booklet, the Cecil Series is excellent for agricultural crops. Furthermore, approximately 80-90% of the Piedmont was cleared of trees by the late 1800’s for agriculture making way for major crops like wheat, soybean, corn and tobacco but also serves as a good soil for loblolly pine tree plantations (Soil Science Society of America, p. 2). As far as natural forest, hard mast producing trees like oak and hickory thrive in this soil series. My research thus far shows that this soil profile seems to be most suitable for a variety of trees, agricultural crops, and is even a soil well suited for development of urban environments.

 

 Soil ProfileDispersion Map

 

Brad

Brodie Henson posted Nov 4, 2024 7:51 AM
Hello everyone,

PART 1:

My name is Brodie Henson and I am 27 years old. I am currently living in South Carolina but am originally from Illinois. I am currently serving in the active-duty air force at Joint Base Charleston, South Carolina. I am a security forces member or military police as other branches call it. After completing my degree program in environmental science, I hope to get out of the military and become a game warden. In my spare time I like to do outdoor activities as well as play video games. My family includes my wife and my two cats. I am taking this course to fulfill my credit requirements in my degree plan. I look forward to reviewing CO-4 explain the impacts of human activities on soil.

 

PART 2:

The state soil for South Carolina is called Lynchburg soil. This soil is created due to coastal flooding conditions in poorly drained areas causing marine sediment to deposit into the earth. This is typically a marine deposit sediment. This is a very rich soil that can be used for several purposes including farming and crop cultivating. Woodlands are also very predominant in this soil profile. These soils are important for their respective ecosystems, acting as a filter to delicate marine ecosystems as well as delicate wetland ecosystems. This soil is generally moist and easy to dig into making it good for building housing on as well. However, flooding is a major issue when it comes to this soil due to it becoming easily over saturated. This occurs especially during major tropical storms or other heavy rainmaking systems. Below are some pictures of the soil geography:

Soil Profile:

Soil Profile

Soil Range:

Soil Range

I hope everyone has a great week and I look forward to working with everyone throughout the course!

-Brodie

Lets get dirty! Craig Nalley
CRAIG NALLEY posted Nov 4, 2024 5:59 PM
Class and Professor,

I am very excited to be starting this class. Mainly because I have little to no understanding of soil sciences and really did not know how large the field was until reading the introductory resources and videos. My name is Craig Nalley and just hit my one year anniversary of retirement from the US Army. I was a Senior Armor Sergeant at the time of my retirement and served as a First Sergeant for my last real assignment in the Army. I am taking this class from Idaho and am currently working at Lowe&#x27;s to keep the human interaction alive. I am at 102 credits towards my degree, which is Environmental Sciences with a Fish and Wildlife Management focus. I have completed all the concentration requirements and am looking forward to graduation as early as May 2025. I would like to use this degree to better understand the Idaho wildlife conservation plans and population of animals in the state and how they are suffering or thriving. My brother in law is an avid hunter and said a few years ago there was a nasty winter that killed a lot of the deer population. I would really like to understand why, and how we are attempting to bring back that population. I am married with 2 kids a 16, and 13 year old. The one just recently got his drivers license and a job so we rarely see him anymore. Now on to what we all came here for SOIL!

The state soil of Idaho is the Threebear soil and surprisingly to me it is only found in the northwestern part of the state. It does make sense that this would be the state soil due to the vast amounts of forest and logging that is in the Northern part of the state. Logging and timber business is actually worth 1.5 billion dollars to the state and Threebear is great soil to grow trees.




Threebear soil profile consists of a yellowish brown silt loam as the top layer, the subsoil upper layer is light yellowish to brown silt or loam, the subsoil middle is very pale brown silt loam and the subsoil lower is a firm brittle fragipan of yellowish brown silt loam. The other use for the threebear soil is wildlife habitat it helps with things like the filtration of water of the wildlife and grows a multitude of things for the wildlife to eat. Below is a monolith found on the NRSC website giving us a fantastic view of all the layers of the Threebear soil. I am looking forward to learning with all of you over the next eight weeks!

Craig

In Maine, the state official soil is Chesuncook soil. The Chesuncook Series was established in 1992 in Franklin and Somerset Counties in the western part of Maine. The soil consists of deep well drained soil on upland till plains, hills and mountainside slopes. They are found mostly on forested landscapes with slopes ranging from 3% to 45%. This soil is good for farmlands, hay fields and timber production because of its well drained capabilities.

W1, George Steward: Texas Soil - Houston Black
George Steward posted Nov 6, 2024 6:29 AM•466 WordsLast edited: Thursday, November 7, 2024 6:39 AM EST
Hey everyone! My name is George, and I’m excited to dive into soil science with all of you this semester. I’ll be joining you from the heart of Texas, in the vibrant city of Austin. When I’m not out enjoying the rolling hills and unique ecosystems of the Texas Hill Country, I spend my days engaged in environmental work, focusing on soil and tree health. My current projects include assessing trees including heritage live oaks for development and, more recently, exploring septic system preservation techniques to seal a seepage issue and protect our yard and precious groundwater resources. You could say I’ve got my hands deep in the dirt, figuratively and literally.

I’m pursuing a degree in Environmental Science, and this course perfectly fits my interests. Soil is at the center of everything—ecosystem health, water quality, and even how we interact with our environment as humans. I’m hoping to gain a deeper understanding of soil processes and management to better serve my local community and, hopefully, expand my work to influence larger regenerative projects. Outside of work and studies, I love spending time outdoors with my family and exploring regenerative living techniques that mimic nature&#x27;s processes.

One thing that stood out to me after reading the syllabus is the focus on soil classifications and their practical implications. I’m particularly interested in soil-water interactions and how soil health impacts water infiltration and storage, especially in our water-scarce region of Texas. We live in the karst region known as the Edwards Plateau with a heavy deposit of limestone. The is a unique environment because of this which is very different from the official Texas soil type.

PART 2: Texas Official State Soil: Houston Black Clay

Texas is a massive state, and our soils are as diverse as our landscapes. Our official soil is the Houston Black series, a Vertisol that covers more than 1.5 million acres across the state, primarily in the Blackland Prairie region. Houston Black Clay is characterized by its rich, dark color and high shrink-swell capacity, meaning it expands when wet and contracts when dry. This unique property can be both a blessing and a curse, contributing to fertile farmland while also causing foundation issues for buildings and roads.

The major human uses of Houston Black Clay include agriculture, where it supports crops like cotton, corn, and sorghum. However, managing this soil requires careful attention to its drainage and erosion potential. Urban development in Texas has faced challenges due to this soil&#x27;s movement, leading to innovative engineering solutions. Here’s a tip for those interested: never underestimate the power of soil structure!

I’ve included images of the soil profile and a distribution map to bring this discussion to life.

Looking forward to hearing everyone’s perspectives and learning with you this semester!

Grace Johnston posted Nov 6, 2024 8:47 PM
PART 1: 

Good evening Class!

My name is Grace and I’m from Minnesota, I’ve been in the National Guard here for a little over 10 years now. I currently work full time for the Guard both in the Joint Operations Center and helping out in a supply room, but I hope to be working for the DNR or something similar once I graduate with my degree. Hopefully even before, if I find an opening! I have a dog named Raja who I rescued when she was 9, at this point she’s just an old lady who wants to sleep all the time. I’m pretty big into the outdoors so during my spare time I often go hiking, rollerblading, or camping when I have the time. However even when I’m busy, just taking time to sit outside on the deck helps center me. I’m taking this course due to my major being Environmental Science with a concentration in Fish and Wildlife management.

PART 2:

With Minnesota being my home state, I’m representing their state soil which is Lester. Despite being voted as the state soil in 1985, it wasn’t approved by the governor of Minnesota until 2012, a whole 27 years later! This soil was formed from woody vegetation being removed for agricultural reasons. It holds an average annual temperature of 49 degrees. With this soil being found in 17 counties in the south-central part of the state, the area it’s in still covers over 600,000 acres. With the main use being in agricultural farms, this soil is incredibly helpful to Minnesota!

W1: Maryland Soil
Contains unread posts
Keily Leiva-Diaz posted Nov 8, 2024 5:45 PM
Hello everyone, my name is Keily and i&#x27;m from Maryland where I live with my husband and two kids. I&#x27;m currently working in payment processing for a credit union but i&#x27;d love to work in a national park one day as a park ranger. However, recently i&#x27;ve been seeing a lot of things regarding wildlife biology that I was interested in but i&#x27;m not sure yet. I&#x27;m working towards an environmental science degree and when i&#x27;m not doing school work, I&#x27;m practicing crochet or embroidery for fun.

In Maryland, the official soil is called sassafras. It is one of the oldest soil profiles in the United States dating back to 1901. You can see by the soil profile below that there are different textures that make it up. What&#x27;s great about sassafras soil is that it can be used for anything so its dominance, especially in the Maryland area, makes for high results and good product. &quot;Sassafras soil is divided by the Chesapeake Bay into the eastern and western shores, comprising a total of nearly 5,000 square miles, or about one-half of the area of Maryland.&quot; (Cowherd)

sassafras soil profile

This soil is able to grow diverse vegetation because it is moderately permiable, warms up quickly in the spring season and is one of the most productive soils used for farming and forestry. Maryland is not the only state this is found in, it is also in Pennsylvania, Delaware, Virginia and Washington DC with one of its main uses being construction and recreational development.

soil distribution for sassafras in maryland

-Keily

Sources:

Cowherd, W. (n.d.). Sassafras. Sassafras Maryland State Soil. https://www.soils4teachers.org/files/s4t/k12outreach/md-state-soil-booklet.pdf 

Sassafras -- Maryland State Soil - USDA. (n.d.). https://www.nrcs.usda.gov/sites/default/files/2022-10/Sassafras%20Fact%20Sheet.pdf

Part 1 

Hello, Class! 

Hope everyone is doing well and looking forward to the next 8 weeks of Soil Science. My name is Kyle West, I am enlisted in the U.S. Air Force, and I am currently stationed at Kirtland AFB, New Mexico which is where I will be residing through the duration of this course.  

My AFSC is Security Forces, assigned to the 377th Security Forces Group, and I currently perform duties as the Resource Advisor for my unit. When I have free time, I enjoy spending time with my two daughters, playing video games, going on adventurous hikes, and finding opportunities to practice my photography skills.  

This course is just another steppingstone towards the completion of my bachelor&#x27;s degree in environmental science with a concentration in Fish &amp; Wildlife Management. With the completion of this degree, I hope to pursue a career as a Wildlife Officer for the State of Ohio once I retire from the Air Force (6 years from now). 

As I reviewed the syllabus, one course objective stood out to me the most... 

CO-4 Explain impacts on soils from human activities including pollution, erosion, salinity, and other emerging issues. 

Throughout previous courses, I have always been intrigued to learn the level of impact we as humans have on the environment around us. Looking forward to learning how our soil is affected. 

 

Part 2 

For Part 2, I will discuss the official soil series for Ohio since that is where I am from.  



Figure 01. A soil pit in Miamian soil.

The Ohio State soil is the Miamian soil series which is deep and moderately well-drained soil. Deposited materials from continental glaciers during the last ice age were a contributing factor in the development of this soil series. 



Figure 02. Location of the Miamian soil in Ohio and parts of Indiana and Illinois. 



Figure 03. Physiographic Regions of Ohio.

Miamian soil is prevalent in the Central Lowland area, the Till Plain. The Eastern Cornbelt Plains is the broad Ecoregion, but the more specific Ecoregion where Miamian soil thrives is called the Loamy High Lime Till Plains. The soils within this range were formulated mostly on low gradient glacial till plains from parent materials of high lime glacial till. These soils are perfectly suited for large scal agriculture of corn and soybean production.  



Figure 04. Ecoregions of Ohio.

This concludes my week 1 discussion. I hope you enjoyed the topics that I shared this week. Feel free to further this discussion with any additional questions or information. I wish everyone the best of luck throughout this course!

Respectfully, 

Kyle


References

Slater, B., &amp; Wilson, T. (n.d.). Miamian - Ohio State Soil. https://www.soils4teachers.org/files/s4t/k12outreach/oh-state-soil-booklet.pdf

Puerto Rico’s Official Soil Series: Humatas
The Humatas soil series is Puerto Rico’s designated official soil, characterized by its deep, well-drained, reddish-yellow, clay-rich structure primarily found in tropical and subtropical regions. This soil is formed from volcanic rock weathering and is common in the island’s mountainous, humid uplands (USDA, n.d.).



Humatas Soil Profile
Humatas soils are part of the Ultisol soil order, known for intense weathering and leaching in warm, humid climates. The soil profile typically includes an organic-rich top layer over a dense, clay-rich subsurface horizon, often described as kaolinitic due to the clay minerals present (Soil Survey Staff, 2023). This profile includes:

O Horizon: A thin organic layer, supporting vegetation and nutrient cycling.
A Horizon: A mineral-rich surface layer, darkened by organic matter.
Bt Horizon: A clay-rich, leached subsurface horizon with a reddish hue due to iron oxide accumulation.
Distribution of Humatas Soils in Puerto Rico
Humatas soils cover roughly 10% of Puerto Rico’s land area, mainly in central mountainous regions and along the northern slopes where rainfall is significant. This distribution provides natural support for Puerto Rico’s coffee plantations, tropical fruits, and forestry (USDA NRCS, n.d.). The map below highlights the areas where Humatas soils are predominantly found:



Major Human Uses of Humatas Soils
Agriculture: Humatas soils are valuable for agricultural use, especially for crops that thrive in humid, well-drained soils like coffee (Coffea arabica), bananas (Musa spp.), and citrus fruits. The clay-rich subsoil helps retain moisture, reducing irrigation needs, which is beneficial in Puerto Rico’s seasonal climate (Bridges, 2021).

Forestry and Erosion Control: Due to their natural drainage and structure, Humatas soils support tropical hardwood forests and erosion control initiatives, particularly on steep slopes. Reforestation and controlled grazing practices are often implemented to minimize soil erosion and degradation (Herrera, 2019).

Environmental Significance: Beyond agriculture, Humatas soils play a crucial role in hydrology, filtering rainwater and replenishing aquifers. This supports both regional biodiversity and water availability for Puerto Rican communities (Soil Survey Staff, 2023).

Paraphrase: 

To me, the Humatas soil series in Puerto Rico stands out as a vital part of the island’s ecosystem and economy. As Puerto Rico’s official soil, Humatas has unique qualities that support both its natural environment and its agricultural systems. This deep, reddish-yellow soil is rich in clay, which helps retain water which is a key feature in the island’s tropical and so often humid climate. Formed from the weathering of volcanic rock, Humatas soils are common in Puerto Rico’s mountainous uplands, where they benefit from high rainfall. Puerto Rico is an interesting landscape in my opinion due to the shift from a beach to a somewhat mountain like landscape.

Humatas soils are classified as Ultisols, which means they undergo intense weathering and leaching in the tropical climate. In structure, the soil has a thin organic layer on top, followed by a mineral-rich layer, and a dense, clay-heavy subsoil. This profile is packed with iron oxides, giving its reddish tint, and with kaolinitic clay minerals that retain essential nutrients and moisture for the plant growth.

This soil series spans about 10% of Puerto Rico’s landscape, predominantly in the central mountains and northern slopes. Because of its moisture-retaining properties and natural drainage, Humatas soil is ideal for coffee cultivation (lots of coffee beans are grown on island), tropical fruits, and even forestry. Its distribution is also strategic for the island’s water supply; as rainwater filters through the soil, it helps replenish aquifers and support regional biodiversity.

Humatas soils are also environmentally significant beyond farming. Their natural drainage supports tropical hardwood forests and erosion control on steep terrains. With the right forestry practices, such as reforestation and regulated grazing, these soils help prevent erosion and land degradation—key for sustaining Puerto Rico’s landscapes and rural communities.

In sum, Humatas soils are a cornerstone of Puerto Rico’s agricultural production, hydrology, and conservation efforts, showcasing how a single soil series can support both the people and ecosystems of an entire region.

References
Bridges, E. M. (2021). World Soils and their Regional Distribution. Cambridge University Press.
Herrera, J. (2019). Erosion control and land management practices in Puerto Rico. Journal of Soil and Water Conservation, 74(3), 58-66.

Soil Survey Staff. (2023). Humatas Series. Official Soil Series Descriptions. Retrieved from https://www.nrcs.usda.gov

PART 2:

The San Joaquin soil, officially designated as California’s state soil in 1997, spans over 500,000 acres in the Central Valley, particularly near the valley&#x27;s southern end. Known for its unique profile, San Joaquin has a surface of brow loam, with deeper layers of brown clay and an indurated durian that limits water movement. This soil type is significant for supporting California’s agricultural industry, as it is used for cultivating crops like wheat, rice, almonds, and grapes as well as for pasture and urban development. The soil’s composition and structure make it well suited for the region&#x27;s needs, though the hardpan layer can present challenges by restricting root growth and water infiltration. San Joaquin soil is also recognized globally, with a profile displayed in the Netherlands World Soil Museum, reflecting its importance in both agricultural and environmental contexts.

               In 1997 California official designated the state soil to be San Joaquin soil. It is found mostly in the central valley from the east side of San Joaquin and lower Sacramento counties. This also happens to be a large agricultural area of California supporting crops of Almonds, pistachios, grapes, figs, oranges and almonds. Even with its ability to support such a wide range of crops, San Joaquin soil has its limitations. If we were to follow a droplet of water down through the soil horizons we would find higher levels of clay the deeper we went. This increasing concentration of clay inhibits root growth and water absorption. This clay density and lack of water movement can limit what crops can grow and is not good for irrigated crops.

Good evening Professor and Classmates,

Part 1

My name is Robyn. I grew up in Indiana, but I now reside in the Dallas Fort Worth area. I recently separated from the Air Force in March. I am still not acclimated to the hot summers that Texas has to offer but I do enjoy the winter months. I started this degree plan while I was in the military with the goal of becoming a weather forecaster. Since getting out and becoming a mom, my priorities have changed. However, I still have a goal of finishing my degree. I am taking this course as it is one of the last two classes that I need for my concentration. A course objective that stood out to me is identifying and describing key physical properties of soil. Before taking this course, I did not really think about the physical properties of soil. I am interested to learn more about soil texture, structure, porosity, and water-holding capacity.



Part 2

I live east of the Dallas Fort Worth area where the Blackland Prairies reside. The soil where I live is black or dark-gray alkaline clay. The soil in this region was formed from clayey sediments or from chalk limestone deposits. This soil will shrink as it dries and swell as it is hydrated. The soil is poorly drained as the soil ranges from shallow to very deep. Blackland Prairie soil is known to be hard to manage because of being tough and sticky. If there is high water capacity coupled with the clay, then it creates low oxygen conditions in the soil. This results in low rainfall infiltration as well as poor water moving throughout the soil. This can make farming difficult due to seed bedding in the springtime. Cracks in the soil can improve soil-water intake, however, problems can occur such as zones of weakness that allow the same crack to form at the same place during dry conditions on untilled soil. Tilled soil allows for cracking pattern to be disrupted along with disease problems to decline during the next crop rotation.





A map of texas with different colored statesDescription automatically generated

 

Thank you for taking time to read my discussion post. I look forward to replies.

Robyn

General Soil Map of Texas. (2024). USDA. https://maps.lib.utexas.edu/maps/texas/texas-general_soil_map-2008.pdf

Part 2. 

Idaho&#x27;s official soil is the Threebear series. The soil series was established in 2003 making it one of the newer soils recognized in Idaho. The Threebear consists of mountain slopes, benches, hills, and plateaus. General elevation range is 1600 to 12000 feet and is found in over 53,000 acres in the state.  The soil is made up of volcanic layer ranging from to 14 to 23 inches ash which make it susceptible to erosion.  This land is used primarily for timber production and grazing livestock. Timber market alone is over $1.5 billion industry. 

The surface layer is ashy over loamy, mixed, frigid Alfic Udivitrands. Shown below.

Meet Your Instructor
Contains unread posts
Sylvia Nemmers posted Oct 17, 2024 3:07 PM
Hello everyone!

 

One of my earliest memories was receiving a beautiful pink tea set on my 7th birthday. I immediately took it into the bathroom; where the cream pitcher became a beaker, the cups became flasks, and the sugar bowl and spoon transformed into a mortar and pestle for grinding things to a powder. The teapot held things such as vinegar and baking soda to become a spouting volcano.   From these humble beginnings my career in science was launched.

 

 As a result of more formal training, I hold a BS and MS in Biochemistry from Kansas State University, and a PhD in Environmental Science - Soil Chemistry - from New Mexico State University. I have worked as a high school math/science teacher, as well as home schooling my children.  Since 2005 I have taught thousands of undergraduate and graduate students at the university level.

 

During my doctoral research, I investigated the mobility of arsenic through the soil when land applied with municipal wastewater effluent. While this may sound a bit obscure; its relevance stems from the fact that in 2006 the US EPA implemented a lower maximum contaminant level for arsenic, causing many communities to use processes such as reverse osmosis to remove arsenic from their drinking water. The problem then arises; how to dispose of the arsenic that has been removed from the drinking water? My research found that land applying the arsenic with the rest of the municipal wastewater may not be the best way to dispose of it.

 

I have lived in seven states and six countries  throughout my life, allowing me to look at the topic of environmental science from a more global perspective.  I have three wonderful children, who at some point in their lives attended school online. This allows me to see both sides of the online learning process.

 

I love to teach and enjoy helping people who have always said of themselves, &quot;I am not a science person&quot; to discover that indeed they can be a &quot;science person.&quot;   I myself avoided taking foreign language all through high school, and the first three years of my BS, because I thought I was &quot;not a language person.&quot; As it has turned out, I am fluent in Arabic and can muddle my way through in Greek. The lesson here is that we need not label ourselves or others, limiting what can be done. Rather we must envision what can be done, and put our best effort to that goal.

Dr. Nemmers

Part 2

Colorado&#x27;s state soil

Seitz

Seitz soils are primarily located in southwestern Colorado, and found at higher, cooler elevations. In 1972 the soils were first established in the area near Rio Grande County, Colorado. The soil gained it&#x27;s name from the stream which was close to where the soil scientists first described the soil. 

The soil is found on hills, high valleys, ridges, and mountain slopes. Due to the mountainous locations of the soil, the primary uses are grazing cattle, forestry, and outdoor recreation activities, such as hiking and mountain biking. The largest uses of the soil are timber production, live stock, home to wildlife animals including grouse, mule deer, elk, and black bear. Cleared forest can be used for pasture land. 

The plant community consists of an Engelmann spruce, subalpine fir or Rocky Mountain Douglas fir canopy with a sparse understory of grasses, forbs, and shrubs. The landscape relief and the forest vegetation supported by the Seitz soils combine to form panoramic views and ambience that attract many visitors to Colorado. 

There is about 350,000 acres of Seitz soils in Colorado. (Symbols)

The Seitz soils are very deep, well drained soils developed in colluvium and slope alluvium. To put it simply the mountainous rock that gets weathered away begins to creep and fall downslope due to the gravity and water. Once the materials reach a stable position in the landscape they accumulate and over time even further weather occurs ultimately creating the Seitz soils. Typically found on slopes ranging from 2-65 percent incline. 

The topsoil has a texture of clay loam to sand loam, usually with a lot of gravel or rock fragments. Dark Grey to black with moisture.  Seitz soil typically has about 5 soil horizons.

   Soil profile mapmap 2

References 

Box. (n.d.). https://nrcs.app.box.com/s/b7j0swuayb9ifqckz1020tjzpt9tusti/file/1000288963185   

Seitz, C. S. S., Soil Science Society of America, Photo Soil Monolith: Chip Clark/Smithsonian Institution, NRCS, &amp; Miller, B. (n.d.). Seitz Soil. Colorado State Soil. https://www.soils4teachers.org/files/s4t/k12outreach/co-state-soil-booklet.pdf

State Soils - Colorado. (n.d.). https://forces.si.edu/soils/interactive/statesoils/html/State-Soils/Default.aspx?selection=Colorado&amp;tab=where

Symbols, S., USA. (2017b, December 2). Seitz Soil. State Symbols USA. https://statesymbolsusa.org/symbol-official-        item/colorado/state-soil/seitz-soil

copywriting guidance....
Learning New Skills
If you have a Chat GPT thread that you use and want it to provide you with better responses in certain areas, you can give it additional prompts like the “copywrite” prompt below. I first asked ChatGPT what made a great copywriter, then I told it to create me a prompt using that information. Additionally, I recommend providing ChatGPT with a writing style guide in order to avoid the overly formal or robotic tone often associated with AI.
—
Human Sounding Writing Style Guide

Adopt a more human-like writing style and avoid an overly formal or robotic tone. Follow these guidelines to achieve a natural, engaging voice:
Conversational Tone: Write as if having a conversation with a friend. Use a friendly, approachable tone that is relaxed yet respectful. Incorporate contractions (e.g., don&#x27;t, can&#x27;t, it&#x27;s) to sound more natural.
Sentence Variety: Mix short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm. This helps keep the reader engaged and makes the content dynamic.
Personal Touch: Use personal pronouns (I, you, we) to foster a sense of connection. Include anecdotes or relatable examples to illustrate points, making the content more engaging.
Simplicity and Clarity: Use clear, straightforward language. Avoid jargon or overly complex words unless necessary. Break down complex ideas into simpler, more digestible parts.
Empathy and Understanding: Show empathy and acknowledge the reader&#x27;s feelings or concerns. Use supportive phrases like &quot;I understand how you feel&quot; or &quot;That makes sense.&quot;
Humor and Wit: Incorporate light humor or wit where appropriate to make the content enjoyable. Use humor sparingly and ensure it fits the context.
Active Voice: Use active voice to create direct, dynamic sentences. For example, say &quot;I recommend trying this approach&quot; instead of &quot;This approach is recommended.&quot;
Pacing and Flow: Maintain a smooth flow by connecting sentences and paragraphs logically. Use transitional phrases to guide the reader.
Natural Phrasing: Avoid overly formal or technical phrasing. Use language that feels natural and spontaneous.
Smart Brevity: Focus on delivering concise, clear, and impactful messages.
Feedback and Adaptation: Pay attention to feedback and adjust the style based on what resonates most with the reader. Continuously strive to improve the naturalness of the writing.

–
Style Guide for Beautiful Writing

Hello ChatGPT! I want you to enhance your writing skills based on the following lesson, which emphasizes the importance of sentence variety to create engaging and dynamic content. Please use this as a guide when creating written content for me.

Lesson on effective writing:

This sentence has five words. Here are five more words. Five-word sentences are fine. But several together become monotonous. Listen to what is happening. The writing is getting boring. The sound of it drones. It&#x27;s like a stuck record. The ear demands some variety.

Now listen. I vary the sentence length, and I create music. Music. The writing sings. It has a pleasant rhythm, a lilt, a harmony. I use short sentences. And I use sentences of medium length. And sometimes when I am certain the reader is rested, I will engage him with a sentence of considerable length, a sentence that burns with energy and builds with all the impetus of a crescendo, the roll of the drums, the crash of the cymbals-sounds that say listen to this, it is important.

So write with a combination of short, medium, and long sentences. Create a sound that pleases the reader&#x27;s ear. Don&#x27;t just write words. Write music.

Guidelines:

Vary Sentence Length: Use a mix of short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm in your writing. This variety helps to maintain the reader&#x27;s interest and makes the content more engaging.

Engage the Reader: Use longer, more complex sentences sparingly to emphasize important points and to create a sense of build-up and climax in your writing.

Create Musicality: Aim to create a flow in your writing that feels like music. Think about the rhythm and harmony of your sentences, ensuring they are pleasing to read.

Avoid Monotony: Steer clear of using too many short sentences in a row, as this can become monotonous. Balance is key to maintaining the reader&#x27;s attention.

Emphasize Key Points: Use varied sentence structures to highlight important information and to keep the reader engaged throughout the text.

By following these guidelines, you will be able to create written content that is dynamic, engaging, and enjoyable for readers. Let&#x27;s write content that not only conveys information but also delights the reader&#x27;s senses.

–
Personalized Voice and Writing Style Guide
Hello ChatGPT! I&#x27;d like you to help me create a voice and style guide that aligns with my unique communication preferences. To do this effectively, please ask me a series of questions to better understand how I like to communicate. 
–
Copywriter Skill Prompt
Hello ChatGPT! I&#x27;d like to equip you with a valuable skill – that of a proficient copywriter – so that you can assist me in generating high-quality content for various topics in our dedicated thread. This isn&#x27;t something you need to adopt right now, but rather a set of instructions for when I do need you to craft content. You will continue your role as ChatGPT, pulling from all the information we&#x27;ve discussed so far and supplementing it with additional research as necessary.

What Makes a Great Copywriter:

A great copywriter possesses the ability to create engaging, informative, and persuasive content that resonates with the target audience. They understand the importance of clarity, brevity, and connecting with readers on an emotional level. As a copywriter, your aim will be to deliver content that informs, educates, or motivates, depending on the context and purpose.

How We&#x27;ll Collaborate:

When I provide you with a topic (e.g., &quot;I want you to write content on workplace diversity&quot;), your role will shift to that of a copywriter.
You&#x27;ll consider the information shared in our conversation, utilizing the knowledge you&#x27;ve gained as ChatGPT.
If needed, you&#x27;ll also supplement your understanding with information from reliable sources available to you.
You will then ask me relevant questions to ensure you can craft the best article.

Example Questions for Crafting Great Content:

What is the medium for this content (e.g., blog, professional article, etc.)?
What is the primary purpose of this content (e.g., inform, educate, motivate)?
Who is the target audience for this content?
What specific information should be included?
Are there any key points or data that need to be highlighted?
How do you want the readers to feel after reading this content?
Are there any specific keywords or phrases to be included?
Do you have any preferences for the tone (e.g., professional, conversational)?
Are there any guidelines or formatting requirements?

Your Role as a Copywriter:

I&#x27;ll provide you with the topic and answer your questions.
You will use your ChatGPT expertise and additional research to craft the article.
After writing, you will provide a brief breakdown of each section&#x27;s purpose, then let me know where you feel we can make improvements to the content (if relevant). 

Feel free to fully embrace the role of the copywriter, focusing on asking questions that ensure the content&#x27;s quality and relevance. Once I provide the necessary information, you can take charge and create the article. If you believe more information is needed, feel free to ask for clarification or additional details. Your assistance in creating compelling content will be invaluable, and your versatile skills will greatly enhance our collaboration!
–

Henson Week 2 Mesopotamia

Brodie Henson posted Nov 11, 2024 10:26 AM
Crabben, J. van der. (2024, November 5). Agriculture in the Fertile Crescent &amp; Mesopotamia. World History Encyclopedia. https://www.worldhistory.org/article/9/agriculture-in-the-fertile-crescent--mesopotamia/

Map

Map 

Hello everyone,

For this week&#x27;s discussion I have decided to research how the people of ancient Mesopotamia interacted with the soil present in their environment. The major use of the soil in this location was farming. This soil was named by the Mesopotamians as alluvial soil. This name was created to sperate the different soils in the region. Alluvial soil was named because of the soil&#x27;s location which was in the flood plain of Euphrates and Tigris Rivers. The soil&#x27;s location led to the soil being full of moisture and nutrients, which made it phenomenal for farming. The best soil in the area was located in between the Tigris and Euphrates Rivers. You can see the soil range in the map above. This soil was labeled as a loam-like structure/texture, and the topsoil was the part with the most nutrients. The culture of the Mesopotamians was greatly affected from the soil in their area. Their culture greatly revolved around farming and the harvesting of livestock. With a rich soil, all of these were possible. The alluvial soil type also allowed, for the first-time, year-round farming which led to fully stablished settlements. Nomads no longer had to travel from area to area in order to grow crops. The main crops that were grown wheat, barely, and oats. In the long term, this soil allowed for mass farming of crops, which allowed the Mesopotamian empire to rise into a large-scale empire. This soil also allowed a farming population to support a non-farming population for the first time in recorded history. This soil can be labeled as a massive steppingstone for soil usage when it comes to agriculture and allowed the Mesopotamian culture to grow rapidly. 

-Brodie

References:

Crabben, J. van der. (2024, November 5). Agriculture in the Fertile Crescent &amp; Mesopotamia. World History Encyclopedia. https://www.worldhistory.org/article/9/agriculture-in-the-fertile-crescent--mesopotamia/

Yucatán Peninsula (southeast México), York
Lindsey Taylor York posted Nov 9, 2024 9:48 AM
Citation:
Estrada, F., et al. (2009). Maya soil classification in southeastern Mexico: A participatory approach. PMCID: PMC2831876. https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC2831876/

Map:



Yucatán Peninsula (southeast México)



LP = Leptosol, CM = Cambisol, LV = Luvisol, AR = Arenosol, GL = Gleysol, ST = Stagnosol, VR = Vertisol, NT = Nitisol and SC = Solonchack

Answers:

How they used the soils: Maya farmers classified soils for agricultural purposes, using physical properties like rock types, color, and depth to assess land suitability for crops. This classification helped them make informed farming decisions.

How they named them: Soils were named based on visual and physical characteristics, such as stone content, color, and water behavior. Local names like Ch&#x27;och&#x27;ol and Tzek&#x27;el reflected these attributes.

Importance of soil to their culture: Soil classification was integral to Maya farming, reflecting their deep understanding of the land’s role in sustaining their communities. Proper soil management supported crop selection, land use, and environmental sustainability.

These naming conventions and classification practices tie into our course material on soil taxonomy, ethnoecology, and the importance of localized soil knowledge in land management.

The Maya people have developed a soil classification system based on practical farming needs. They identify soils using characteristics like stone content, color, and depth, assigning names that reflect these properties. This system allows farmers to assess land for cultivation and ensures sustainable farming practices, which is deeply tied to Maya culture and their environmental knowledge. Their system aligns with certain scientific classifications but includes unique traits like rock formations, which are critical for agricultural success in the region.

Referenced:

Estrada, F., et al. (2009). Maya soil classification in southeastern Mexico: A participatory approach. PMCID: PMC2831876. https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC2831876/

Aymara, Ray
Contains unread posts
RACHAEL CARAWAY posted Nov 11, 2024 2:43 AM
Postigo, J. C., &amp; Laura Valdez, S. (2022). The sociocultural construction of soil among communities of the Bolivian Altiplano: Potential for supporting transitions to Sustainability. Mountain Research and Development, 42(4). https://doi.org/10.1659/mrd.2022.00015

Click on the rectangle

Geography to Aymara

The Aymara are the indigenous people of the Altiplano region of South American and have been herders and agriculturalist since before the Incan Empire. Concentrated mostly in the central Andes in Peru and Bolivia, the Aymara have survived the harsh climates of the soil poor and windy plateaus for hundreds of years. Their main crops consist mostly of potatoes, corn, wheat and beans. Traditional farming techniques combined with learned skills have allowed the Aymara to adapt and remain in balance with the natural world for centuries.

As global soil quality continues to deplete, and climate change worsens researchers are looking towards more sustainable farming practices. Ethnopedologists are eager to understand the traditional and learned approach the Aymara have with sustainable farming.

The Aymara have long known the importance and value of quality soil. They have a unique way of categorizing their territories to optimize sustainable farming as well as community and land management. The Aymara use descriptions and meanings to identify different zone characteristics that make it “homogenous” and identifiable. Each Homogenous zone depiction may include identifiable terms referring to topography, climate and ownership but all zones will include details of the soil composition. It will refer to the color (mineral and chemical composition), texture (sand, silt or clay), structure (granular, prismic, platy), taste (salinity) and smell. This system allows the Aymara to spatially organize their territories as well as allowing the community to change its expectations of the territory as the environment changes. Researchers are hoping to use these sorts of indigenous and modern sustainable farming techniques to coproduce hybrid categories (Indigenous-scientific) of soil knowledge.

 

 

 

Sources:

Britannica, T. Editors of Encyclopaedia (2024, May 28). Aymara. Encyclopedia Britannica. https://www.britannica.com/topic/Aymara

Ta‘ū, American Samoa, Week 2 Steele
Contains unread posts
Zane Steele posted Nov 11, 2024 4:17 PMLast edited: Monday, November 11, 2024 7:33 PM EST
Greetings Class,

The indigenous location I choose was a small island on the Samoa archipelago. Ta‘ū is the easternmost, youngest, and largest island of the Samoa archipelago. The entirety of the archipelago is created by volcanic activity. The island is extremely wet, and heavily vegetated.

The research paper I choose was titled &quot; Distribution of Soil Nutrients and Ancient Agriculture on Young Volcanic Soils of Ta‘ū, American Samoa” this group of scientists were interested in the relationship between soil characteristics and indigenous agricultural practices and noticed there was little data in the area. The data collected was expected to provide insights into indigenous agricultural practices. The group analyzed patterns in soil chemistry and vegetation within the Manu’a islands of American Samoa. Soils were sampled along transects that crossed through precontact settlement zones in the upland of Fiti‘uta on Ta‘ū island, a location characterized by young (&lt;100 ky) volcanic substrates and very high (&gt;3800 mm y−1) annual rainfall. (Autufuga)

Soils were processed to determine different soil fertility properties that have been proposed as predictors of intensive rainfed tuber production in Hawaii, and other locations. Surveys of plants were conducted to assess pervious patterns of land use.  The soils presented with moderate values of fertility, measured by pH, base saturation, exchangeable calcium, and total and exchangeable phosphorus, despite the high rainfall. (Autufuga) The limited prior data and the newly collected data suggest that agroforestry and arboriculture were key components of past agricultural practices.

Soils on Ta‘ū consist of several soil series formed from volcanic basalt and volcanic ash deposits. Multiple soil samples were collected and found that the samples were high in organic matter, with carbon contents ranging from 12.8 to 23.4, and very strongly acidic to neutral with pH values ranging from 4.62 to 6.

 The soil is a huge factor for land use and human use among societies past and present. The data collected and compared to pervious studies conducted on islands such as Hawaii finds that the soil and the climate of Ta’u would have been an arboricultural cropping system also known as alley cropping. Like how you see fruit trees cropped. The knowledge can know inform contemporary practices to improve the allocation of soils and agriculture to provide a sustainable cropping system for the environment.

Autufuga, D., Quintus, S., Yoo, K., Day, S., Huebert, J., Deenik, J., &amp; Noa, K. L. (2023). Distribution of Soil Nutrients and Ancient Agriculture on Young Volcanic Soils of Ta‘ū, American Samoa. Soil Systems, 7(2), 52. https://doi.org/10.3390/soilsystems7020052

View Image - Figure 1. Ta‘ū Island, indicating the location of the project study area.

Ta‘ū Island, indicating the location of the project study area.

Zane

---

**Title: Nisqually People’s Soil Classification and Environmental Management**

**Citation:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [Link to the document]

**Map Link:**
[View the Nisqually River Delta Map](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/Nisqually/)

**Summary:**
The Nisqually people have a long-standing and intimate relationship with the Nisqually River Delta, where they have engaged in sustainable practices that prioritize ecological balance. The Nisqually soil classification system has been influenced by their traditional environmental stewardship and their understanding of the land’s role in sustaining their community. Their soil classification is closely tied to the health of estuarine ecosystems, as they have traditionally used the land for fishing, hunting, and limited cultivation, always mindful of maintaining harmony with the environment.

1. **How They Used the Soils:** The Nisqually people used the rich, alluvial soils of the river delta for growing traditional plants and supporting wildlife habitats essential for fishing and gathering. The river and its sediments played a crucial role in the area’s ecology, and the soil’s fertility supported plant species that provided medicine and food. These practices minimized disruption to the landscape, emphasizing balance and sustainability.

2. **How They Named Soils:** The soil naming by the Nisqually is not extensively documented, but their knowledge incorporated observable attributes, such as color, texture, and how soils interacted with water. They used terms reflective of the soil’s role in their cultural and subsistence practices, much like other indigenous groups who classify soils based on their practical applications in agriculture, hunting, and gathering.

3. **The Importance of Soil to Their Culture:** Soil was a cornerstone of the Nisqually culture. It supported estuarine habitats that were crucial for salmon populations, a cultural and economic staple. The Nisqually people&#x27;s practices, including controlled burns and careful management of plant species, demonstrated a holistic approach to land stewardship. This connection to the soil is mirrored in their cultural narratives and spiritual beliefs, emphasizing their responsibility to protect the land for future generations.

By examining the Nisqually’s relationship with their environment, we see echoes of modern conservation principles. The Nisqually approach aligns with our course discussions on ecosystem management, demonstrating a holistic strategy where the health of the soil, water, and biodiversity are all interconnected. The restoration of estuarine habitats at the Nisqually NWR today reflects this enduring legacy, aiming to return the delta to a more natural state that supports fish and wildlife.

**References:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*.

---

Let me know if you need assistance with specific images or maps!

**ASSISTANT**:
Indigenous communities, such as the Nisqually people, have developed intricate soil classification systems that are deeply intertwined with their cultural practices and environmental stewardship. While specific scholarly articles detailing the Nisqually&#x27;s soil classification are limited, we can draw insights from broader studies on indigenous soil knowledge and apply them to the Nisqually context.

**Indigenous Soil Classification Systems:**

Indigenous communities often classify soils based on observable characteristics such as color, texture, moisture, and vegetation. These classifications are deeply intertwined with cultural practices, agricultural methods, and ecological understanding. For instance, a study on indigenous soil classifications highlights that local knowledge encompasses soil properties, processes, classifications, management, and soil-plant relationships. citeturn0search1

**Application to the Nisqually People:**

The Nisqually people, known as the &quot;people of the river, people of the grass,&quot; have traditionally inhabited the Nisqually River Delta. Their intimate relationship with the land suggests a nuanced understanding of soil types, particularly in relation to the estuarine ecosystems they managed. While specific terms used by the Nisqually for soil types are not extensively documented, we can infer that their classifications were likely based on factors such as:

- **Proximity to Water:** Soils near the river and delta would have been recognized for their fertility and suitability for certain plant species.

- **Vegetation Indicators:** The presence of specific plants would signal particular soil conditions, guiding agricultural and gathering practices.

- **Soil Texture and Color:** These attributes would inform decisions on planting, building, and other cultural activities.

**Cultural Significance:**

For the Nisqually, soil was more than a physical medium; it was integral to their way of life. The fertile soils of the delta supported plant species used for food, medicine, and materials. The health of these soils was directly linked to the abundance of salmon and other wildlife, central to Nisqually culture and sustenance.

**Conclusion:**

While direct documentation of the Nisqually&#x27;s soil classification system is scarce, understanding their deep connection to the land provides valuable insights. Their practices reflect a sophisticated ecological knowledge, emphasizing sustainability and harmony with nature. This perspective aligns with broader indigenous approaches to soil and land management, offering lessons for contemporary environmental stewardship.

- Nisqually River Council. (n.d.). Land Acknowledgment. citeturn0search4

Here&#x27;s a more engaging, story-like version with a touch of wit:

**Title: Digging into the Nisqually Way: How Soil Made (and Saved) a People**

**Citation:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [Link to the document]

**Map Link:**
[View the Nisqually River Delta Map](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/Nisqually/)

**Summary:**
Imagine being part of a community that calls the river its lifeblood, where the earth isn’t just dirt but a living, breathing part of the family. That’s the story of the Nisqually people, “people of the river, people of the grass,” who have thrived for generations in the rich landscape of the Nisqually River Delta. Their relationship with the soil was more than just a practical one—it was spiritual, essential, and pretty much the glue that held everything together.

1. **How They Used the Soils:** The Nisqually people weren’t just planting crops willy-nilly. They knew their stuff. The lush, alluvial soils of the river delta—think of the type of soil that would make any farmer&#x27;s heart race—were their bread and butter. These fertile lands weren’t just for feeding families but also for supporting habitats teeming with life, from salmon swimming through the delta waters to the towering plants they used for medicine and food. The soil was their pharmacy, their supermarket, and their spiritual sanctuary all rolled into one.

2. **How They Named the Soils:** Now, while we don’t have a cheat sheet of Nisqually soil names, we do know that indigenous cultures had some seriously clever naming conventions. The Nisqually would have likely classified soil based on the stuff that mattered most—color, texture, and how it felt underfoot after a good rain. Soil wasn’t some boring, static thing to them. It had character. The land spoke, and the Nisqually listened, naming soils with descriptors that helped guide planting, harvesting, and even building.

3. **Cultural Importance:** Soil wasn’t just dirt to the Nisqually; it was life. It cradled the roots of plants they relied on and played a starring role in their fish-filled waterways. Without healthy soil, there’d be no salmon runs, and without salmon, the heart of Nisqually culture would miss a beat. They practiced controlled burns and other smart land management techniques to keep everything in balance—long before &quot;regenerative agriculture&quot; became a buzzword. Their stewardship was less about extracting resources and more about a beautiful give-and-take with Mother Earth, a relationship that modern conservation efforts are still trying to emulate.

Their philosophy? Respect the soil, and it’ll take care of you. Ignore it, and, well, good luck with that. Today, the restoration work at the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge aims to honor these age-old practices, bringing back the wetlands and rich estuarine soils that sustain both people and wildlife.

The Nisqually people didn’t have advanced scientific instruments, but they had wisdom that made them some of the original soil scientists, working with nature’s blueprint instead of against it. Their way of understanding soil has a lot to teach us about working with the land rather than fighting it, making it a pretty solid example of ecological smarts that we’d do well to remember.

**References:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [Link to the document]

**ASSISTANT**:
**Title: The Nisqually People and Their Soil Wisdom: An Analysis**

**Citation:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [Link to the document]

**Map Link:**
[View the Nisqually River Delta Map](https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/)

---

For this week&#x27;s discussion, I’ve chosen to explore the soil knowledge of the Nisqually people, who have a deep-rooted connection to the land and water systems of the Nisqually River Delta in Washington State. This connection to the soil is not only culturally significant but aligns beautifully with the soil physical properties we’re studying this week.

### Physical Properties of Nisqually Soils

The Nisqually River Delta is characterized by rich, alluvial soils, formed through the accumulation of river sediments over time. According to the *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan* by the U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service (2005), these soils have a high organic matter content, making them extremely fertile and well-suited for supporting diverse ecosystems. The soils are predominantly silty loams and clays, with excellent water-holding capacity and a structure that promotes healthy root growth for native vegetation.

The physical properties of the Nisqually soils, such as texture, porosity, and structure, were essential for the Nisqually people’s agricultural and environmental management practices. The texture of these alluvial soils, for example, consists of a balance of sand, silt, and clay, which influences water infiltration rates and soil aeration—two key factors for plant health and crop yields (Soil Health Nexus, n.d.). The soil’s high organic matter also enhanced its structure, creating aggregates that reduced erosion and promoted nutrient retention, crucial for both plant and aquatic life.

### Soil Usage and Cultural Practices

The Nisqually people used their understanding of these soil properties to optimize land use. They practiced seasonal burning and careful land management to maintain soil fertility and prevent erosion. By understanding the porosity and drainage of different soil types, they were able to grow important plant species while also preserving critical habitats for salmon and other wildlife (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2005). This harmonious relationship with the land highlights how the Nisqually people&#x27;s practices were sustainable and ecologically beneficial, long before modern soil science concepts became widely known.

### Paraphrasing Course Content

This week&#x27;s content emphasized the importance of soil texture, structure, and organic matter in influencing overall soil health (Soils4Teachers, n.d.). The Nisqually people’s practices echo these principles, as they managed soil in ways that preserved its physical properties. For example, by using controlled burns, they maintained the organic matter and prevented compaction, which would otherwise reduce soil porosity and restrict plant root growth. Their practices align with modern conservation efforts that emphasize soil structure and organic content for sustainable land use.

**Conclusion**

In summary, the Nisqually people’s knowledge of soil physical properties, such as texture, porosity, and organic content, was advanced and ecologically sound. Their traditional methods are a testament to the importance of understanding soil properties to sustain both human and environmental health. These insights continue to inform conservation and restoration projects today, demonstrating the lasting impact of indigenous knowledge on modern soil science.

**References:**

Soil Health Nexus. (n.d.). Soil physical properties. Retrieved from [https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/](https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/)

Soils4Teachers. (n.d.). Physical properties of soil. Retrieved from [https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties](https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties)

U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually)

**Title: Indigenous Soil Knowledge of the Nisqually People: A Scholarly Perspective**

**Citation:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually)

**Map Link:**
[View the Nisqually River Delta Map](https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/)

---

**Introduction**

The Nisqually people, indigenous to the Pacific Northwest, have long demonstrated a profound understanding of soil properties within the Nisqually River Delta. Their traditional ecological knowledge encompasses detailed observations of soil texture, structure, and fertility, which have been integral to their agricultural and environmental practices. This discussion explores the physical properties of soils as understood by the Nisqually people and examines how their indigenous knowledge aligns with contemporary soil science.

**Physical Properties of Nisqually Soils**

The Nisqually River Delta is characterized by alluvial soils rich in organic matter, primarily composed of silty loams and clays. These soils exhibit high fertility and excellent water-holding capacity, supporting diverse plant species and robust ecosystems (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2005). The texture and structure of these soils facilitate healthy root development and efficient nutrient cycling, essential for both native vegetation and agricultural crops.

**Indigenous Soil Classification and Management**

While specific scholarly articles detailing the Nisqually&#x27;s soil classification are limited, indigenous communities often classify soils based on observable characteristics such as color, texture, moisture, and vegetation. These classifications are deeply intertwined with cultural practices, agricultural methods, and ecological understanding. For instance, a study on indigenous soil classifications highlights that local knowledge encompasses soil properties, processes, classifications, management, and soil-plant relationships. citeturn0search3

The Nisqually people&#x27;s soil management practices included controlled burns to maintain soil fertility and prevent erosion. By understanding the porosity and drainage of different soil types, they were able to grow important plant species while also preserving critical habitats for salmon and other wildlife (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2005). This harmonious relationship with the land highlights how the Nisqually people&#x27;s practices were sustainable and ecologically beneficial, long before modern soil science concepts became widely known.

**Parallels with Contemporary Soil Science**

Modern soil science emphasizes the importance of physical properties such as texture, structure, and organic matter content in determining soil health and fertility. The Nisqually people&#x27;s traditional practices reflect an intrinsic understanding of these principles. Their methods of soil classification and management align with contemporary concepts of sustainable land use and conservation, underscoring the value of integrating indigenous knowledge into modern environmental stewardship.

**Conclusion**

The Nisqually people&#x27;s deep connection to their land and sophisticated understanding of soil properties have sustained their communities for generations. Their traditional ecological knowledge offers valuable insights into sustainable soil management practices that resonate with current scientific approaches. Recognizing and respecting this indigenous knowledge is crucial for developing holistic and effective environmental conservation strategies.

**References**

- Soil Health Nexus. (n.d.). Soil physical properties. Retrieved from [https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/](https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/)

- Soils4Teachers. (n.d.). Physical properties of soil. Retrieved from [https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties](https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties)

**ASSISTANT**:
Here&#x27;s the revised discussion post that meets the assignment criteria with clear connections to the course material:

---

**Title: The Nisqually People’s Soil Management Practices**

**Citation:**
U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually)

**Map Link:**
[View the Nisqually River Delta Map](https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/)

---

**Summary of the Source**

The Nisqually people, an indigenous community native to the Pacific Northwest, have maintained a deep connection to the land and soil of the Nisqually River Delta. Their traditional ecological practices reflect an intrinsic understanding of soil physical properties, including texture, structure, and organic matter content, which are essential to both agriculture and ecosystem conservation.

### **How They Used the Soils**
The Nisqually’s relationship with the soil is evident in their sustainable practices. Controlled burns were a fundamental method they used to enhance soil fertility, promote nutrient cycling, and prevent soil compaction, a concept that aligns with our current understanding of soil aggregate stability (Soil Health Nexus, n.d.). These burns helped maintain soil structure, enabling better water infiltration and reducing erosion, especially in a landscape prone to flooding. Additionally, they managed riparian areas to ensure soil health along the delta, which also supported salmon populations—an essential food and cultural resource.

### **How They Named the Soils**
While direct records of Nisqually soil names are scarce, indigenous soil classification typically relies on observable characteristics. The Nisqually people likely used practical descriptors for soils, such as terms reflecting fertility, moisture retention, or ease of cultivation. This mirrors other indigenous cultures worldwide, who name soils based on physical traits, including texture (sandy, clayey, loamy) and organic content. The importance of naming and understanding soil characteristics was crucial for farming decisions and maintaining ecological balance.

### **The Importance of Soil to Their Culture**
Soil health was central to the Nisqually people’s cultural and environmental practices. Fertile soils supported crops and native plants vital for sustenance, medicine, and spiritual ceremonies. Moreover, the soil played a key role in the preservation of salmon habitats. Healthy riparian soil prevented erosion and ensured clean, cool water flow—a critical factor for salmon spawning. This cultural and ecological significance exemplifies how soil was far more than a resource; it was a lifeline that sustained the community’s well-being and traditions (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2005).

### **Connection to Course Material**
Our course this week emphasizes physical properties of soil, such as texture, structure, and water-holding capacity, which are key to understanding soil health and functionality. The Nisqually people’s practices align well with these concepts. For instance, controlled burns improve soil structure by preventing compaction and promoting pore space, which aids in water infiltration—a topic discussed in our materials (Soil Health Nexus, n.d.). Their management of organic matter mirrors our course’s focus on the role of organic content in enhancing soil fertility and supporting microbial life, illustrating a holistic understanding of soil health long before modern soil science (Soils4Teachers, n.d.).

**References:**
- Soil Health Nexus. (n.d.). Soil physical properties. Retrieved from [https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/](https://soilhealthnexus.org/resources/soil-properties/soil-physical-properties/)
- Soils4Teachers. (n.d.). Physical properties of soil. Retrieved from [https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties](https://www.soils4teachers.org/physical-properties)
- U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2005). *Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from [https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually)
