Saline Wetlands in Western Australia-Craig
Contains unread posts
CRAIG NALLEY posted Nov 19, 2024 5:19 PMLast edited: Tuesday, November 19, 2024 5:24 PM EST
How important are plants interactions with the soil below them to infiltration, percolation, and saturation? A new study from the Western Australia University and the Beijing Forestry University has uncovered a relationship between these two in the Western Australian Saline wetlands that prove it is a critical component of not only the plant and soil but the restoration of a failing wetland. The model shows us that plants that have denser root systems closer to the surface allow water to move more freely into the soil underneath it (Lumeng, 2024). It helps with infiltration of the water through the system by creating macropores giving the water an easier route to the subsurface. Once it has infiltrated it allows for easier percolation into the depths of the system. This is critical to the wetlands and helps them enhance their soil by forming a more cohesive environment in which the wetlands are able to thrive. It is stated in the article that the vegetation-soil-hydrology of the area, if not in harmony, could cause a collapse of the system (Lumeng, 2024). 



What we see in this graph is a representation of the macropores and the relation of the water infilitration with those macropres present. The one on the left is the volume of infiltration, and the one on the right shows the amount of macropores observed using CT Scans and underground radars. This is quite fascinating to see the results of this study and shows the direct relation ship that soil and water have with each other. 

Craig

Plant Roots Key to Water Movement and Wetland Restoration. Phys.org. (2024, November 12). https://phys.org/

In Italy farmers are seeing some of the toughest conditions that they have faced in their lifetimes. In southern Italy they are facing the exact opposite conditions of extreme drought that has left the crops brittle and drying and the area of the country has devastated water reserves by trying to keep up with the amount of water needed to sustain the crops but also to sustain life in the area. The north had an extremely wet spring that delayed the sowing and now with the fall becoming even more wet the soil is slippery and not in an ideal condition to be harvested. These issues can lead to crops not being as nutrient dense and in turn harm the species that are relying on these nutrients in their every day lives.

W3, Steward: Balancing Amazonian Soil Water Dynamics
George Steward posted Nov 21, 2024 7:17 AM•392 Words
Soil Water Dynamics in the Amazon: A Critical Balance

President Joe Biden’s historic visit to the Amazon rainforest shed light on urgent environmental issues, including the profound impacts of conventional agriculture on soil water dynamics. As forests are cleared for cattle ranching and large-scale crops, the region&#x27;s natural water cycle is disrupted, creating long-term challenges for the soil and ecosystem.

Trees in the Amazon play a crucial role in maintaining soil moisture. Through transpiration, they release water into the atmosphere, driving rainfall patterns that sustain the forest and agriculture alike. Deforestation removes this vital function, exposing the soil to erosion, nutrient loss, and reduced water retention. The soil’s capillary fringe—the zone where water is drawn upward—is also compromised, making it harder for plants to access water even during wet seasons (Fiola et al., 2020).

amazon

This cycle worsens drought conditions, as Amanda Kerrick highlighted in her post, with less moisture in the atmosphere leading to reduced rainfall. Degraded soil struggles to support crops, driving further land clearing and perpetuating the problem (World Resources Institute, 2017). President Biden’s visit emphasized the global importance of addressing these interconnected issues and promoting sustainable practices.

One promising solution is agroforestry, where trees are integrated with crops and livestock to restore soil health. By mimicking natural ecosystems, agroforestry improves water retention, reduces erosion, and rebuilds the capillary fringe, offering hope for a more sustainable future (World Wildlife Fund, 2024). Stories like that of Dirce Soares, a Brazilian farmer championing agroforestry, highlight how integrating trees into farmland not only restores soil health but also offers economic and ecological resilience (World Resources Institute, 2024).

Protecting the Amazon is not just about saving trees and preserving this unique, diverse ecosystem—it’s about safeguarding the delicate balance of soil, water, and life that sustains the planet.

References
Fiola, J. C., Rabenhorst, M. C., &amp; DeBusk, W. F. (2020). Solving the mysteries of water and air underground. Soil Science Society of America Journal. Retrieved from https://www.soils.org/news/science-news/solving-mysteries-water-and-air-underground/

World Resources Institute. (2017). 3 surprising ways water depends on healthy forests. Retrieved from https://www.wri.org/insights/3-surprising-ways-water-depends-healthy-forests

World Resources Institute. (2024). Dirce Soares: How agroforestry helps restore Brazil&#x27;s Atlantic Forest. Retrieved from https://www.wri.org/faces-restoration/soares-agroforestry-brazil

World Wildlife Fund. (2024). Agroforestry solutions for sustainable agriculture. Retrieved from https://www.worldwildlife.org

Post Staff. (2024, November 17). President Biden becomes first sitting president to venture into Amazon rainforest: &#x27;Fight for humanity.&#x27; New York Post. Retrieved from https://nypost.com/2024/11/17/us-news/president-biden-becomes-first-sitting-president-to-venture-into-amazon-rainforest-fight-for-humanity/?utm_source=chatgpt.com

Minnesota Drought, All It Took Was 3 Months - Grace
Contains unread posts
Grace Johnston posted Nov 20, 2024 6:35 PM
Good afternoon, Class!

I decided to write about the drought in Minnesota for this week&#x27;s discussion. I have both a news article, along with information I got from our DNR site. The reason I wanted to pick this news article is because just 3 months ago the entire state had a 0% drought due to heavy and continuous rain this spring. This was the first time in three years that the entire state was drought free. Of course, this caused its own problems with flooding rivers and lakes, but after a too warm winter with little snow fall the state had been in a drought. The flooding had caused problems with farmers starting their harvest on time, while the drought that&#x27;s hitting the state now, is causing an early harvest for farms in the southwest. According to the USDA Crop Progress report for Minnesota (2024) &quot;Topsoil moisture supplies were 26% very short, 42% short, and 32% adequate. Subsoil moisture supplies were 17% very short, 34% short, 48% adequate, and 1% surplus.&quot; Farmers in Minnesota are hoping for a heavy snowfall this year to both help with the drought we are currently in, along with helping the alfalfa fields that currently have sand veins running through them. The plus side to the drought that we have had this fall was that farmers saved money on drying out their crop after harvest. 

-Grace

W3: European Peatlands &amp; Degradation
Contains unread posts
Keily Leiva-Diaz posted Nov 19, 2024 11:11 AM
Urgent action needed to reverse soil degradation in Europe

Hello everyone,

Today I wanted to talk about a report that came out of Europe a few weeks ago. The 2024 State of Soils in Europe report is able to distinguish the state of soil degradation not only across the EU but in places including the Western Balkans, Turkey and Ukraine. (Jones, 2024) Based on the results obtained from this years report, an urgent need for change was issued. 

Farming areas are experiencing human made pollutions but the peatlands are also experiencing degradation. I didn&#x27;t know this at first but a peatland is a wetland where the soil is made up of plant material. This peat soil can be thicker in other areas but the plant material never fully decomposes so it&#x27;s in a constant state of decay. Any harm to these areas can cause biodiversity loss because they store greenhouse gasses and prevent them from being out in the atmosphere. These areas are being drained using draining ditches for their peat as well as forestry and farming which has increased their risk of climate change. Below I&#x27;ve added an image of the peatlands soil profile where they are measuring the depth of the organic horizon to identify how much carbon is being stored in the soil. (Jones, 2024) An initiative to rewet these lands has been suggested but it&#x27;s been assessed that a large percentage is irreparable.

peatland profile

Europe has faced soil pollution for many years with tillage erosion being the biggest cause of degradation for crop lands as well. Unfortunately, they&#x27;ve estimated that, due to a recent increase in water erosion, up to 50% of EU soils could be affected by 2050. All of this along with a history of improper farming and overgrazing makes soil more susceptible to deterioration. This is why the newest strategy they&#x27;ve begun to implement is so important to the future of european agriculture and overall soil health. 

-Keily

Source: 

Arias-Navarro, C., Baritz, R. and Jones, A. editor(s), 2024. The state of soils in Europe.

Kyle West - Soil Moisture Conditions Expected to Worsen Across Texas
Contains unread posts
Kyle West posted Nov 19, 2024 11:45 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 20, 2024 12:41 AM EST
Hello Class!

For this week&#x27;s discussion, I have chosen a news article from the Texas A&amp;M Agrilife&#x27;s Digital Magazine and Newsroom posted on 1 October 2024 which discusses the soil moisture conditions expecting to worsen across the state of Texas. Feel free to access the link below for further details.

https://agrilifetoday.tamu.edu/2024/10/01/soil-moisture-conditions-expected-to-worsen-across-state/

Summary:

The recent rains in Texas briefly benefited agriculture across the state, aiding crop planting and growth. However, soil moisture levels have since declined due to insufficient rainfall and higher-than-normal temperatures. Experts warn that the return of the La Niña weather pattern, with drier and warmer conditions, could lead to poor conditions for growth across cool-season crops like wheat and impact planting conditions for the spring of 2025. Drier soils are now a concern across much of the state.

Drought conditions in Texas have significantly impacted corn and sorghum production. Harvests began unusually early, signaling poor conditions with 75% of corn harvested by September 8 which was the earliest in 40 years. Sorghum yields averaged 53 bushels per acre, coming in below the 10-year average, while corn yields were 121 bushels, slightly below last year’s average. Lower-than-normal harvest percentages and yields reflect the challenging growing conditions statewide.



Figure 1. Drought Conditions Across a Corn Field

Drought conditions persist across Texas, with dry soils and high temperatures causing stress amongst crops. Some areas received scattered rain, benefiting crops like cabbage and fall vegetables, but many regions remain too dry for planting. West Texas saw mixed soil conditions, while the rain in the Lower Rio Grande Valley improved pastures and aided in planting, though irrigation water shortages may reduce vegetable acreage. Despite brief cooling periods and isolated rainfall, dryland cotton and other crops continue to face severe challenges across Texas.



Figure 2. Irrigation Water Valve

Texas agricultural conditions vary widely by region. Scattered rain improved pastures and crops in some areas, while others faced persistent drought, dry soils, and pest infestations. Cotton, corn, and peanut harvests progressed, though yields were mixed. Wheat planting continued but struggled in dry conditions. Livestock generally fared well, but supplemental feeding increased in drier regions. Rangeland and forage quality varied, with armyworms and grasshoppers posing challenges. Some regions anticipate lower acreage due to water shortages.

Course Content Connection:

This news article is relative to this week&#x27;s course objective #2 of defining the physical and biological properties of soil while we Identify and describe key physical properties of soil, such as texture, structure, porosity, and water-holding capacity. Due to the drought conditions across Texas, the soil&#x27;s water-holding capacity is affected as it&#x27;s dependence for crop growth is compromised. 

I hope you enjoyed the information I shared this week. Looking forward to any questions or insight to further the discussion. Thank you!

Respectfully,

Kyle

Reference

Schattenberg, P. (2024, October 10). Soil moisture conditions expected to worsen across state. AgriLife Today. https://agrilifetoday.tamu.edu/2024/10/01/soil-moisture-conditions-expected-to-worsen-across-state/

Virginia&#x27;s Expanding Drought and Its Impact on Soil Moisture
Contains unread posts
Lindsey Taylor York posted Nov 20, 2024 3:47 PM
Good afternoon class,

Virginia&#x27;s Expanding Drought and Its Impact on Soil Moisture

In recent weeks, Virginia has faced severe drought conditions, leading to a drought watch advisory for 38 counties, including Augusta and Rockingham. This has been triggered by below-average rainfall and intensified by high temperatures. The Virginia Department of Environmental Quality (DEQ) and the Drought Monitoring Task Force have urged residents and local governments to implement water conservation measures. The lack of precipitation has caused significant reductions in soil moisture, which is vital for agricultural productivity, groundwater recharge, and ecosystem stability (Virginia Department of Environmental Quality, 2024).

Agriculture has been notably affected, with crops struggling due to cracked, dry soils. This situation mirrors broader impacts seen across the Southeast, where reduced soil moisture has led to challenges such as poor forage for livestock, delayed crop planting, and increased wildfire risks. The persistent dry weather also threatens long-term soil fertility by hindering organic matter decomposition and nutrient cycling (Drought.gov, 2024).

Efforts to combat these issues include encouraging community-wide water-use reductions and drought planning to mitigate worsening conditions. However, with no significant rain forecasted in the short term, concerns remain about prolonged agricultural and ecological stress.

Citations:

Virginia Department of Environmental Quality. (2024, November 8). Drought watch advisories issued for 38 counties due to lack of rain. Augusta Free Press. Retrieved from https://www.augustafreepress.com
Drought.gov. (2024, November 1). Drought impacts to agriculture and soil moisture. Retrieved from https://drought.gov

W3: Devastating floods in Spain
Contains unread posts
McKenzie Baker posted Nov 19, 2024 2:16 PM
Hello Class,

For this week&#x27;s discussion, I chose to highlight the devastating floods currently affecting southern and eastern Spain. The floods were caused by a high-altitude, low-pressure system (DANA) that trapped warm air masses over the Mediterranean, resulting in torrential rainfall. Over 200 fatalities have been reported, thousands of people have been displaced, and entire villages have been destroyed after receiving a year&#x27;s worth of rainfall in just eight hours. The overflow of rivers like Magro, Turia, and Poyo severely impacted areas such as the Albufera National Park and nearby urban regions. Satellite data, including Sentinel-1 and Sentinel-2 imagery, revealed that 15,633 hectares of land and approximately 190,000 people were affected. Additionally high resolution imagery identified 4,100 hectares of damaged land and 3,906 buildings, with significant disruption to railways and roads. This crisis underscores the importance of soil science in disaster response. Data from the SMOS satellite, which uses microwave radiometers, showed that saturated soil conditions before and during the floods amplified the damage. Excessive soil moisture reduced the land’s ability to absorb further rainfall, leading to severe waterlogging and increased surface runoff. This event highlights the need to prioritize soil health and re-evaluate land use practices to mitigate the impact of future disasters. Understanding and monitoring soil moisture levels not only aids in emergency response but also supports long term strategies for disaster mitigation and climate resilience. 



(Devastating Floods in Spain Witnessed by Satellites, n.d.)

(Devastating Floods in Spain Witnessed by Satellites, n.d.)

Reference:

Devastating floods in Spain witnessed by satellites. (n.d.). https://www.esa.int/Applications/Observing_the_Earth/Devastating_floods_in_Spain_witnessed_by_satellites

Soil moisture concerns in Illinois
Contains unread posts
RACHAEL CARAWAY posted Nov 18, 2024 5:06 PM
Illinois Farmers Welcome Much-Needed Rain Amid Soil Moisture Concerns

The recent rainfall in Illinois has been a mixed blessing for the region, with significant impacts on both soil moisture and flooding. Farmers, such as Steve Turner in Cass and Morgan counties, welcomed the rains as a much-needed relief to replenish subsoil (B horizon) moisture, which had been severely depleted during the summer drought. This rain was especially crucial for crops like winter wheat, which had begun to dry out before the storm system moved through. The rainfall’s infiltration into the soil was effective, with little runoff, allowing water to percolate down and recharge the soil profile. However, despite these benefits, Turner expressed concerns that the region is still in a moisture deficit, as the rain was not enough to fully saturate the deeper layers of the soil.

In contrast, certain areas along the Mississippi and Meramec rivers faced flash flooding due to the intensity of the rainfall. This rapid water influx can overwhelm the soil&#x27;s infiltration capacity, leading to increased pooling, runoff and erosion, particularly in fields lacking sufficient vegetative cover. Such fast-moving storms have become more common, posing a challenge to farmers who must manage the risk of soil degradation and the potential loss of nutrients.

Illinois&#x27; state soil, Dummer soil (silty, clay loam), is known for its high cation exchange capacity (CEC), which helps it retain essential nutrients for plant growth. However, even soils with high CEC can struggle to handle extreme rainfall if the soil is already compacted or dry, limiting its ability to percolate water effectively. This phenomenon is a growing concern for farmers who are seeing more erratic rainfall patterns, which disrupt the natural processes of moisture absorption and nutrient cycling.

Figure1. Water storage in soil



waterstoragesoil.png (900×300)

Overall, while the recent rains have helped to mitigate some of the drought impacts and replenish soil moisture, they have also underscored the challenges of managing soil health in the face of shifting climate patterns, particularly regarding infiltration, saturation, and nutrient retention. The delicate balance between maintaining soil moisture for crop growth and avoiding the detrimental effects of flooding will continue to be a critical issue for Illinois farmers.

Sources:

Callman, R. (n.d.). Soils-Illinois. Natural Resources Conservation Service. https://www.nrcs.usda.gov/conservation-basics/conservation-by-state/illinois/soils-illinois#regions

Winkelpleck, S. (2024, November 8). Illinois farmers welcome much-needed rain amid soil moisture concerns. Successful Farming. https://www.agriculture.com/illinois-farmers-welcome-much-needed-rain-amid-soil-moisture-concerns-8742487

North Carolina aftermath of Helene
Contains unread posts
reimond romero posted Nov 20, 2024 9:23 PM
Hey Class,

While storms strike with a swift devastating blow, the aftermath can be felt for weeks and months following their landfall. News stories quickly pick up the next hot topic but currently there are people in this country who are living in extreme conditions following this disaster. Thankful stories like this one coming from The Guardian are shedding some light for us. Some may be under the notion that once the heavy rainfall and flooding has left an area then there is little to worry about. As we have learned our soil has varying levels of porosity. This allows the ground to soak up toxic wastes from landfills, destroyed homes, and vehicles that have been swept away. 

Farms who would have otherwise been unaffected are still impacted due to septic water leeching into the groundwater and shutting down crops production (The Guardian, 2024). The ground can only absorb so much moisture at a time. This causes standing water to also be an issue. Pools of this stagnant water breed insects with diseases and allows bacteria to be rampant. Wildlife and livestock who drink this infected water can now enter the human consumption chain, further spreading new bacteria. Some things that can be done to combat these conditions are a swift cleanup, focused resources to those impacted, and a constant follow-up to measure soil moisture as well as its toxicity levels. 

https://i.guim.co.uk/img/media/0c09f52619ed73665a086594aee68454effd3eed/0_117_4032_2688/master/4032.jpg?width=620&amp;dpr=1&amp;s=none&amp;crop=none Photo 

References 

The Guardian. (2024, October). North Carolina farms face depleted, toxic soil after historic Helene flooding. The Guardian .

Robyn Welch W3: Western North Dakota&#x27;s Drought Conditions
Contains unread posts
Robyn Welch posted Nov 20, 2024 5:11 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 20, 2024 5:12 PM EST
Good evening Professor and Classmates,

https://www.jamestownsun.com/business/planting-cover-crops-still-beneficial-in-fall-even-under-dry-conditions

I found this article regarding North Dakota’s current drought conditions (lack of soil moisture) which are affecting this fall season cover crops. I found this article interesting as I was stationed in Minot, ND and was familiar with seeing cover crops while stationed there. Not only did I experience the drought seasons but also got to record rainfall/snowfall amounts on base while being a weather forecaster there.

In western North Dakota, farmers will use cover crops such as cereal rye or triticale during the fall. However, due to the dry season many of the plants have not yet emerged. A majority of western North Dakota have seen less than one-half inch of rain for more than 30 days (“Planting Cover Crops”). The lack of rainfall causes a huge threat for the cover crops and winter cereals to thrive due to the struggle of moisture intake and germination. If there is not enough moisture in the soil this fall, then the seeds will stay dormant until the springtime when enough moisture and warmth are accessible to the seeds. But some cover crops need to emerge or else the dormancy will not be broken in time with moisture imbibition which will cause the seed to die in the ground during the winter months. There needs to be enough soil moisture to promote seed sprouting but also a cap to the amount of soil moisture to stop the further growth of the seed. Farmers are recommended to plant in soil temperatures when the ground has cooled to 35 degrees Fahrenheit but before the ground freezes solid or covered in snow (“Planting Cover Crops”). The temperature of the soil along with the amount of moisture in the soil are important factors for successful germination. If cover crops do not emerge in the fall, then in early spring farmers can hope to see visible growth.

 

Thank you for taking time to read my discussion post. I look forward to replies.

Robyn

References

Planting Cover Crops Still Beneficial in Fall, Even Under Dry Conditions. (2024, November). The Jamestown Sun. https://www.jamestownsun.com/business/planting-cover-crops-still-beneficial-in-fall-even-under-dry-conditions

La Niña... Idaho Moisture… Campbell W3
Contains unread posts
Scott Campbell posted Nov 21, 2024 7:31 AM
Hi everyone, 

This week I found an article published on November 20th, regarding Idaho&#x27;s moisture expectations with it being a La Niña winter. Idaho experienced a year of limited rain fall and hot temperatures in 2023/2024 (Oct. to Sept.). Since 1896, water levels have been measured and in 2023, Idaho was 33rd driest. It also fell 4th for the highest air temperatures.  The lack of water and hot temperatures had a large impact on farmers and their crops. The La Nina could bring the precipitation that is greatly needed. A La Nina phenomenon is marked by cooling of the ocean surface, which brings colder temperatures and greater rainfall. National Oceanic Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) estimates a 57% chance of La Nina conditions by late November, and 70% by January. 

With higher precipitation expected, Idaho rivers and reservoirs will hopefully be back to normal levels going into next year&#x27;s growing season. With Idaho having 22,877 farms and 11.5 million acres of farmland, water is life.   

La Nina Weather 2024 - Cordey Kissiah

This image above shows the variation between El Nino and La Nina. As you can see, the La Nina brings cold pressure in and States like Idaho benefit with increased moisture. 

McCandless, Royce Nov. 2024- With La Nina on the horizon Idaho hopes for more moisture after historically hot, dry year.   With La Niña on the horizon, Idaho hopes for more moisture after historically hot, dry year | State News | postregister.com

Colorado&#x27;s Winter Storms bring drought relief to Southeast Colorado, Zane Steele, Week 3
Contains unread posts
Zane Steele posted Nov 20, 2024 1:04 PM
https://coloradosun.com/2024/11/08/first-winter-storm-drought-relief-southeast-colorado/

Greetings Class,

For this weeks discussion I decided to learn a little more about where I am from and where I am stationed, with that I have decided to look at an article that relates to the winter storms in Colorado. 

Summary

The article from The Colorado Sun (November 8, 2024) discusses the impact of early winter storms in southern and eastern Colorado, which have brought much-needed snowfall to the region. While the early snowfall represents a 200% increase in November compared to other years the article warns the audience there are currently still drought conditions. Roughly 34% of the state had been experiencing some level of drought early this winter. While snow precipitation looks promising, the full impact will be realized only if the winter months continue to bring enough moisture.  The early storms will help to increase the soil moisture in the eastern plains area of the state that had low levels of rainfall of the summer of 2024. Snowpacks are critical for Colorado&#x27;s water supply, as they not only help increase soil moisture when they fall, but they also store water that gradually melts in the spring, helping to maintain soil moisture levels for agriculture and ecosystem health throughout the year.

Course connections

In Colorado, snowpack plays a vital role in replenishing soil moisture during the winter months. The snow provides not just immediate benefits by adding moisture to the soil, but also long-term relief when it melts in the spring, contributing to water availability for both agricultural and environmental needs. Snowpacks help prevent soil erosion, reduce wildfire risks, and promote soil health by maintaining organic matter and nutrients in the soil. This cycle of snow accumulation and gradual meltwater runoff is crucial for soil stability and water supply in regions that rely on seasonal snowmelt. 

The snow storms that happen in Colorado contribute to the states largest water reservoir by volume.  Snowmelt runoff provides as much as 80% of Colorado’s annual surface water supply.(Bastone) 

Additionally, the snowpacks add to weathering process of soil. The moisture from the snowstorms helps to breakdown the parent material contributing to soil formation. A physical change occurs with the freezing and thawing process happens breaking down larger particles into smaller and more fertile particles. 

The gradual release of water from the snowpacks will add to soil moisture and improve the rate of the soil to weather. 

When the melting occurs a chemical reaction is possible via dissolution, or oxidation. By breaking down certain minerals, nutrients become available for plant growth. Which will improve the fertility of the soil. 

The article talks about the importance of snowstorms for increasing soil moisture levels, particularly in regions like the eastern plains of Colorado that had experienced drought conditions. This is directly related to the water-holding capacity of the soil. Snowmelt infiltrates the soil and increases its ability to retain moisture, improving its porosity. 



 

References

Bastone, K. (2024, May 17). Understanding Snow - Water Education Colorado. Water Education Colorado. https://watereducationcolorado.org/publications-and-radio/headwaters-magazine/spring-2024-the-snow-issue/understanding-snow/#/

Bausch, N. S. (2024, January 6). Colorado farmers begin 2024 with less soil moisture than 2023. Successful Farming. https://www.agriculture.com/colorado-farmers-begin-2024-with-less-soil-moisture-than-2023-8423159#:~:text=Productive%20snowstorms%20during%20the%20second%20half%20of,very%20short%2C%2029%%20short%2C%20and%2055%%20adequate.

Mullane, S., Mullane, S., &amp; Mullane, S. (2024, November 8). The first winter storm of the season brings drought relief to southeast Colorado. The Colorado Sun. https://coloradosun.com/2024/11/08/first-winter-storm-drought-relief-southeast-colorado/

### Response to Amanda Kerrick’s Post: *South America Drought*

Hi Amanda,  

Your post does a fantastic job of connecting the drought in the Amazon to its cascading impacts on soil, biodiversity, and human livelihoods. I found your mention of Terra Preta particularly intriguing—it’s a brilliant example of how ancient soil management practices can inform modern solutions. Magnesium ties into this conversation as it’s vital for chlorophyll production, enabling photosynthesis in plants. With the Amazon’s declining moisture and forest cover, the disruption of photosynthesis could further deplete soil nutrient cycles, including magnesium availability, intensifying the challenges you described.

Do you think leveraging biochar techniques like those that created Terra Preta could help restore soils impacted by the drought, especially in maintaining magnesium and other essential nutrients?  

-George

### Response to Angel Delgado-leach’s Post: *Wk 3 - Angel*

Hi Angel,  

Your post on satellite technology’s role in monitoring soil moisture is fascinating and highlights how innovative tools are shaping environmental management. What’s especially cool is how magnesium ties into this picture—it’s a key component in soil nutrient cycling and plant health, and monitoring soil moisture could indirectly help track how well magnesium and other nutrients are retained in drought-prone or waterlogged soils.  

Do you think future satellite models could integrate nutrient-specific data, like magnesium or calcium availability, to give an even clearer picture of soil health? It seems like a logical next step for optimizing both crop yields and soil conservation.  

Great insights!  

-George

**ASSISTANT**:
### Extended Response to Amanda Kerrick’s Post: *South America Drought*

Hi Amanda,  

Your post does a fantastic job of connecting the drought in the Amazon to its cascading impacts on soil, biodiversity, and human livelihoods. I found your mention of Terra Preta particularly intriguing—it’s a brilliant example of how ancient soil management practices can inform modern solutions. Magnesium ties into this conversation as it’s vital for chlorophyll production, enabling photosynthesis in plants. With the Amazon’s declining moisture and forest cover, the disruption of photosynthesis could further deplete soil nutrient cycles, including magnesium availability, intensifying the challenges you described.

I’m currently working with a biostimulant that contains biochar, and I have a sample produced from pecan shells, which makes for a sprayer-nozzle-friendly particle size—no clogging issues! Biochar shows huge promise in agriculture, especially for retaining nutrients like magnesium in the soil, improving water retention, and boosting microbial life. I’ve seen its benefits firsthand on my small-scale operations. It’s amazing how techniques like biochar production, rooted in the Terra Preta practices, could help restore degraded soils in the Amazon and beyond.

Do you think scaling up the use of biochar or integrating it with agroforestry could help mitigate some of the impacts of deforestation and soil degradation in drought-prone areas like the Amazon?  

-George  

---

### Extended Response to Angel Delgado-leach’s Post: *Wk 3 - Angel*

Hi Angel,  

Your post on satellite technology’s role in monitoring soil moisture is fascinating and highlights how innovative tools are shaping environmental management. What’s especially cool is how magnesium ties into this picture—it’s a key component in soil nutrient cycling and plant health, and monitoring soil moisture could indirectly help track how well magnesium and other nutrients are retained in drought-prone or waterlogged soils.

I know that satellite imagery will eventually have these capabilities. In fact, platforms like SkyFi are already paving the way, leveraging advanced technology like hyperspectral imaging to analyze soil and vegetation conditions. When we combine satellite imagery with automated ground truth data collection, we’ll likely be able to determine soil nutrient content, including magnesium levels, with a high degree of accuracy. This could completely transform how farmers manage soil fertility and optimize crop yields. It’s exciting to think about where this technology is headed and the possibilities it opens for sustainable agriculture.

What applications do you see for this kind of tech in regions experiencing extreme soil degradation or in advancing precision agriculture practices?  

-George

**ASSISTANT**:
Based on the information provided in the document, the top five most abundant soil types within the Williamson County, Texas area (as listed in the Soil Map) are:

1. **Houston Black clay (HoB)** - **42,402.3 acres (5.8% of the area)**  
   - **Properties**: Found on 1 to 3 percent slopes, this soil is a deep, fertile clay type that supports robust agricultural activities, particularly crops like cotton and sorghum.

2. **Branyon clay (BrB)** - **40,706.1 acres (5.6% of the area)**  
   - **Properties**: Found on 1 to 3 percent slopes, this clay soil has high water retention and nutrient content, ideal for farming but can be challenging due to slow drainage.

3. **Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD)** - **53,544.8 acres (7.4% of the area)**  
   - **Properties**: Found on 1 to 8 percent slopes, this cobbly clay has limited depth to bedrock, often used for grazing or wildlife habitat rather than intensive cultivation.

4. **Eckrant stony clay (EeB)** - **48,225.0 acres (6.6% of the area)**  
   - **Properties**: Found on 0 to 3 percent slopes, it has a high stone content, making it suitable for rangeland and less favorable for crop production.

5. **Denton silty clay (DnB)** - **30,183.2 acres (4.2% of the area)**  
   - **Properties**: Found on 1 to 3 percent slopes, this silty clay has good nutrient-holding capacity and is often used for agriculture, including grain and forage crops.

Let me know if you&#x27;d like to explore more about their uses, challenges, or management strategies.

Amanda Kerrick posted Nov 28, 2024 12:38 PM
Hello Class. 

The AOI that I chose was Skagit County, Washington, because this is where I currently reside.

soil 

The year of the data that I will be using is from August 27, 2024.

The top 5 most abundant soils in this area from this date are shown in the image below: 



The date of the oldest archived data is from 1960 and the top 5 soils are shown in the image below: 



This area is very mountainous and has many little inlets and sounds  that make up the Western coasts of the county. It also has volcanic activity, which impacts the soil. I noticed that from the archived data there was not as many soil types as there are in the recent years, but 4 of the top 5 from 1960 were classified as &quot;Rough mountainous/broken land&quot;. In the detailed definitions of this type of soil it states that in all of these soil/land types that there is &quot;glacial material/debris&quot;, making the top soil very rocky and bouldery.

Weather patterns in the area vary. Thinking about the PACNW, someone might think that it is very rainy, and yes most of it is actually considered a rainforest because of the amount of precipitation that happens in some areas. But there are other areas, on the leeside of mountains where the rain doesn&#x27;t happen quite as often, this is called a &quot;rainshadow&quot;. Because of the lack of rain the soil is drier and not as useful in agriculture or forestry as other parts of the county. For the top 5 of the recent soils, they all get a significant amount of rain, with the highest being Kindy gravelly silt loam getting 80 inches of annual precipitation. Climatology plays an important role in much of the soil in this area, with mean temperatures in the 40s-50s.

One impact to the soil is the logging that is being done in the forests and the forest fires that happen every summer and burn large parts of the land. Another impact is because all of the rain, that landslides and erosion is very common, as well as avalanches. Looking at the SuperFund site, there were a lot of sites around Washington, but none specifically for Skagit County. I also looked at the population rise from 1990-2023 and it increased by 1.5% ( about 52,000 people) and while that may seem like a lot, it is not compared to other parts of the state with the highest population increase being in Lincoln County, in Easter Washington. This could potentially be from the increasing housing costs, but the affect of a growing population can impact the soils with increased in consumers, more land use to include increased agriculture and commercial building. 

References:

Population. USDA ERS - Data Products. (n.d.). https://data.ers.usda.gov/reports.aspx?ID=17827

Web soil survey. (n.d.). https://websoilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/app/WebSoilSurvey.aspx

The population of North Carolina was approximately 2,206,000 people around 1918, according to the US Census Bureau. As of 2024, the approximate number is 10,975,000 (census.gov, 2024). Brodie Henson posted Nov 27, 2024 5:22 AMLast edited: Wednesday, November 27, 2024 6:54 AM EST
Hello everyone,

For our final project and this week&#x27;s discussion I have decided to conduct my soil research on Berkeley County in the state of South Carolina. I have selected this county and state because this is where I currently live. I would like to be a game warden in the area I currently live in eventually. 

Here is the soil survey map for Berkeley County, South Carolina:

Soil Data (Current)

Berkeley county has 787,669.40 acres within its boundaries and these acres contain 39 different soil profiles. The most recent tabular soil data was taken on August 29th, 2024 and the most recent spatial data was taken on September 16th, 2019. Here are the top five soil profiles currently in my area of interest based of the most acreage and percentages:

Current Soil Profiles

I was able to sift through the records and find a surprising soil sample report that was conducted in the year of 1918. This sampling was conducted by W. J. Latimer who was conducting the sampling on behalf of the United States Department of Agriculture. Below are the results from the 1918 sampling:

1918

I found it very interesting that the names of the soils have changed so much. However, loam is still the most abundant soil profiles from then to now. I feel as though this is due to the close proximity to the ocean as well as the heavy saltwater intrusion that occurs in the flats of the low country due to high tides. The soil composition has changed a lot due to human occurrences. Berkeley county is one of the fasted growing counties in the country due to its beautiful weather and sense of tourism. This makes it a highly desired place to live. Construction is greatly impacting the area to build more housing and infrastructure which can definitely affect soil profiling. Construction can add soil to different areas causing a complete change in a soil profile. Construction also utilizes compaction which is the use of heavy equipment which pushes the soil down compacting it to make it flatter. 

More people moving to Berkeley County also results in the overflow of trash in landfills. This results in more soil being used for landfills which can damage soil of not utilized correctly resulting in pollution. Landfills can throw off acidity levels making them unsafe for plants and other organisms within the ecosystems affected. This is due to industrial chemicals and other wastes being thrown into landfills.

These are just two examples of how humans have could have potentially changed the soil profiles within Berkeley County over time.

I hope everyone has a great rest of the week and I look forward to replies. Have a Happy Thanksgiving!

-Brodie

**ASSISTANT**:
**Williamson County, Texas**

For this project, I’ve chosen Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). This county holds personal significance as it is part of the Edwards Plateau region, where I reside and conduct much of my work in arboriculture and environmental science. It’s also fascinating because of its karst geology, which strongly influences soil formation, hydrology, and ecosystem dynamics.

**AOI Map and Data**

Here’s a soil survey map of Williamson County:  
*(Embed a map image from the Web Soil Survey or similar tool here)*  

The most recent tabular soil data is from **August 2024**, and the spatial data was last updated in **September 2019**.

**Top 5 Most Abundant Current Soils**  
Based on the latest Web Soil Survey data, the top 5 most abundant soil series in Williamson County are as follows:

| Symbol | Name                                        | Acres   | %     |
|--------|---------------------------------------------|---------|-------|
| ErG    | Eckrant extremely stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes | 48,310  | 6.7   |
| ErC    | Eckrant-Rock outcrop complex, rolling       | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DeB    | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes    | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DoC    | Doss silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes      | 32,200  | 4.5   |
| Br     | Branyon clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes         | 34,560  | 4.8   |

**Oldest Archived Soil Survey**  
The oldest available soil survey for Williamson County dates back to **1983**. Here are the top 5 soils from that report:

| Symbol | Name                                        | Acres   | %     |
|--------|---------------------------------------------|---------|-------|
| ErG    | Eckrant extremely stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes | 48,310  | 6.7   |
| ErC    | Eckrant-Rock outcrop complex, rolling       | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DeB    | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes    | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DoC    | Doss silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes      | 32,200  | 4.5   |
| Br     | Branyon clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes         | 34,560  | 4.8   |

**Reflection on Changes Over Time**

While the top soil types haven’t shifted dramatically in percentage or presence, demographic and land-use changes in Williamson County likely have impacted soil health and distribution. The population has exploded, with suburban sprawl and infrastructure development rapidly replacing agricultural and natural lands. This shift likely affects soil compaction, permeability, and erosion rates.

**Demographic/Land Use Shifts**  
Urbanization has increased impervious surfaces, reducing infiltration and exacerbating runoff and soil erosion. Additionally, the pressure of farming practices has likely shifted from traditional crops to development, leading to habitat fragmentation.

**Natural Disasters**  
Droughts are a significant natural disaster in this region, affecting soil moisture, microbial activity, and vegetation. Severe weather events like heavy rains also increase erosion, particularly in areas with shallow soils like those found on the Edwards Plateau.

**Pollution and SuperFund Sites**  
Williamson County has a relatively low number of SuperFund sites compared to more industrialized areas. However, the growth in urban and industrial activities increases the potential for soil contamination, especially from construction, oil leaks, and pesticide use in suburban landscapes.

**Future Considerations**  
Efforts like using cover crops, reintroducing native plants, and promoting sustainable land management can help maintain soil health. Monitoring soil changes over time, particularly in a region with karst geology, is essential to balancing ecological integrity with human development. Biochar also shows potential for improving soil health, as I’ve seen firsthand in my small-scale operations, using biochar made from pecan shells.

Let me know your thoughts and how this relates to your regions of study!

- USDA Web Soil Survey. (2024). https://websoilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/  
- Williamson County Soil Survey (1983). https://soilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/  
- Population Trends and Urbanization Data. https://www.census.gov/

**ASSISTANT**:
**Williamson County, Texas**

For this project, I’ve chosen Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). This county holds a special place for me, as I live and work here, and it’s part of the Edwards Plateau, a region shaped by unique karst geology. The interaction of limestone, water, and soil in this area creates fascinating ecosystems that I study regularly in my work with trees and soil health. Exploring the soils of Williamson County feels like connecting with the very foundation of this landscape.

---

### **AOI Map and Data**

Here’s a soil survey map of Williamson County:  
*(Embed map image here.)*

- **Tabular Data:** Version 22, updated August 30, 2024  
- **Spatial Data:** Version 7, updated June 11, 2020  

---

### **Top 5 Most Abundant Current Soils**

Based on the latest Web Soil Survey data, the five most abundant soil types in Williamson County are:

| Symbol | Name                                        | Acres   | %     |
|--------|---------------------------------------------|---------|-------|
| ErG    | Eckrant extremely stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes | 48,310  | 6.7   |
| ErC    | Eckrant-Rock outcrop complex, rolling       | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DeB    | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes    | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DoC    | Doss silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes      | 32,200  | 4.5   |
| Br     | Branyon clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes         | 34,560  | 4.8   |

---

### **Oldest Archived Soil Survey**

I also dug into the archived soil survey for Williamson County, which dates back to **1983**. Here&#x27;s what the top five soil types looked like then:

| Symbol | Name                                        | Acres   | %     |
|--------|---------------------------------------------|---------|-------|
| ErG    | Eckrant extremely stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes | 48,310  | 6.7   |
| ErC    | Eckrant-Rock outcrop complex, rolling       | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DeB    | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes    | 42,530  | 5.9   |
| DoC    | Doss silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes      | 32,200  | 4.5   |
| Br     | Branyon clay, 0 to 1 percent slopes         | 34,560  | 4.8   |

---

### **Reflection on Change Over Time**

While the data from 1983 and 2024 shows consistency in the dominant soil types, the changes in how these soils are managed are where the real story lies. Williamson County has transformed from a primarily agricultural area to one of the fastest-growing counties in Texas. This shift impacts the soils in profound ways:

1. **Demographic &amp; Land Use Shifts:**  
   Rapid urbanization has introduced more impervious surfaces like roads and buildings, reducing natural infiltration. This has likely increased runoff and erosion while altering water retention in the soil.

2. **Natural Disasters:**  
   Droughts and intense rain events are common in this region. Droughts stress the soil microbiome, while heavy rains exacerbate erosion, particularly in areas where the natural vegetation has been removed.

3. **Pollution:**  
   As the population grows, there’s a risk of soil contamination from construction activities, oil leaks, and pesticide runoff in suburban landscapes. The karst geology makes these issues more critical, as pollutants can infiltrate the groundwater quickly.

---

### **Future Considerations**

Efforts to improve soil health, such as incorporating biochar, can make a significant difference in this region. I’ve personally worked with a biostimulant containing biochar, some of which was produced from pecan shells—a material that creates a fine, sprayer-friendly particle size. Biochar has shown remarkable promise in enhancing soil structure and water-holding capacity, even in challenging conditions like those found here. Its ability to restore soils aligns beautifully with sustainable land management practices.

---

### **Closing Thoughts**

Williamson County’s soils tell a story of resilience and change. By looking at the data and reflecting on how this landscape has been shaped over time, I’m reminded of the importance of balancing development with conservation. As we continue to grow as a community, maintaining the health of these soils will be critical—not just for the environment but for all who call this region home.

---

- Williamson County Soil Survey (1983). https://soilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/  
- Population Trends and Urbanization Data. https://www.census.gov/

### **Reflection on Change Over Time**
Comparing the soil data from 1983 to 2024 highlights both continuity and shifts. While certain soil types, like the Eckrant and Houston Black clay series, remain prominent, urbanization and land use changes have undoubtedly influenced soil health and distribution:

1. **Demographic &amp; Land Use Shifts:**  
   Williamson County has experienced explosive growth in recent decades, transforming from a rural agricultural area into a hub of urban development. This has increased impervious surfaces, leading to more runoff and erosion, while reducing natural infiltration and altering soil structure.

2. **Natural Disasters:**  
   Frequent droughts and intense rainstorms characteristic of Central Texas continue to stress soils. Droughts reduce organic matter and microbial life, while heavy rains accelerate erosion, particularly in areas without vegetative cover.

3. **Pollution &amp; Agricultural Practices:**  
   Urban expansion often brings potential soil contamination from construction activities, oil leaks, and the use of pesticides and fertilizers in suburban landscapes. The karst geology of the Edwards Plateau makes these issues even more pressing, as pollutants can rapidly infiltrate groundwater.

---

### **Future Considerations**

In my work, I’ve experimented with a biostimulant containing biochar made from pecan shells, which offers a fine particle size ideal for use with sprayer nozzles. Biochar enhances soil structure, increases water-holding capacity, and improves microbial activity. Scaling such solutions could restore degraded soils and support sustainable development in this fast-growing county.

From the &quot;Full Soil Report&quot; and soil survey data for Williamson County, there don&#x27;t seem to be any directly identified Superfund sites in the data. If you want to explore further impacts:

1. **Urban Development:** The increase in impervious surfaces such as roads and buildings reduces natural water infiltration, potentially leading to erosion and compacted soils in urban areas.

### Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas

For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). Located in the heart of the Edwards Plateau, this region features a fascinating interplay between urban development, agricultural activity, and natural landscapes. I chose this site because I live in Central Texas and have always been intrigued by the delicate balance between preserving native ecosystems and meeting the demands of a growing population.

#### Recent Soil Data and Survey Details
- **Tabular Soil Data**: Version 22, August 30, 2024  
- **Spatial Soil Data**: Version 7, June 11, 2020  

The top five most abundant soils in the current Web Soil Survey are:  
| **Symbol** | **Name**                                   | **Acres**  | **%**  |  
|------------|-------------------------------------------|------------|--------|  
| EaD        | Eckrant cobbly clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes | 53,544.80  | 7.40%  |  
| EeB        | Eckrant stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes  | 48,225.00  | 6.60%  |  
| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 42,402.30  | 5.80%  |  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 40,706.10  | 5.60%  |  
| DnB        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes   | 30,183.20  | 4.20%  |  

#### Archived Soil Survey Data
The earliest archived soil survey for Williamson County is from 1983. Here are the top five most abundant soils from that report:  
| **Symbol** | **Name**                                   | **Acres**  | **%**  |  
|------------|-------------------------------------------|------------|--------|  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 59,360     | 8.30%  |  
| EaD        | Eckrant stony clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes  | 58,880     | 8.20%  |  
| DaC        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes   | 48,640     | 6.80%  |  
| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 40,000     | 5.60%  |  
| SeC        | San Saba clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes       | 37,760     | 5.30%  |  

---

#### Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts
Over time, shifts in land use, urban expansion, and agricultural practices have likely impacted Williamson County soils. Here are a few key points:  

- **Urban Development**: Rapid population growth and urbanization have resulted in more impervious surfaces like roads and buildings. This reduces natural water infiltration, increasing erosion and compaction, particularly in clay-heavy soils like Houston Black and Branyon.

- **Agricultural Practices**: The prevalence of Houston Black and Branyon clay soils reflects the county’s agricultural heritage. However, long-term farming practices, such as tillage and fertilizer use, may have altered soil profiles and nutrient availability over the years.

- **Industrial Influence**: While the soil survey and reports do not identify active Superfund sites in Williamson County, localized impacts from landfills and quarries have likely disrupted soil composition and hydrology in specific areas.

- **Environmental Changes**: Drought cycles and flood events in Central Texas also play a role in soil health. Soils with high clay content, such as Branyon and Denton, are particularly susceptible to compaction during droughts and erosion during heavy rains.

---

#### Reflection on Change Over Time
The comparison between current and archived data shows how soil classifications and use have evolved. The presence of quarries and landfills suggests localized disruptions, while urban growth has reshaped the landscape, potentially impacting soil chemistry and structure. Despite these changes, many of the dominant soil types, like Eckrant cobbly clay and Houston Black clay, remain central to the region’s identity and functionality.

These observations reinforce the importance of sustainable land management. Whether through urban planning, regenerative agriculture, or conservation efforts, balancing development with environmental stewardship is essential for preserving the health and productivity of Williamson County soils.

---

- USDA. (1983). *Soil Survey of Williamson County, Texas*. Retrieved from [Internet Archive](https://archive.org/details/usda-soil-surveys).  
- USDA NRCS. (2024). *Web Soil Survey*. Retrieved from [https://websoilsurvey.sc.egov.usda.gov/](https://websoilsurvey.sc.egov.usda.gov/).

---

### Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas

For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). Located in Central Texas, this county presents a fascinating dichotomy of soils shaped by its geography and human activity. A natural division runs through the county along Interstate Highway 35, with heavy limestone soils dominating the west and fertile black clay soils to the east. I chose this site because of its rich ecological and agricultural history, and I live nearby, making it personally relevant to explore.

---

#### Recent Soil Data and Survey Details
- **Tabular Soil Data**: Version 22, August 30, 2024  
- **Spatial Soil Data**: Version 7, June 11, 2020  

The top five most abundant soils in the current Web Soil Survey are:  
| **Symbol** | **Name**                                   | **Acres**  | **%**  |  
|------------|-------------------------------------------|------------|--------|  
| EaD        | Eckrant cobbly clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes | 53,544.80  | 7.40%  |  
| EeB        | Eckrant stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes  | 48,225.00  | 6.60%  |  
| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 42,402.30  | 5.80%  |  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 40,706.10  | 5.60%  |  
| DnB        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes   | 30,183.20  | 4.20%  |

#### Archived Soil Survey Data
The earliest archived soil survey for Williamson County is from 1983. Here are the top five most abundant soils from that report:  
| **Symbol** | **Name**                                   | **Acres**  | **%**  |  
|------------|-------------------------------------------|------------|--------|  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 59,360     | 8.30%  |  
| EaD        | Eckrant stony clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes  | 58,880     | 8.20%  |  
| DaC        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes   | 48,640     | 6.80%  |  
| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 40,000     | 5.60%  |  
| SeC        | San Saba clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes       | 37,760     | 5.30%  |  

---

#### Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts
The division of Williamson County by Interstate 35 reveals distinct geological and land use patterns. The west side, with its limestone-heavy soils like Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD) and Eckrant stony clay (EeB), supports ranching and natural landscapes. In contrast, the eastern side, dominated by the fertile black clay soils like Houston Black (HoB) and Branyon clay (BrB), has been historically vital for agriculture, especially row crops like cotton and sorghum.

Here are a few notable insights:  

- **Urban Growth**: Rapid urbanization along the Interstate 35 corridor has transformed the county, introducing impervious surfaces like roads and buildings. This development reduces water infiltration and increases erosion risk, particularly in the east where clay soils are prone to compaction.

- **Agricultural Practices**: Long-term farming, particularly in the eastern regions, has impacted soil profiles. The repeated use of fertilizers and tilling has altered nutrient availability and, in some cases, contributed to erosion and runoff.

- **Environmental Concerns**: Local quarries and landfills, as well as drought and flood cycles, have affected soil chemistry and hydrology. Although the review of EPA SuperFund data did not identify active sites within Williamson County, nearby environmental disruptions could indirectly influence soil quality.

---

#### Reflection on Change Over Time
The 1983 survey shows many of the same soil types, but shifts in urban and agricultural use have likely led to changes in soil structure and health. The expansion of urban areas, particularly around Round Rock and Georgetown, has transformed previously agricultural lands into developed spaces, reducing soil biodiversity and natural functions. 

This analysis highlights the importance of balancing land use with ecological stewardship. Sustainable practices, such as soil regeneration and urban planning that integrates natural landscapes, can help mitigate the impacts of development and preserve soil health for future generations.

---

- USDA. (1983). *Soil Survey of Williamson County, Texas*. Retrieved from [Internet Archive](https://archive.org/details/usda-soil-surveys).  
- USDA NRCS. (2024). *Web Soil Survey*. Retrieved from [https://websoilsurvey.sc.egov.usda.gov/](https://websoilsurvey.sc.egov.usda.gov/).

### Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas

For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). Located in Central Texas, this county presents a fascinating dichotomy of soils shaped by its geography and human activity. A natural division runs through the county along Interstate Highway 35, with heavy limestone soils dominating the west and fertile black clay soils to the east. I chose this site because of its rich ecological and agricultural history, and I live nearby, making it personally relevant to explore.

---

#### Recent Soil Data and Survey Details
- **Tabular Soil Data**: Version 22, August 30, 2024  
- **Spatial Soil Data**: Version 7, June 11, 2020

| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 42,402.30  | 5.80%  |  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 40,706.10  | 5.60%  |  
| DnB        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes   | 30,183.20  | 4.20%  |

#### Archived Soil Survey Data
The earliest archived soil survey for Williamson County is from 1983. Here are the top five most abundant soils from that report:  
| **Symbol** | **Name**                                   | **Acres**  | **%**  |  
|------------|-------------------------------------------|------------|--------|  
| BrB        | Branyon clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes        | 59,360     | 8.30%  |  
| EaD        | Eckrant stony clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes  | 58,880     | 8.20%  |  
| DaC        | Denton silty clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes   | 48,640     | 6.80%  |  
| HoB        | Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes  | 40,000     | 5.60%  |  
| SeC        | San Saba clay, 1 to 5 percent slopes       | 37,760     | 5.30%  |  

---

#### Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts
The division of Williamson County by Interstate 35 reveals distinct geological and land use patterns. The west side, with its limestone-heavy soils like Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD) and Eckrant stony clay (EeB), supports ranching and natural landscapes. In contrast, the eastern side, dominated by the fertile black clay soils like Houston Black (HoB) and Branyon clay (BrB), has been historically vital for agriculture, especially row crops like cotton and sorghum.

Urban growth along Interstate 35 has transformed Williamson County, particularly in cities like Round Rock, Georgetown, and Cedar Park. This expansion is now accelerating northwestward along Highway 183, stretching from Leander and Liberty Hill toward Lampasas. The transformation includes the extension of Highway 183 into a toll road up to Highway 29, with plans for further expansion. To illustrate the scale of this growth, Liberty Hill ISD is now the 12th fastest-growing school district in the United States, highlighting the rapid demographic and infrastructural changes in this region.

---

#### Reflection on Change Over Time
The 1983 survey shows many of the same soil types, but shifts in urban and agricultural use have led to significant changes in soil structure and health. In urbanized areas, impervious surfaces have disrupted water infiltration and increased erosion. The western limestone soils, once predominantly ranchland, now face pressures from construction and development. Similarly, the fertile clay soils in the east have experienced compaction and nutrient depletion due to long-term agricultural practices.

These changes underscore the need for sustainable soil management. As development continues to expand northwest of Austin, balancing urban growth with ecological stewardship becomes crucial. Implementing strategies like soil regeneration, green spaces, and water management can help mitigate the impacts of rapid urbanization and ensure the long-term health of Williamson County&#x27;s diverse soils.

---

W4/5: Soil Surveys 
Step 1: Complete the checklist found in Week 4&#x27;s Learning Material: &quot;Web Soil Survey (WSS) Tips&quot;.

Step 2: Publish your post using the information you collected on the checklist no later than Friday of Week 4.  Your initial post must include, but is not limited to the following:

the specific US County and State you are using for your AOI
why you selected this site
an embedded map image (can be from the soil survey tool or elsewhere) that shows the location of your AOI
the year(s) of the data that they are using to create the web survey, this will different for most locations but should be fairly recent.  (NOTE: This web version is what you&#x27;ll use for your final project)
the top 5 most abundant soil series or associations in your county currently
the date of the oldest available archived (via pdf) soil survey from this area 
the top 5 most abundant soil series or associations from the old survey
your thoughts about change over time and potential soil impact from things like:
demographic/land use shifts
natural disasters
major pollution events or SuperFund sites
or anything else you can think of that might impact soils
These next TWO weeks are designed to get you started on your final project!  This should be a back-and-forth active discussion for the whole two weeks with double the amount of interactions with your instructor and fellow students. Initial posts are due Friday of Week 4 (rather than Wednesday) to give you a few more days. The more time you spend in the discussion now, the less time you&#x27;ll need at the end of the course when completing your final project presentation.

This assesses CO-3, CO-4 and CO-5.

Amanda Kerrick, Skagit County, WA
Contains unread posts
Amanda Kerrick posted Nov 28, 2024 12:38 PM
Hello Class. 

The AOI that I chose was Skagit County, Washington, because this is where I currently reside.

soil 

The year of the data that I will be using is from August 27, 2024.

The top 5 most abundant soils in this area from this date are shown in the image below: 



The date of the oldest archived data is from 1960 and the top 5 soils are shown in the image below: 



This area is very mountainous and has many little inlets and sounds  that make up the Western coasts of the county. It also has volcanic activity, which impacts the soil. I noticed that from the archived data there was not as many soil types as there are in the recent years, but 4 of the top 5 from 1960 were classified as &quot;Rough mountainous/broken land&quot;. In the detailed definitions of this type of soil it states that in all of these soil/land types that there is &quot;glacial material/debris&quot;, making the top soil very rocky and bouldery.

Weather patterns in the area vary. Thinking about the PACNW, someone might think that it is very rainy, and yes most of it is actually considered a rainforest because of the amount of precipitation that happens in some areas. But there are other areas, on the leeside of mountains where the rain doesn&#x27;t happen quite as often, this is called a &quot;rainshadow&quot;. Because of the lack of rain the soil is drier and not as useful in agriculture or forestry as other parts of the county. For the top 5 of the recent soils, they all get a significant amount of rain, with the highest being Kindy gravelly silt loam getting 80 inches of annual precipitation. Climatology plays an important role in much of the soil in this area, with mean temperatures in the 40s-50s.

One impact to the soil is the logging that is being done in the forests and the forest fires that happen every summer and burn large parts of the land. Another impact is because all of the rain, that landslides and erosion is very common, as well as avalanches. Looking at the SuperFund site, there were a lot of sites around Washington, but none specifically for Skagit County. I also looked at the population rise from 1990-2023 and it increased by 1.5% ( about 52,000 people) and while that may seem like a lot, it is not compared to other parts of the state with the highest population increase being in Lincoln County, in Easter Washington. This could potentially be from the increasing housing costs, but the affect of a growing population can impact the soils with increased in consumers, more land use to include increased agriculture and commercial building. 

References:

Population. USDA ERS - Data Products. (n.d.). https://data.ers.usda.gov/reports.aspx?ID=17827

Web soil survey. (n.d.). https://websoilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/app/WebSoilSurvey.aspx

The population of North Carolina was approximately 2,206,000 people around 1918, according to the US Census Bureau. As of 2024, the approximate number is 10,975,000 (census.gov, 2024). There have been diverse natural disasters, including bizarre snow and ice storms, massive flooding caused by hurricanes, and random earthquakes. The EPA SuperFund site lists 47 events in the state that have potentially triggered changes in soil chemistry. Of these 47, I could identify three major pollution events that didn’t occur in Stanly County but in the adjoining counties of Mecklenburg and Cabarrus (epa.gov, 2024). Stanly County has a large amount of farmland, specifically crops. As we’ve learned in past lessons, soil disturbances and disruptions in root systems cause soil alterations over time. I hypothesize that constant tillage and exposure to various chemicals also affect soil changes and/or adaptations in this area.

Week 4/5 Henson (Berkeley County, SC)
Brodie Henson posted Nov 27, 2024 5:22 AMLast edited: Wednesday, November 27, 2024 6:54 AM EST
Hello everyone,

For our final project and this week&#x27;s discussion I have decided to conduct my soil research on Berkeley County in the state of South Carolina. I have selected this county and state because this is where I currently live. I would like to be a game warden in the area I currently live in eventually. 

Here is the soil survey map for Berkeley County, South Carolina:

Soil Data (Current)

Berkeley county has 787,669.40 acres within its boundaries and these acres contain 39 different soil profiles. The most recent tabular soil data was taken on August 29th, 2024 and the most recent spatial data was taken on September 16th, 2019. Here are the top five soil profiles currently in my area of interest based of the most acreage and percentages:

Current Soil Profiles

I was able to sift through the records and find a surprising soil sample report that was conducted in the year of 1918. This sampling was conducted by W. J. Latimer who was conducting the sampling on behalf of the United States Department of Agriculture. Below are the results from the 1918 sampling:

1918

I found it very interesting that the names of the soils have changed so much. However, loam is still the most abundant soil profiles from then to now. I feel as though this is due to the close proximity to the ocean as well as the heavy saltwater intrusion that occurs in the flats of the low country due to high tides. The soil composition has changed a lot due to human occurrences. Berkeley county is one of the fasted growing counties in the country due to its beautiful weather and sense of tourism. This makes it a highly desired place to live. Construction is greatly impacting the area to build more housing and infrastructure which can definitely affect soil profiling. Construction can add soil to different areas causing a complete change in a soil profile. Construction also utilizes compaction which is the use of heavy equipment which pushes the soil down compacting it to make it flatter. 

More people moving to Berkeley County also results in the overflow of trash in landfills. This results in more soil being used for landfills which can damage soil of not utilized correctly resulting in pollution. Landfills can throw off acidity levels making them unsafe for plants and other organisms within the ecosystems affected. This is due to industrial chemicals and other wastes being thrown into landfills.

These are just two examples of how humans have could have potentially changed the soil profiles within Berkeley County over time.

I hope everyone has a great rest of the week and I look forward to replies. Have a Happy Thanksgiving!

-Brodie

References:

United States Department of Agriculture. (2000). Erosion and Sedimentation on Construction Sites. https://mostcenter.umd.edu/sites/default/files/2020-02/Erosion_and_Sedimentation_on_Construction_Sites.pdf

Rinkesh. (2023, November 16). Causes, effects and solutions to landfills. Conserve Energy Future. https://www.conserve-energy-future.com/causes-effects-solutions-of-landfills.php

Bannock County, ID-Craig
Contains unread posts
CRAIG NALLEY posted Nov 26, 2024 7:30 PM
Class,

For this week I chose to do some research about my home county of Bannock County, ID. The research into the soils around the area could become important in the near future when I find my future position with the Department of Fish and Game. Understanding the soil of my area will help me assist with wildlife management plans and the deep rooted agricultural aspect of Bannock County. 



As we can see there are quite a lot of different soils located in a pretty small area relatively to other counties in the country. This map encompasses 498,095 acres of land. With 172 different soil profiles found all over the county. The most recent soil survey was completed both spatial and tubular data taken August 22, 2024. The top five soils are listed here with there acreage and percentage of total found in the area. 



The oldest soil survey I could find in the archives for this area was from issued by the Department of Agriculture and Soil Conservation Service in September of 1987. I was kind of hoping for a survey that was a little older like the one in the video but unfortunately this is what was archived. The top five soils have some different soils and some that are very similar.



Analyzing a couple of the similar soils it looks like there has been a lot of erosion from the late 80s to now especially in the map symbol thirty. In 1987 there were 28,100 acres of a silt loam and in the most recent survey the amount of acreage has reduced down to 23, 899 acres of the same soil. Very interesting to see the changes between the two reports and it will be interesting to read deeper into the differences from then and now. 

I took one of the recommendations to look further into the soil of the area and a focus area that interests me a lot and that was the superfund. For those that do not know what a superfund is it stems from the late 1970&#x27;s where toxic waste sites were receiving national attention. Congress than passed the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation and Liability Act (CERCLA), this act became known informally as a superfund (EPA, n.d). A notable superfund that occurred in Bannock county was the Eastern Michaud Flats Contamination. This site contained cadmium and radium-226 in the soil that was at levels concerning to human health. The contamination came from two companies called FMC and Simplot. My dad used to work for FMC before it was closed in 2001 and remediation of the pollutants put off by the plant began. Since it&#x27;s closure the concentration of the waste in the soil has steadily decreased. All of the remediation was directed by the EPA and has shown some great strides over the last couple of decades. The site is still monitored to ensure the soil is still recovering and the remediation of the area was successful.  

Craig

Environmental Protection Agency. (2017, October 20). Eastern Michaud Flats Contamination Site Profile. EPA. https://cumulis.epa.gov/supercpad/SiteProfiles/index.cfm?fuseaction=second.Cleanup&amp;id=1001308#bkground

George Steward: Williamson County, Texas
George Steward posted Nov 30, 2024 2:12 PM•694 WordsLast edited: Sunday, December 1, 2024 8:09 AM EST
Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas
For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). Located in Central Texas, this county presents a fascinating dichotomy of soils shaped by its geography and human activity. A natural division runs through the county along Interstate Highway 35, with heavy limestone soils dominating the west and fertile black clay soils to the east. I chose this site because of its rich ecological and agricultural history, and I live nearby, making it personally relevant to explore.

AOI Map and Data
Here’s a soil survey map of Williamson County:


Recent Soil Data and Survey Details
Tabular Soil Data: Version 22, August 30, 2024

Spatial Soil Data: Version 7, June 11, 2020

The top five most abundant soils in the current Web Soil Survey are:

Eckrant cobbly clay, 1 to 8 percent slopes

Eckrant stony clay, 0 to 3 percent slopes

6.60%

Houston Black clay, 1 to 3 percent slopes

Archived Soil Survey Data
The earliest archived soil survey for Williamson County is from 1983. Here are the top five most abundant soils from that report:
