Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts
The division of Williamson County by Interstate 35 reveals distinct geological and land use patterns. The west side, with its limestone-heavy soils like Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD) and Eckrant stony clay (EeB), supports ranching and natural landscapes. In contrast, the eastern side, dominated by fertile black clay soils like Houston Black (HoB) and Branyon clay (BrB), has been historically vital for agriculture, especially row crops like cotton and sorghum.

Urban growth along Interstate 35 has transformed Williamson County, particularly in cities like Round Rock, Georgetown, and Cedar Park. This expansion is now accelerating northwestward along Highway 183, stretching from Leander and Liberty Hill toward Lampasas. The transformation includes the extension of Highway 183 into a toll road up to Highway 29, with plans for further expansion. To illustrate the scale of this growth, Liberty Hill ISD is now the 12th fastest-growing school district in the United States, highlighting the rapid demographic and infrastructural changes in this region.

Reflection on Change Over Time
The 1983 survey shows many of the same soil types, but shifts in urban and agricultural use have led to significant changes in soil structure and health. In urbanized areas, impervious surfaces have disrupted water infiltration and increased erosion. The western limestone soils, once predominantly ranchland, now face pressures from construction and development. Similarly, the fertile clay soils in the east have experienced compaction and nutrient depletion due to long-term agricultural practices.

These changes highlight the need for sustainable soil management.  In 1983, Williamson County, Texas, had an estimated population of approximately 92,624 residents. By 2024, this number has surged to an estimated 722,109, reflecting significant growth over the past four decades. As development continues to expand northwest of Austin, balancing urban growth with ecological stewardship becomes all the more important. Implementing strategies like soil regeneration, green spaces, and water management can help mitigate the impacts of rapid urbanization and ensure the long-term health of Williamson County&#x27;s diverse soils.

References
EPA. (2024). Superfund Sites Where You Live. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/superfund/search-superfund-sites-where-you-live.

Natural Resources Conservation Service. (1983). Soil survey of Williamson County, Texas. U.S. Department of Agriculture. Retrieved from https://archive.org/details/usda-soil-surveys 

Natural Resources Conservation Service. (2024). Web soil survey. U.S. Department of Agriculture. https://websoilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov

U.S. Census Bureau. (n.d.). Resident Population in Williamson County, TX. Federal Reserve Economic Data. Retrieved November 30, 2024, from https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/TXWILL5POP

World Population Review. (2024). Williamson County, Texas Population 2024. Retrieved November 30, 2024, from https://worldpopulationreview.com/us-counties/texas/williamson-county

U.S. Census Bureau. (2024). County population estimates. https://www.census.gov

Keily Leiva-Diaz posted Nov 29, 2024 8:00 PM
Hello everyone,

This week I did research on Queen Anne&#x27;s county in Maryland. I chose Prince George&#x27;s County originally because that&#x27;s where I live now but some of the data wasn&#x27;t as up to date as I&#x27;d wanted. Queen Anne&#x27;s has been around for almost as long as PG though so I decided to make the small switch. 

Here is the soil survey map for Queen Anne&#x27;s county.

soil map

The tabular data says version 21 and it was recorded on September 6, 2024. The spatial data says version 8 and it was recorded on September 13, 2019. Currently, the most abundant soil profiles in this area are mentioned below. I&#x27;m familiar with the sassafras profile because it&#x27;s the oldest soil in Maryland but seeing it on a map with other soils put the numbers into perspective.

Below were the five most abundant profiles during that time and I have to say I was shocked. Not only because some of the soils are different but because the acres have changed a lot.

However, sassafras type soil continues to be the most abundant along with silt loam. There&#x27;s no more than a 2 percent slope which I believe benefits the soil since there is little to no erosion. The silt loam is usually found near areas where it&#x27;s difficult to drain completely but this can be good for many crops like corn and soybeans if proper drainage is in place.

The population during this time was around 18,000 and according to the soil survey from 1966, there were a few learning curves needed to work with the soil. &quot;All the soils are acid, and some are extremely acid. For these reasons, fertilizer and lime are needed to obtain high yields of most crops.&quot; (USDA, 1966) They would learn from past mistakes using the lime and fertilizer on the crops and test the soils for chemicals. These days the population is over 50,000 and steadily growing by almost 2% every year. This means there is a lot of construction to accommodate the new population and plenty of foot traffic in the area that hasn&#x27;t stopped in decades.

-Keily

Hello Class!

Apologies for my later than usual post. I&#x27;ve been quite busy this holiday week. Hope you all had a great Thanksgiving! For this week&#x27;s discussion, I chose Montgomery County, Ohio as my area of interest for the web soil survey research. 



I chose this county for not only being the county I grew up in, but also for its diverse landscape throughout the region. Montgomery County houses one of Ohio&#x27;s major cities known as Dayton and includes a lot of industrial complexes. Unknown	Miami Black Clay Loam	18,000	5.80%
Unknown	Miami Loam	14,000	4.50%
Unknown	Meadow	7,200	2.30%
Change Over Time &amp; Potential Soil Impact

From 1900 to present day, Montgomery County underwent significant urban development and challenges. Interurban travel established its major city, Dayton as a regional hub, and the 1913 Great Flood prompted flood control measures. By the 1920s, Montgomery County saw rapid industrial growth, creating extensive city plans and zoning laws. The Great Depression slowed development, but World War II boosted wartime production. Post-war efforts focused on freeway systems, urban renewal, and suburban expansion. Population throughout the county peaked in 1960 before declining. Community preservation, historic districts, and infrastructure projects revitalized neighborhoods. Initiatives for the county&#x27;s major city, Dayton, like CitiPlan 20/20 and landmark developments like Fifth Third Field and the Schuster Center shaped Dayton and Montgomery County&#x27;s modern identity.

Furthermore, in Montgomery County, Ohio, soil changes stem from agriculture, urbanization, and climate change. Fertile &quot;Miamian&quot; soil is prone to erosion, especially on slopes, due to past tillage practices. Urbanization has reduced soil permeability, causing runoff and compaction. Climate change could worsen soil saturation, erosion, and nutrient loss due to heavier rainfall. Mitigation strategies include conservation tillage, cover cropping, and green infrastructure like rain gardens. Urban forestry can also enhance water infiltration and reduce erosion, addressing long-term soil health challenges in the region.

The developments across Montgomery County, such as urbanization, flood control, and infrastructure projects, significantly impacted soils across the region in five major ways:

Flood Control Measures: The establishment of the Miami Conservancy District and extensive flood prevention projects likely altered soil dynamics by reducing natural flooding, which affects sediment deposition and replenishment of fertile soils.

Urbanization: Increased construction of roadways, buildings, and suburban developments across the county led to soil sealing, compaction, and reduced permeability, disrupting natural drainage and soil health.

Industrial Growth and Decline: As the county experienced heavy industrial activity and later urban decline, soil contamination with pollutants like heavy metals, hydrocarbons, and industrial waste could have incurred.

Historic Preservation and Renewal: Efforts to stabilize and revitalize neighborhoods throughout the county might include soil remediation and landscaping, improving soil quality in targeted areas.

Zoning and Land Use Changes: Shifts from industrial to mixed-use areas or open spaces may affect soil conservation and land rehabilitation.

Overall, urban development and conservation efforts have reshaped soil health, erosion patterns, and land productivity throughout Montgomery County.

This concludes my Soil Survey discussion. Please feel free to expand this conversation with any questions. Thank you!

References

Bartenstein, F. (2004, April). TimeLine - The Planning of Dayton. City of Dayton; Department of Planning and Community Development.

Great Lakes Adaptation Assessment for Cities. (2013). The Potential Impacts of Climate Change on Dayton, Ohio. Ann Arbor.

Slater, B., &amp; Wilson, T. (n.d.). Miamian; Ohio State Soil. Madison; Soil Science Society of America.

Thoughts on Changes Over Time:
Over the past several years, the area has seen changes in land use, notably increased suburban development due to population growth, leading to soil compaction and decreased permeability. This has likely affected soil fertility and moisture retention, especially in areas with higher slopes. Additionally, the encroachment of invasive species, coupled with climate shifts, may alter the composition of native soils, increasing erosion and nutrient leaching in certain areas. As Troutdale continues to urbanize, sustainable soil management will be critical in maintaining soil health and ecosystem function.

Oldest Archived Survey

 

The oldest available archived pdf soil survey from this area is from 1931, with the file name craigOK1931. This survey was conducted by A.C. Anderson and A.W. Goke from the United States Department of Agriculture. Additional contributions were made from the Oklahoma Agricultural Experiment Station. Some key points from this survey included, a map, general farming soils, soil color, subsoil variations, soils for specific uses, and recommendations for soil management.

 Observations

 

The change in soil classification from the 1931 soil survey to the most recent study is one important finding. Soil names were different in the 1930’s and the soil did not classify kinds using the current symbols. The population shift over time is another interesting detail. Craig County’s population, which was 18,052 in the 1930’s, has since dropped to 14,494. By lowering land use stresses, this population decline may improve the soil health by providing a chance for soil recovery and increased sustainability.

 

 

-Kenzie

RACHAEL CARAWAY posted Nov 29, 2024 7:20 PM
Hello Class,

For this week’s discussion, I’m excited to focus on El Dorado County, California, a place that holds a special place in my heart as the county where I grew up. According to the 2020 Census, the population of El Dorado County was 191,185, spanning 1,707.8 square miles in the western Sierra Nevada region of California.

Figures 1 and 2: Geographic location of El Dorado County, California



A computer screen shot of a mapDescription automatically generated

Web Soil Survey Analysis

Using the United States Department of Agriculture (USDA) website, I obtained recent soil survey data for El Dorado County. The tabular data was collected on August 30, 2024, and the spatial data was collected on September 16, 2019. The survey revealed the following five most abundant soils in the county:

Comparison and Implications

Over the past 50 years, the most abundant soils in El Dorado County have shown minimal change. These soils are predominantly found on slopes ranging from 2% to 70%, with shallow depths to parent material and limited runoff. However, wildfires have played a role in slight shifts in soil composition. As noted by Neary, Ryan, and DeBano (2005), wildfires significantly impact soil by altering its physical, biological, and chemical properties, which can lead to erosion and degradation.

This comparison highlights the resilience of the county’s soil landscape despite environmental challenges, while also underscoring the importance of managing wildfire impacts to preserve soil health.

Ray

Hey Class,

Seeing how this is a class that focuses on soil composition and uses, I figured I would find a county that uses it soil for once of the most important resources, food. Clark County, Ohio is plum smack in the middle of Ohio. The county has a total area of 256,936.2 acres (USDA, 2019). As I said I was looking for a county that was committed to agriculture production. But just how committed is Clark County. Well, it is using 85% of its total acreage to support the agriculture business (OSU, 2024). That leave just 15% for home development, schools, infrastructure, and any other manmade structures.

Yearly rainfall could seep deeper in the ground and change its total composition.

Greater Fairbanks Area, Alaska Zane Steele Week 4&amp;5
Contains unread posts
Zane Steele posted Nov 28, 2024 7:07 PMLast edited: Monday, December 2, 2024 1:04 PM EST


Hello Class,

For my Area of Interest, I have chosen the Greater Fairbanks Area in Alaska. I selected this site because I am scheduled to PCS to the area next June. Since this will be my first time living in such a different region, I thought it would be both interesting and valuable to explore this area for my project.

Tabular Data Date: September 16, 2024
Spatial Data Date: October 22, 2019
Current Survey: Top 5 Most Abundant Soils
Symbol	Soil Name	Acres	Percentage
139	Jarvis-Salhaket complex	21,980.4	8.3%
181	Tanana mucky silt loam	12,975.5	4.9%
186	Urban land	12,364.1	4.7%
133	Goldstream peat, 0-3% slopes	11,273.6	4.3%
118	Fairbanks silt loam, 12-20% slopes	9,610.8	3.6%

Archived Survey: Top 5 Most Abundant Soils
Symbol	Soil Name	Acres	Percentage
-	Bradway very fine sandy loam	12,128	4.8%
-	Fairbanks silt loam, 7-12% slopes	10,660	4.2%
-	Tanana silt loam	16,525	6.5%
-	Minto silt loam, 3-7% slopes	16,420	6.4%
-	Salchaket very fine sandy loam, moderately deep	13,352	5.2%
Soils
My Area of interest includes a diverse range of soils, with the notable types described above. There are a mix of well-drained soils in upland areas and poorly drained, wetland soils in lower-lying regions. Common soil types include silt loams and peat, which support a variety of landforms such as hills, floodplains. The area experiences cold temperatures and short growing seasons, influencing soil properties like drainage, water retention, and fertility. Soils range from moderately fertile and suitable for limited agricultural use to wet, poorly drained soils found in wetter areas. 

Population 
Since 1960, Alaska has experienced significant population growth. Particularly, the growth in &quot;nonsubsistence areas,&quot; defined as regions where hunting and fishing are not the principal economic or cultural activities, has increased by 284.7%. This substantial population increase is likely to have influenced the land and soil in the area, as urbanization and infrastructure expansion often lead to soil disturbance, compaction, and changes in land use patterns.

Natural Disasters 
In 1967, Fairbanks experienced the heaviest rainfall in recorded history, causing the Chena River to flood. This event turned the river into a raging torrent, flooding areas such as Pioneer Park and downtown Fairbanks, causing extensive damage, killing six people, and resulting in nearly $85 million in damages. The prolonged flooding altered the local soil structure, with potential erosion, deposition, and compaction impacts that likely have had long-term effects on soil properties.

Pollution 
Fairbanks has faced significant air pollution challenges. According to the American Lung Association, it was ranked as the most polluted city in the U.S. for year-round particle pollution based on data from 2014-2016. This pollution, primarily from wood-burning stoves, has health risks but may also have indirect effects on the surrounding soils. Soot and particulate matter could accumulate on the soil surface, affecting plant life and soil microfauna, which in turn impacts soil fertility and structure.

Considering the combination of population growth, natural disasters, and pollution, I believe that the soils in the Greater Fairbanks Area have undergone significant changes over the past 60 years. Increased urbanization has likely altered soil composition and drainage properties, while flooding events would have contributed to erosion and deposition. Additionally, pollution could impact soil health through the deposition of harmful particles, further influencing plant and microbial activity in the region.

References

American Lung Association. (n.d.). Fairbanks ranks as Most-Polluted city in the nation. https://www.lung.org/media/press-releases/fairbanks-ranks-as

Fall, J. A. (2019). Alaska population trends and patterns, 1960–2018.

Floods of August 1967 in east-central Alaska | U.S. Geological Survey. (n.d.). https://www.usgs.gov/publications/floods-august-1967-east-central-alaska#:~:text=East%2Dcentral%20Alaska%20had%20record,annual%20precipitation%20for%20this%20area.
Web Soil Survey. (n.d.-b). https://websoilsurvey.nrcs.usda.gov/app/WebSoilSurvey.aspx

U.S. Census Bureau. (n.d.). Explore Census data. https://data.census.gov/profile/Fairbanks_city,_Alaska?g=160XX00US0224230
Zane

**ASSISTANT**:
Here’s a refined version of your response:

---

**Hi Rachael,**

I hope you had a fantastic Thanksgiving! I really enjoyed your post about El Dorado County. The resilience of the Auburn and Mariposa very rocky silt loams over the past 50 years is fascinating, especially given the wildfire impacts you mentioned. I’ve done some research on the role of fire suppression in the region and how it has contributed to more intense wildfires. It’s remarkable how these soil types persist despite such challenges, underscoring the importance of thoughtful ecosystem management. My research also highlighted how regular wildfires, prior to industrialization, played a vital role in maintaining healthy and productive forests, with Indigenous tribes conducting controlled burns as part of their stewardship practices.

My wife and I have explored the areas around Sacramento and Lake Tahoe, and it’s such a beautiful region. We even considered moving there since she’s from San Diego, but we’re now leaning toward the Pacific Northwest. That said, I’ve always been intrigued by the dominant soils of the Sierra Nevada, particularly those above 4,000 feet that support sequoia tree species. Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas
For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). Located in Central Texas, this county presents a fascinating dichotomy of soils shaped by its geography and human activity. A natural division runs through the county along Interstate Highway 35, with heavy limestone soils dominating the west and fertile black clay soils to the east. I chose this site because of its rich ecological and agricultural history, and I live nearby, making it personally relevant to explore.
AOI Map and Data

Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts
The division of Williamson County by Interstate 35 reveals distinct geological and land use patterns. The west side, with its limestone-heavy soils like Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD) and Eckrant stony clay (EeB), supports ranching and natural landscapes. In contrast, the eastern side, dominated by the fertile black clay soils like Houston Black (HoB) and Branyon clay (BrB), has been historically vital for agriculture, especially row crops like cotton and sorghum.
Urban growth along Interstate 35 has transformed Williamson County, particularly in cities like Round Rock, Georgetown, and Cedar Park. This expansion is now accelerating northwestward along Highway 183, stretching from Leander and Liberty Hill toward Lampasas. The transformation includes the extension of Highway 183 into a toll road up to Highway 29, with plans for further expansion. To illustrate the scale of this growth, Liberty Hill ISD is now the 12th fastest-growing school district in the United States, highlighting the rapid demographic and infrastructural changes in this region.
Reflection on Change Over Time
The 1983 survey shows many of the same soil types, but shifts in urban and agricultural use have led to significant changes in soil structure and health. In urbanized areas, impervious surfaces have disrupted water infiltration and increased erosion. The western limestone soils, once predominantly ranchland, now face pressures from construction and development. Similarly, the fertile clay soils in the east have experienced compaction and nutrient depletion due to long-term agricultural practices.
These changes highlight the need for sustainable soil management. In 1983, Williamson County, Texas, had an estimated population of approximately 92,624 residents. By 2024, this number has surged to an estimated 722,109, reflecting significant growth over the past four decades. As development continues to expand northwest of Austin, balancing urban growth with ecological stewardship becomes all the more important. Implementing strategies like soil regeneration, green spaces, and water management can help mitigate the impacts of rapid urbanization and ensure the long-term health of Williamson County&#x27;s diverse soils.

**Soil Survey and Land Use Changes in Williamson County, Texas**

For this week’s discussion, I selected Williamson County, Texas, as my area of interest (AOI). **Observations on Land Use and Soil Impacts**

The division of Williamson County by Interstate 35 reveals distinct geological and land use patterns. The west side, with its limestone-heavy soils like Eckrant cobbly clay (EaD) and Eckrant stony clay (EeB), supports ranching and natural landscapes. In contrast, the eastern side, dominated by fertile black clay soils like Houston Black (HoB) and Branyon clay (BrB), has been historically vital for agriculture, especially row crops like cotton and sorghum (Natural Resources Conservation Service, 1983; 2024).  

Urban growth along Interstate 35 has transformed Williamson County, particularly in cities like Round Rock, Georgetown, and Cedar Park. This expansion is now accelerating northwestward along Highway 183, stretching from Leander and Liberty Hill toward Lampasas. The transformation includes the extension of Highway 183 into a toll road up to Highway 29, with plans for further expansion. To illustrate the scale of this growth, Liberty Hill ISD is now the 12th fastest-growing school district in the United States, highlighting the rapid demographic and infrastructural changes in this region (U.S. Census Bureau, n.d.).

---

**Reflection on Change Over Time**

The 1983 survey shows many of the same soil types, but shifts in urban and agricultural use have led to significant changes in soil structure and health. In urbanized areas, impervious surfaces have disrupted water infiltration and increased erosion. The western limestone soils, once predominantly ranchland, now face pressures from construction and development. Similarly, the fertile clay soils in the east have experienced compaction and nutrient depletion due to long-term agricultural practices.  

These changes highlight the need for sustainable soil management. In 1983, Williamson County, Texas, had an estimated population of approximately 92,624 residents (U.S. Census Bureau, n.d.). By 2024, this number has surged to an estimated 722,109, reflecting significant growth over the past four decades (World Population Review, 2024). As development continues to expand northwest of Austin, balancing urban growth with ecological stewardship becomes all the more important. Implementing strategies like soil regeneration, green spaces, and water management can help mitigate the impacts of rapid urbanization and ensure the long-term health of Williamson County&#x27;s diverse soils.

Sulfur (S) - Little
Contains unread posts
Bradley Little posted Dec 9, 2024 1:04 AM
Class,

The sulfur cycle is the biogeochemical process that moves sulfur through the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and lithosphere. The attached photo illustrates how sulfur cycles through these stages.  Atmospheric sulfur can be deposited into the earth&#x27;s surface through precipitation or dry deposition. In this respect, sulfur can be absorbed into plants through the soil and subsequently absorbed into animals who eat the plants. When living organisms die, sulfur is returned to the soil via decomposition. This sulfur is generally broken down by microorganisms and released into the soil as sulfates. Sulfur is released back into the atmosphere through several different means. The decomposition of organic matter plays a significant role and can be influenced by humans through the burning of fossil fuels or even agricultural activities like tillage and fertilizer use. 

Soils play a role in this cycle through microbial activity and decomposition. Microorganisms transform sulfur and are involved in key processes like soil oxidation and reduction; this allows plants to absorb sulfur that infiltrates soil via precipitation. Furthermore, decomposing organic matter is stored in the soil prior to conversion, soil acting as a food bank for the microbial process. Soils act as a buffer in this process as well, regulating deposits from precipitation or decomposition by facilitating plant uptake and directly influencing leaching. Soil is a reservoir for nutrients, including sulfur, becuase soil receives, converts, and stores sulfur produced by organic matter and inorganic processes.

I&#x27;ll edit this post with a few resources shortly and add some information on sulfur conversion in water that results from direct wet deposition and runoff. Similar to dry deposition, a conversion process to sulfates/sulfides occurs followed by absorption into soils.

Sulfur Cycle

Brad

References:

Will update shortly

Potassium (K) Henson
Contains unread posts
Brodie Henson posted Dec 9, 2024 11:27 AMLast edited: Monday, December 9, 2024 12:10 PM EST
Hello everyone,

For this week&#x27;s discussion we have been tasked to conduct a further investigation into how the element potassium moves through the biogeochemical cycle. Below are a couple of standard Biogeochemical cycle diagrams that I will be utilizing for this discussion.

cycle

Mukherjee, S. (2023, February 2). Biogeochemical cycles: Definition, types, examples &amp; importance. Science Facts. https://www.sciencefacts.net/biogeochemical-cycles.html

Cycle

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

As you can see, I chose diagrams that did not contain very much water in the cycle. I chose this because potassium is not as abundant on earth or in the atmosphere as nitrogen and carbon. This is more of a sedimentary cycle. Let&#x27;s break down the diagrams. To begin with, potassium can be introduced directly into the soil via fertilizer or animal droppings. Plant residue can also place potassium directly into the soil. Once potassium is absorbed into the soil, decomposition begins via bacteria and fungi to break down the potassium for soil use. Process turns into a cycle when animals eat the plants and other organisms that have utilized the broken-down potassium. The cycle then goes back to the animal droppings. Some losses from the soil include run off for surface water on the earth&#x27;s surface. This may allow for a tad bit of potassium to be evaporated off of the earth&#x27;s surface and returned in the form of rain. This would be a very small amount, not nearly the percentage of carbon and/or nitrogen that would fall from the skies in the for of precipitation. Another loss of potassium for soil use could be caused from harvesting of crops. This is due to the churning of soil as well as nutrients being ripped out of the ground from farm equipment such as plows. These issues can be easily fixed by just adding more safe amounts of fertilizer as well as letting the soil rest for a set amount of time so nutrients can be organically replaced. 
I hope everyone has a great week and I look forward to hearing your responses!
-Brodie
References:
Admin. (2023, October 13). Biogeochemical cycles - a detailed overview. BYJUS. https://byjus.com/biology/biogeochemical-cycles/

Mukherjee, S. (2023, February 2). Biogeochemical cycles: Definition, types, examples &amp; importance. Science Facts. https://www.sciencefacts.net/biogeochemical-cycles.html

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

Phosphorous (P)-Craig Nalley
Contains unread posts
CRAIG NALLEY posted Dec 9, 2024 5:42 PM
Class, 

I chose to do my research on Phosphorous (P) and I discovered it is quite an important element to not only plants and soil but also is part of the building blocks for DNA and RNA. Looking at the cycle P it is mainly added to soil through organic and inorganic materials such as manure, fertilizers, and decomposing plants( Southwest Research and Outreach Center, n.d.). P is released in aerosol form from of volcanic eruptions which rain down ash and lava which carry P. It is then transported through the water which allows it to leach into the soil and be used by plants and animals. P is removed from the soil by the plants of the earth, and then as mentioned in the cycle above is replaced by those dead plants as they decompose which is one transport method of this nutrient (Khan academy, n.d.). It is also transported through the food chain as animals eat plants that contain P and as they themselves are consumed P can remain in organisms or be broken down into waste and deposited back into the environment.



Rock is where we will find the largest reservoirs of P and as the rocks weather and are transported by leaching of the element into the water and soils where they become available for use to plants and animals. Reservoirs of P are known to be at the ocean, it is transported from oceanic homes by a process called uplift and with anything geology it is very slow. The average phosphate residence time in oceanic sediment is 20,000-100,000 years (Khan Academy, n.d.). I found this great video that covers the P Cycle very well also. This video is a great and easy to understand about the whole process of the P cycle https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Wzo-uFS7LUA.

Human impacts on the P cycle has had some pretty bad consequences. We use it in our fertlilizer and detergents which is released back into the environment in overwhelming amounts. Like the video says these additions to the soil create an excess in the soil of P and then runoff occurs(Moomoo math, 2019). Which in turn can create harmful algae blooms which are also very harmful to the ecosystem(Moomoo math, 2019). 

Khan Academy. (n.d.). The Phosphorous cycle. Khan Academy. https://www.khanacademy.org/science/ap-college-environmental-science/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:the-living-world-ecosystems-and-biodiversity/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:biogeochemical-cycles/a/ap-es-the-phosphorus-cycle

MoomooMath and Science. (2019). The Phosphorous Cycle. YouTube. https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Wzo-uFS7LUA

Openstax, C. (2016). Phosphorous Cycle. Khan Academy. Retrieved December 9, 2024, from https://www.khanacademy.org/science/ap-college-environmental-science/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:the-living-world-ecosystems-and-biodiversity/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:biogeochemical-cycles/a/ap-es-the-phosphorus-cycle.

Calcium (Ca) and the Soil Cycle
Contains unread posts
RACHAEL CARAWAY posted Dec 9, 2024 8:33 PMLast edited: Monday, December 9, 2024 10:18 PM EST
 

A diagram of a structure of a rockDescription automatically generated with medium confidence

Calcium (Ca) and Its Role in the Soil Cycle

Calcium (Ca) is an essential element in soil ecosystems, playing a critical role in plant growth, soil structure, and the health of soil organisms. While often overshadowed by nutrients like nitrogen and phosphorus, calcium is vital for ecosystem stability and nutrient cycling.

Calcium moves through Earth&#x27;s systems in the calcium cycle. In the lithosphere, it’s stored in rocks like limestone and gypsum, which release calcium into the soil through weathering (Coleman et al., 2004). Plants absorb calcium from the soil and return it through decay (White &amp; Broadley, 2003). In the hydrosphere, calcium enters water systems, supporting marine organisms like mollusks (Gissel-Nielsen, 1987). In the atmosphere, while calcium itself doesn&#x27;t exist in the gaseous phase, calcium compounds like calcium carbonate buffer acid rain. This helps reduce soil acidification caused by pollutants, maintaining a more stable and neutral soil pH (Coleman et al., 2004).

A diagram of calcium cycleDescription automatically generated

In soil, calcium helps bind particles into aggregates, improving soil structure, aeration, and water infiltration, which supports plant root growth (Coleman et al., 2004). It also strengthens plant cell walls and aids in the transport of other nutrients (White &amp; Broadley, 2003). Calcium carbonate in the soil helps neutralize acidity, maintaining a healthy pH for microbial activity and nutrient availability (Gissel-Nielsen, 1987).

Calcium is also essential for various soil organisms. Microfauna, such as nematodes and protozoa, rely on calcium to maintain the structural stability of their cell membranes and support vital cellular processes (Tedersoo et al., 2014). These organisms feed on calcium-dependent bacteria and other microorganisms, which are critical to nutrient cycling and soil health. Mesofauna, like mites and springtails, use calcium to build strong exoskeletons, aiding in organic matter decomposition and nutrient cycling (Coleman et al., 2004). Macrofauna like earthworms and ants use calcium for their exoskeletons and skeletons, and earthworms help improve soil structure by passing calcium-rich material through their digestive systems (Tedersoo et al., 2014).

Calcium also supports the decomposition process. It neutralizes acids produced during the breakdown of organic material, speeding up decomposition (Tedersoo et al., 2014). Calcium-rich plant material decomposes faster, releasing nutrients like phosphorus into the soil, where they can be used by plants (White &amp; Broadley, 2003; Gissel-Nielsen, 1987).

Calcium is crucial for maintaining soil health and ecosystem stability. It supports soil fauna, aids decomposition, and ensures the availability of essential nutrients. Proper calcium management can help improve soil productivity and long-term ecosystem resilience.

Ray

 

Refrences:

Coleman, D. C., Callaham, M. A., &amp; Crossley, D. A. (2004). Fundamentals of soil ecology. Elsevier Academic Press.
Gissel-Nielsen, G. (1987). The role of calcium in the soil ecosystem. Journal of Soil Science, 38(1), 13-22.
Tedersoo, L., Bahram, M., Toots, M., et al. (2014). Global diversity and geography of soil fungi. Science, 346(6213), 1256688.
White, P. J., &amp; Broadley, M. R. (2003). Calcium in plants. Annals of Botany, 92(4), 487-511.

### Magnesium (Mg): The Unsung Hero in Soil and Plant Health

Hello Class,

This week, I’ve chosen magnesium (Mg) as the element to explore, a vital yet often overlooked nutrient that quietly powers life both above and below ground. Magnesium serves as the heart of chlorophyll, enabling plants to capture sunlight and produce the energy that fuels growth, and it does far more than that. Its role in soil health, plant physiology, and even human health reveals just how interconnected our ecosystems truly are.

#### The Magnesium Cycle: A Symphony of Movement
The magnesium cycle moves through rocks, soils, water, and living organisms, creating a dynamic system that sustains life. It begins with weathering—magnesium is released from rocks like dolomite and serpentine into soils, where plants take it up through their roots. From there, it moves up the food chain, supporting muscle function and cellular processes in animals and humans alike. Once plants or animals die, magnesium returns to the soil through decomposition, completing the cycle.

Interestingly, magnesium also interacts with water systems. In areas with high rainfall or sandy soils, it can leach away, creating deficiencies. This delicate balance makes soil management critical to maintaining magnesium levels for agriculture.

#### Why Magnesium Matters for Soil and Plants
Magnesium’s impact on plants is profound. It’s not just a piece of the puzzle; it’s the centerpiece of chlorophyll, driving photosynthesis and energy production. Without magnesium, plants lose their vibrant green color and struggle to thrive. Beyond chlorophyll, magnesium helps regulate the plant’s uptake of other nutrients, improving overall health and resistance to stress.

In soils, magnesium influences the cation exchange capacity (CEC), which determines how nutrients are retained and made available to plants. Soils low in magnesium often struggle with structure, becoming compacted and less aerated. Adding magnesium improves soil texture, water infiltration, and the availability of other vital nutrients like potassium and calcium.

#### Biostimulants and Magnesium: A Perfect Partnership
Here’s where things get exciting. Biostimulants, like the one analyzed in the lab test I provided, offer a natural way to enhance magnesium availability. The REGENiGROW solids analysis shows a magnesium content of 1.56%, and this can be a game-changer for magnesium-deficient soils. Biostimulants improve root architecture, helping plants access magnesium more efficiently while stimulating microbial activity that makes magnesium more bioavailable.

For example, beneficial microbes can help solubilize magnesium from insoluble minerals, making it easier for plants to absorb. This process not only boosts crop yield but also contributes to long-term soil health by reducing the need for chemical fertilizers.

#### A Shared Connection: Magnesium in Human and Plant Physiology
What’s truly fascinating is the parallel between how magnesium supports plant and human health. Just as magnesium stabilizes chlorophyll in plants, it stabilizes ATP (the energy currency of cells) in humans. It’s involved in over 300 enzymatic reactions in the human body, supporting muscle function, nerve signaling, and even heart health. In plants and humans alike, magnesium is the unsung hero, quietly keeping the machinery of life running smoothly.

#### Closing Thoughts
Magnesium might not be the first nutrient we think of when it comes to soil health, but its importance cannot be overstated. It ties together the health of our soils, plants, and even ourselves, creating a web of life that’s as beautiful as it is complex. Through tools like biostimulants and sustainable soil management, we have the power to amplify magnesium’s role and unlock its full potential.

I look forward to hearing your thoughts and exploring the other elements you’ve all chosen this week!

Best,  
George

---

### Magnesium (Mg): The Unsung Hero in Soil, Plant, and Human Health

Hello Class,

This week, I’ve chosen magnesium (Mg) as the element to explore—a vital yet underappreciated nutrient that powers life both above and below ground. Magnesium plays a central role in chlorophyll, enabling plants to capture sunlight and produce the energy they need to grow. It also influences soil health and plant vitality while offering fascinating parallels in its significance to human health.

#### The Magnesium Cycle: A Natural Balancing Act
Magnesium cycles through rocks, soils, water, and living organisms, creating a dynamic system of renewal. It begins with the weathering of magnesium-rich rocks like dolomite, serpentine, and basalt, releasing this essential mineral into soils. Plants absorb magnesium through their roots, and it then moves up the food chain to animals and humans. Once plants and animals die, magnesium returns to the soil through decomposition.

Magnesium is also present in water systems, but excessive rainfall or sandy soils can lead to leaching, depleting its availability. This makes soil and water management critical to maintaining adequate magnesium levels for agriculture and ecosystems.

#### Why Magnesium Matters for Plants and Soils
Magnesium is the core element in chlorophyll molecules, which makes it indispensable for photosynthesis and energy production in plants (Epstein &amp; Bloom, 2005). Without sufficient magnesium, plants exhibit chlorosis, a yellowing of leaves, and struggle with stunted growth. Beyond its role in photosynthesis, magnesium supports the activation of enzymes that drive nutrient uptake and carbohydrate metabolism, ensuring plants grow robustly and resist environmental stress.

In soils, magnesium influences the cation exchange capacity (CEC), which determines how nutrients are retained and made available to plants (Brady &amp; Weil, 2016). Magnesium also contributes to soil structure, promoting better water retention and aeration. In soils with low magnesium levels, adding magnesium through natural amendments like Epsom salt (magnesium sulfate) can significantly enhance soil health and plant growth.

#### Epsom Salt: Enhancing Plant and Human Health
Epsom salt has long been valued for its ability to enrich soils and promote plant vitality. When applied to magnesium-deficient soils, it delivers a soluble form of magnesium that plants can readily absorb. This can help restore the green vibrancy of leaves and improve overall crop yields (Salisbury &amp; Ross, 1992). 

Interestingly, Epsom salt offers benefits beyond agriculture. It is widely used in human health for relieving sore muscles, supporting nerve function, and promoting relaxation. These benefits stem from magnesium’s role in stabilizing cellular processes, highlighting its essential function across biological systems (Rude et al., 2006).

#### A Shared Connection: Magnesium in Plant and Human Physiology
Magnesium&#x27;s importance transcends ecosystems, linking plant and human health in profound ways. Just as magnesium stabilizes chlorophyll in plants, it stabilizes ATP (the energy currency of cells) in humans, supporting over 300 enzymatic reactions (Volpe, 2013). These include energy production, muscle function, and nerve signaling. In both cases, magnesium acts as a quiet powerhouse, enabling life to flourish.

#### Closing Thoughts
Magnesium may not receive as much attention as other nutrients, but its role in soil, plant, and human health is indispensable. By understanding its cycle and significance, we can better appreciate the interconnectedness of life and the delicate balance that sustains it. Whether through sustainable soil management or a simple Epsom salt solution, magnesium offers a powerful way to enhance both our ecosystems and our well-being.

I look forward to hearing your thoughts and learning more about the elements you’ve chosen!

Best regards,  
George

### Magnesium (Mg): The Unsung Hero in Soil, Plant, and Human Health

Hello Class,

This week, I’ve chosen magnesium (Mg) as the element to explore—a vital yet underappreciated nutrient that powers life both above and below ground. Magnesium plays a central role in chlorophyll, enabling plants to capture sunlight and produce the energy they need to grow. It also influences soil health and plant vitality while offering fascinating parallels in its significance to human health.

#### The Magnesium Cycle: A Natural Balancing Act
Magnesium moves through rocks, soils, water, and living organisms in a continuous cycle. It begins with the weathering of magnesium-rich rocks like dolomite and serpentine, releasing this essential mineral into soils. Plants absorb magnesium through their roots, and it then moves up the food chain. Once plants and animals die, magnesium returns to the soil through decomposition.

Excessive rainfall or sandy soils can lead to leaching, making magnesium management essential for maintaining healthy ecosystems. Soils act as a reservoir, buffering the availability of this nutrient for plants and microorganisms (Alloway, 2008).

#### Why Magnesium Matters for Plants and Soils
Magnesium is the core element in chlorophyll, making it indispensable for photosynthesis and energy production in plants. Without sufficient magnesium, plants exhibit chlorosis, a yellowing of leaves, and struggle with stunted growth. Beyond its role in photosynthesis, magnesium activates enzymes involved in nutrient uptake and carbohydrate metabolism.

In soils, magnesium improves cation exchange capacity (CEC), enhancing nutrient retention and availability. It also plays a role in soil structure, improving water infiltration and aeration. Applications of magnesium-rich amendments, such as Epsom salt (magnesium sulfate), are often used to correct deficiencies and restore soil health.

#### Epsom Salt: A Bridge Between Human and Plant Health
Epsom salt has long been valued for its dual role in human and plant well-being. When applied to magnesium-deficient soils, it delivers a soluble form of magnesium that plants can readily absorb. This simple treatment can reinvigorate chlorophyll production and improve crop yields (White &amp; Broadley, 2003).

Interestingly, Epsom salt offers benefits for humans as well. It is widely used for relieving muscle tension and supporting nerve function. These benefits stem from magnesium’s critical role in stabilizing cellular processes, which parallels its importance in plant physiology.

#### Closing Thoughts
Magnesium may not receive as much attention as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact on ecosystems, agriculture, and even human health is profound. By understanding its role and cycling, we can better manage soils and appreciate the interconnectedness of life. Whether through enhancing soils or enjoying the benefits of a relaxing Epsom salt bath, magnesium offers a powerful reminder of nature’s subtle brilliance.

### Magnesium (Mg): The Quiet Power Behind Growth and Health

Hi Class,

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

#### A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils
Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.

Soils are both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, in sandy or highly leached soils, magnesium can become scarce—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Alloway, 2008).

#### Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium
Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.

For soils, magnesium works wonders by enhancing cation exchange capacity (CEC), which boosts their ability to hold and release nutrients. It also improves soil structure, making it easier for water to seep in and roots to breathe.

#### From Plants to People: The Epsom Salt Connection
Magnesium has a beautiful duality—it nourishes plants, and it helps us too. In agriculture, Epsom salt (magnesium sulfate) is a game-changer. It provides a quick, soluble source of magnesium to revive struggling plants and restore chlorophyll production.

But its benefits don’t stop there. Epsom salt baths are famous for soothing sore muscles and relaxing the mind, all thanks to magnesium’s knack for stabilizing cellular processes. It’s a gentle reminder of how deeply connected we are to the natural world.

#### Why Magnesium Matters
Magnesium might not be as flashy as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact is undeniable. It fuels plants, supports soil health, and even helps us recharge. By understanding its cycle and role, we can manage soils more thoughtfully and cultivate a healthier relationship with the ecosystems that sustain us.

### Magnesium in Natural Springs: A Case Study of Saratoga Springs, NY

Saratoga Springs, NY, offers a remarkable example of the natural abundance of magnesium in mineral springs. Known for its naturally carbonated waters, this destination has been celebrated for centuries for its therapeutic properties. Springs like Hathorn Spring No. 3 boast high concentrations of minerals, including magnesium, which contributes to their unique, slightly salty taste and perceived health benefits.

Magnesium-rich waters from Saratoga Springs are not only believed to aid digestion but also play a role in relaxation and muscle recovery. Historically, people visited these springs for both drinking and bathing, harnessing magnesium&#x27;s restorative effects for human health. These springs also serve as a vivid illustration of magnesium&#x27;s role in Earth&#x27;s biogeochemical cycles—dissolving from underground mineral deposits and emerging through geysers and artesian wells.

---

### Magnesium (Mg) and Its Role in the Soil Cycle

Magnesium (Mg) plays a vital role in plant health, human physiology, and the broader ecological balance of our planet. Found abundantly in the Earth&#x27;s crust, magnesium cycles through soil, water, and living organisms, fostering growth and vitality at every stage.

#### The Magnesium Cycle
Magnesium moves through the environment via the weathering of rocks, which releases magnesium ions into the soil. Plants absorb these ions through their roots, incorporating magnesium into chlorophyll, the green pigment essential for photosynthesis. From there, magnesium moves up the food chain as herbivores consume plants, and it eventually returns to the soil through decomposition. Soils rich in organic matter or replenished with mineral amendments maintain this vital cycle, ensuring plant and ecosystem health. The influence of magnesium extends to stabilizing soil structure by binding particles, improving water infiltration, and reducing erosion (Alloway, 2013).

#### Epsom Salt: A Practical Source of Magnesium
Epsom salt, or magnesium sulfate, is a widely recognized means of addressing magnesium deficiencies. In agriculture, its application to soils and foliage can enhance plant nutrient uptake, particularly in magnesium-hungry crops like tomatoes, peppers, and roses. This quick and soluble source of magnesium is especially valuable in sandy soils, where magnesium is prone to leaching. However, its use should be guided by soil testing, as overuse may lead to nutrient imbalances or salinization (Real Simple, 2024).

For humans, Epsom salt baths are celebrated for their potential to ease muscle soreness, reduce stress, and promote relaxation. Though scientific evidence on transdermal absorption is limited, many individuals report subjective benefits, integrating Epsom salt baths into wellness routines for physical and mental relief.

#### Natural Springs: A Testament to Magnesium&#x27;s Power
The famous springs of Saratoga Springs, New York, are a testament to magnesium&#x27;s importance. These natural springs, rich in magnesium and other minerals, have long been celebrated for their therapeutic properties. Visitors often remark on the refreshing and revitalizing qualities of these waters, highlighting magnesium&#x27;s central role in health and wellness. The mineral deposits left behind by these geysers also serve as a tangible reminder of magnesium&#x27;s ongoing cycle from the lithosphere to ecosystems and human use.

#### Conclusion
Magnesium is a cornerstone element for life, bridging the needs of plants, soils, and humans. Whether through the application of Epsom salt in agriculture, the relaxing soak of an Epsom salt bath, or the therapeutic waters of natural springs, magnesium demonstrates its versatility and importance across scales. By appreciating and managing magnesium in our soils and lives, we can foster healthier ecosystems and communities.

---

### References

**ASSISTANT**:
You&#x27;re absolutely right—let&#x27;s revert to your preferred style and structure while integrating the new information. Here&#x27;s the revised version that reflects your unique voice:

---

### Magnesium (Mg) and Its Role in the Soil Cycle  

Magnesium (Mg) is the unsung hero of both plant and human health. Essential for photosynthesis in plants and a variety of physiological processes in humans, this element is fundamental to life. Its journey through the Earth&#x27;s soil and ecosystems is a fascinating cycle that deserves more attention.  

#### How Magnesium Moves Through the Environment  
Magnesium begins its cycle with the weathering of rocks, which releases magnesium ions into the soil. These ions are absorbed by plants and incorporated into chlorophyll, powering photosynthesis and supporting plant growth. As plants decompose or are consumed by herbivores, magnesium returns to the soil, completing the cycle. This process is critical for soil structure too, as magnesium helps bind soil particles, enhancing water infiltration and reducing erosion (Alloway, 2013).  

#### Benefits of Epsom Salt for Soil and Health  
Magnesium sulfate, commonly known as Epsom salt, is an accessible and effective way to replenish magnesium in soils. It&#x27;s especially useful for crops like tomatoes and peppers, which demand higher levels of magnesium. In addition to boosting plant health, Epsom salt improves soil quality, particularly in sandy or magnesium-deficient soils. For gardeners, it&#x27;s a simple solution to address visible signs of deficiency, like yellowing leaves, while enhancing the overall vigor of plants (Real Simple, 2024).  

On the human side, Epsom salt baths have long been cherished for their soothing properties. While scientific evidence for magnesium absorption through the skin is limited, people swear by its ability to ease muscle soreness and promote relaxation. It&#x27;s a small but meaningful way magnesium contributes to well-being.  

#### Saratoga Springs: Nature&#x27;s Magnesium Showcase  
Saratoga Springs, New York, offers a vivid reminder of magnesium&#x27;s power. The mineral-rich waters of these natural springs are high in magnesium and have been celebrated for their health benefits for generations. The springs leave visible mineral deposits, showcasing magnesium’s role in the Earth&#x27;s cycle. Visiting these geysers connects you to magnesium’s broader story—one of rejuvenation and resilience.  

#### Why Magnesium Matters  
Magnesium is more than just an element in the soil; it’s a bridge between ecosystems and human health. Whether in the fields through Epsom salt applications or in the healing waters of natural springs, magnesium reminds us of the interconnectedness of life. Managing magnesium wisely in our soils and lives ensures we cultivate stronger plants, healthier ecosystems, and a better quality of life.  

---

Here’s the final, cohesive version with your visit and references seamlessly integrated. All URLs are provided for easy access.

---

### Magnesium (Mg): A Silent Powerhouse in Soil and Health

Magnesium often flies under the radar compared to other macronutrients, yet it’s essential for life—both in plants and humans. This vital mineral plays a central role in chlorophyll production, enabling photosynthesis in plants, and supports numerous physiological processes in humans, from muscle recovery to bone health.

---

#### The Magnesium Cycle: From Soil to Life  
Magnesium’s journey begins in the Earth’s crust, stored in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over millennia, weathering releases magnesium into the soil, making it accessible to plants. As plants absorb magnesium, it forms the backbone of chlorophyll, driving the photosynthetic engine that sustains life. When plants decay or animals excrete waste, magnesium reenters the soil, continuing the cycle.  

Soils with balanced magnesium levels foster better crop yields and healthier ecosystems. Conversely, magnesium deficiencies in soil often result in weak, yellowing plants—a condition known as chlorosis. Addressing these imbalances is vital, particularly in intensively farmed or weathered soils (Epstein &amp; Bloom, 2005).  

---

#### Epsom Salt: A Garden and Health Ally  
A simple way to replenish magnesium in soils is through Epsom salt, which contains magnesium sulfate. This inexpensive addition can restore soil health, improve plant resilience, and enhance flowering in crops like tomatoes and peppers. For humans, Epsom salt baths offer relaxation and muscle recovery, drawing on magnesium&#x27;s ability to reduce inflammation and support cellular repair (Rosen et al., 2023).  

---

#### Saratoga Springs: Nature&#x27;s Magnesium Showcase  
Saratoga Springs, New York, offers a vivid reminder of magnesium&#x27;s power. The mineral-rich waters of these natural springs are high in magnesium and have been celebrated for their health benefits for generations. The springs leave visible mineral deposits, showcasing magnesium’s role in the Earth&#x27;s cycle. Visiting these geysers connects you to magnesium’s broader story—one of rejuvenation and resilience.  

In 2012, I visited Saratoga Springs directly after completing the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling running obstacle race spanning marathon distance up, down, and all around the ski slopes. After such an intense physical challenge, rejuvenation was definitely high on my list, and the springs delivered on that promise.

---

### A Silent Hero in Sustainability  
Magnesium is often overshadowed by nutrients like nitrogen and phosphorus, but it’s indispensable in maintaining soil fertility and promoting ecological balance. Its dual role in plant vitality and human health underscores its importance. From bolstering crops to soothing sore muscles, magnesium is a bridge between nature’s cycles and our well-being.

---

### References  
- Epstein, E., &amp; Bloom, A. J. (2005). *Mineral Nutrition of Plants: Principles and Perspectives* (2nd ed.). Sinauer Associates.  
[https://www.sinauer.com/mineral-nutrition-of-plants-principles-and-perspectives.html](https://www.sinauer.com/mineral-nutrition-of-plants-principles-and-perspectives.html)  

- Rosen, C. J., Bierman, P. M., &amp; Eliason, R. (2023). Fertilizing Fruits and Vegetables. *University of Minnesota Extension*.  
[https://extension.umn.edu/fertilizer-guidance/fertilizing-fruits-and-vegetables](https://extension.umn.edu/fertilizer-guidance/fertilizing-fruits-and-vegetables)  

- Saratoga Springs Visitor Information. (n.d.). Saratoga Springs Natural Mineral Waters. Saratoga.com.  
[https://www.saratoga.com/mineral-waters/](https://www.saratoga.com/mineral-waters/)

now fuse that version with the verbiage in this one and we&#x27;ll have a winner I believe. no need to add the divider lines because I delete them.

Hi Class,

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils

Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.

Soils are both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, magnesium can become scarce in sandy or highly leached soils—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Alloway, 2008).

Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium

Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.

For soils, magnesium works wonders by enhancing cation exchange capacity (CEC), which boosts their ability to hold and release nutrients. It also improves soil structure, making it easier for water to seep in and roots to breathe.

From Plants to People: The Epsom Salt Connection

Magnesium has a beautiful duality—it nourishes plants, and it helps us too. In agriculture, Epsom salt (magnesium sulfate) is a game-changer. It provides a quick, soluble source of magnesium to revive struggling plants and restore chlorophyll production.

But its benefits don’t stop there. Epsom salt baths are famous for soothing sore muscles and relaxing the mind, all thanks to magnesium’s knack for stabilizing cellular processes. It’s a gentle reminder of how deeply connected we are to the natural world.

Magnesium in Natural Springs: Saratoga Springs, NY

Saratoga Springs, NY, offers a glimpse into magnesium’s natural abundance. Known for its mineral-rich waters, springs like Hathorn Spring No. 3 contain high concentrations of magnesium, giving the water its distinctive taste and therapeutic qualities. Visitors have long enjoyed these springs for relaxation, digestion, and muscle recovery, showcasing magnesium’s importance for human health. Seeing the geysers at Saratoga Springs shoot mineral-rich water into the air is a vivid reminder of magnesium’s journey through the Earth, linking geology, ecology, and well-being.


[SOURCE] A photo that I took of a geyser at Saratoga Springs, NY during a trip to Mt. Killington, VT.&amp;nbsp;

Why Magnesium Matters

Magnesium might not be as flashy as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact is undeniable. It fuels plants, supports soil health, and even helps us recharge. By understanding its cycle and role, we can manage soils more thoughtfully and cultivate a healthier relationship with the ecosystems that sustain us.

Looking forward to hearing your thoughts—seeing which elements you’ve chosen to explore and how they all work together!

Best,
George

Hi Class,

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

**A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils**  
Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.  

Soils act as both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, magnesium can become scarce in sandy or highly leached soils—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Epstein &amp; Bloom, 2005).  

**Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium**  
Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.  

For soils, magnesium enhances cation exchange capacity (CEC), boosting their ability to hold and release nutrients. It also improves soil structure, making it easier for water to seep in and roots to breathe.  

**From Plants to People: The Epsom Salt Connection**  
Magnesium has a beautiful duality—it nourishes plants, and it helps us too. In agriculture, Epsom salt is a game-changer. It provides a quick, soluble source of magnesium to revive struggling plants and restore chlorophyll production.  

But its benefits don’t stop there. Epsom salt baths are famous for soothing sore muscles and relaxing the mind, all thanks to magnesium’s knack for stabilizing cellular processes. After all, who doesn’t need a little magnesium-fueled recharge now and then?  

**Magnesium in Natural Springs: Saratoga Springs, NY**  
Saratoga Springs, NY, offers a glimpse into magnesium’s natural abundance. Known for its mineral-rich waters, springs like Hathorn Spring No. 3 contain high concentrations of magnesium, giving the water its distinctive taste and therapeutic qualities.  

I visited Saratoga Springs in 2012, directly after completing the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling running obstacle race spanning marathon distance up, down, and around the ski slopes. After such an intense physical challenge, rejuvenation was high on my list, and the springs delivered. Seeing the geysers shoot magnesium-rich waters into the air was a vivid reminder of this element’s journey through the Earth, linking geology, ecology, and well-being.  

**Why Magnesium Matters**  
Magnesium might not be as flashy as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact is undeniable. It fuels plants, supports soil health, and even helps us recharge. By understanding its cycle and role, we can manage soils more thoughtfully and cultivate a healthier relationship with the ecosystems that sustain us.  

Looking forward to hearing your thoughts—seeing which elements you’ve chosen to explore and how they all work together!  

Best,  
George

Magnesium is more than just an element in the soil; it’s a bridge between ecosystems and human health. Whether in the fields through Epsom salt applications or in the healing waters of natural springs, magnesium reminds us of the interconnectedness of life. Managing magnesium wisely in our soils and lives ensures we cultivate stronger plants, healthier ecosystems, and a better quality of life.

---

**Hi Class,**

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

---

**A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils**  
Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.

Soils act as both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, magnesium can become scarce in sandy or highly leached soils—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Rosen et al., 2023).

---

**Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium**  
Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.

For soils, magnesium enhances cation exchange capacity (CEC), boosting their ability to hold and release nutrients. It also improves soil structure, making it easier for water to seep in and roots to breathe.

---

**From Plants to People: The Epsom Salt Connection**  
Magnesium has a beautiful duality—it nourishes plants, and it helps us too. In agriculture, Epsom salt is a game-changer. It provides a quick, soluble source of magnesium to revive struggling plants and restore chlorophyll production.

But its benefits don’t stop there. Epsom salt baths are famous for soothing sore muscles and relaxing the mind, all thanks to magnesium’s knack for stabilizing cellular processes. After all, who doesn’t need a little magnesium-fueled recharge now and then?

---

**Magnesium in Natural Springs: Saratoga Springs, NY**  
Saratoga Springs, NY, offers a glimpse into magnesium’s natural abundance. Known for its mineral-rich waters, springs like Hathorn Spring No. 3 contain high concentrations of magnesium, giving the water its distinctive taste and therapeutic qualities.

I visited Saratoga Springs in 2012, directly after completing the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling running obstacle race spanning marathon distance up, down, and around the ski slopes. After such an intense physical challenge, rejuvenation was high on my list, and the springs delivered. Seeing the geysers shoot magnesium-rich waters into the air was a vivid reminder of this element’s journey through the Earth, linking geology, ecology, and well-being.

---

**Why Magnesium Matters**  
Magnesium might not be as flashy as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact is undeniable. It fuels plants, supports soil health, and even helps us recharge. By understanding its cycle and role, we can manage soils more thoughtfully and cultivate a healthier relationship with the ecosystems that sustain us.

Looking forward to hearing your thoughts—seeing which elements you’ve chosen to explore and how they all work together!  

**Best,**  
George  

---

**References**

- Rosen, C. J., Bierman, P. M., &amp; Eliason, R. (2023). Fertilizing Fruits and Vegetables. University of Minnesota Extension. Retrieved from [https://extension.umn.edu/fertilizer-guidance/fertilizing-fruits-and-vegetables](https://extension.umn.edu/fertilizer-guidance/fertilizing-fruits-and-vegetables)  

- Saratoga Springs Visitor Information. (n.d.). Saratoga Springs Natural Mineral Waters. Saratoga.com. Retrieved from [https://www.saratoga.com/mineral-waters/](https://www.saratoga.com/mineral-waters/)

**ASSISTANT**:
Hi Class,

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

**A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils**  
Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.

Soils act as both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, magnesium can become scarce in sandy or highly leached soils—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Real Simple, 2024).

**Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium**  
Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.

Pick any single element of interest (e.g., phosphorus, potassium, calcium, sulfur, etc.) EXCEPT for Carbon or Nitrogen and do additional research to flesh out what you have learned from the required readings from Week 5.  Embed a graphic that shows the typical global biogeochemical cycle and describe how the element moves through the various compartments of the Earth, be sure you highlight the role of soils.  Do not cover the same element as another student and be sure to title your post with the element name and symbol (e.g., Potassium (K)) so folks know what has already been posted on.

Zinc (Zn), Amanda Kerrick
Contains unread posts
Amanda Kerrick posted Dec 10, 2024 8:54 PMLast edited: Tuesday, December 10, 2024 9:36 PM EST
Zinc is a micronutrient for soil. A smaller amount of micronutrients are needed for soil than macronutrient. &quot;Micronutrients are generally concentrated in the vascular solution and cytoplasm of plants, so they are present in organic residues and released during the initial physical fragmentation of decomposing organic residues.&quot; (Whalen, J. 2021)  Zinc is primarily stored in the Earth&#x27;s crust, where it is found in various minerals. Through weathering and erosion, zinc is released from these minerals into the soil and water. Soils are a critical compartment in the zinc cycle, as they act as both a source and a sink for zinc. Zinc can be bound to soil particles, especially clay minerals and organic matter. Zinc can also be present in a soluble and bioavailable form, adsorbed to soil particles, which plants can take up. When plants are consumed by herbivores, or when decomposed in the soil, zinc is passed along the food chain. In the biosphere (or living organisms) zinc is absorbed by animals, where it plays similar metabolic roles. Zinc is also released back into the environment when organisms die and decompose. Zinc can be transported through the hydrosphere in water bodies, such as rivers, lakes, and oceans, via surface runoff, atmospheric deposition, and groundwater. Human activities significantly impact the global zinc cycle. Mining and smelting of zinc ores release large amounts of zinc into the environment. Agricultural practices, such as the use of fertilizers and animal manures, can alter the distribution of zinc in soils, increasing the amount and potentially harming the soil and the microbes in it. Zinc is a vital element for plant growth. Deficiencies in zinc can lead to stunted growth and impaired plant functions, while excess zinc in soils can be toxic to plants and microorganisms. 

Image

References: 

Natural. ZINC. International Zinc Association. (n.d.). https://www.zinc.org/natural/

Whalen, J., Ziadi, N., Schoenau, J., Paré, M. C., &amp; Burton, D. (2021, November 25). 1.7: Soil Nutrient cycling. Geosciences LibreTexts. https://geo.libretexts.org/Bookshelves/Soil_Science/Digging_into_Canadian_Soils%3A_An_Introduction_to_Soil_Science/01%3A_Digging_In/1.07%3A_Soil_Nutrient_Cycling

Wk 6 - Argon
Contains unread posts
Angel Delgado-leach posted Dec 11, 2024 10:34 PM
Good evening Class,

Sorry about this post, if something doesn&#x27;t sound right or there are a bunch of questions, please feel free to ask. I am recovering from surgery and I&#x27;ve noticed my typing errors are extremely high. This week I decided to choose my favorite-sounding element, Argon. It has always sounded like a medieval name for some well-known rogue warrior or something. Hope it gave you a little laugh. Here are the specifics about Argon and its Biogeochemical Cycle:

Global Biogeochemical Cycle of Argon
1. The Atmosphere:
Argon is the third most abundant gas in Earth&#x27;s atmosphere, making up about 0.93%, following nitrogen and oxygen. As a chemically inert noble gas, it does not readily form compounds with other elements, allowing it to cycle through the atmosphere in its gaseous form without undergoing significant changes. The primary source of Argon in the atmosphere is volcanic activity, which releases Argon and other gases trapped in the Earth’s mantle.
Once in the atmosphere, Argon is dispersed by wind currents and remains in the atmosphere for long periods. Its inert nature prevents it from reacting with other gases, meaning it does not have an exit pathway like other gases such as oxygen or carbon dioxide. Instead, Argon simply diffuses through the atmosphere, circulating globally without forming compounds.
2. The Lithosphere and Soils:
Argon is primarily found in the Earth’s crust and mantle, where it is trapped in minerals and rocks. Volcanic activity releases Argon into the atmosphere, but it can also be absorbed into the soil. Due to its inert nature, Argon is chemically stable and does not participate in soil-based biogeochemical processes. Instead, it may accumulate in small amounts in soil pore spaces, particularly in regions affected by volcanic eruptions.
Although Argon does not interact significantly with soil components, it can diffuse through the soil, especially near the surface where concentrations are higher due to volcanic emissions. However, its movement through the soil is limited compared to other elements involved in active biogeochemical cycles, as its gaseous state and chemical inertness make it relatively immobile within the soil.
3. The Hydrosphere:
Argon is present in trace amounts in bodies of water, entering the hydrosphere primarily through diffusion from the atmosphere. However, its solubility in water is relatively low compared to gases like oxygen or carbon dioxide, meaning that the concentration of Argon in water remains minimal. Once dissolved, Argon can be carried by ocean currents or move through bodies of water, but it does not play a significant role in aquatic processes like oxygen or carbon dioxide.
4. The Biosphere:
Argon plays a negligible role in biological processes, as it does not participate in essential functions like photosynthesis or respiration. While it is present in trace amounts in the air that organisms breathe, it does not chemically interact with biological systems. As a result, Argon does not cycle through the biosphere in the same way as elements like carbon, nitrogen, or phosphorus and has no direct impact on the biological functions of plants, animals, or microorganisms.
Summary of Argon’s Biogeochemical Cycle
Volcanic emissions release Argon from the Earth’s mantle into the atmosphere, where it remains as an inert gas.
Once in the atmosphere, Argon is dispersed by wind currents and remains chemically unchanged.
In the lithosphere and soils, Argon does not participate in biogeochemical reactions but may be found in trace amounts.
In the hydrosphere, Argon is weakly soluble in water and exists in minimal amounts.
In the biosphere, Argon is present in the air, but it does not play an active role in biological processes.
Argon, being a stable and non-reactive element, moves primarily through the atmosphere, with limited interactions in other Earth compartments. It is an example of an element that, while abundant, does not participate in the active biogeochemical cycles that drive life on Earth.

Breakdown

Bio response

- Angel
