Sulfur (S) - Little
Contains unread posts
Bradley Little posted Dec 9, 2024 1:04 AMLast edited: Wednesday, December 11, 2024 3:49 PM EST
Class,

The sulfur cycle is the biogeochemical process that moves sulfur through the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and lithosphere. The attached photo illustrates how sulfur cycles through these stages.  Atmospheric sulfur can be deposited into the earth&#x27;s surface through precipitation or dry deposition. In this respect, sulfur can be absorbed into plants through the soil and subsequently absorbed into animals who eat the plants. When living organisms die, sulfur is returned to the soil via decomposition. This sulfur is generally broken down by microorganisms and released into the soil as sulfates. Sulfur is released back into the atmosphere through several different means. The decomposition of organic matter plays a significant role and can be influenced by humans through the burning of fossil fuels or even agricultural activities like tillage and fertilizer use. 

Soils play a role in this cycle through microbial activity and decomposition. Microorganisms transform sulfur and are involved in key processes like soil oxidation and reduction; this allows plants to absorb sulfur that infiltrates soil via precipitation. Furthermore, decomposing organic matter is stored in the soil prior to conversion, soil acting as a food bank for the microbial process. Soils act as a buffer in this process as well, regulating deposits from precipitation or decomposition by facilitating plant uptake and directly influencing leaching. Soil is a reservoir for nutrients, including sulfur, because soil receives, converts, and stores sulfur produced by organic matter and inorganic processes.

There are several reservoirs in the sulfur cycle. It is understood that sulfur exists in some form in the atmosphere, biosphere, and hydrosphere. In the atmosphere, sulfur exists as sulfur dioxide and aerosols. Sulfur in this reservoir generally originates from natural processes and the burning of fossil fuels. It has a residence time of several days, primarily depending on precipitation.

Sulfur residence times vary from day to day in the biosphere. Soils contain organic and inorganic sulfur, and microbes transform and cycle within the reservoir. A particularly interesting aspect of the biosphere is the storage of sulfur in rocks. Weathering and erosion release sulfur, either back into the biosphere or into the atmosphere via a chemical reaction, acid rain. The residence time in sedimentary rocks can be millions of years.

Oceans are significant reservoirs containing sulfate ions, with marine life playing a role in cycling. However, sulfur also exists in freshwater from leaching or falling precipitation containing dissolved sulfates. Sulfur can remain in the hydrosphere for long periods. Sulfur primarily exists as stable and soluble ions and does not undergo any significant chemical adaptations. A no-brainer is dilution. Oceans are vast, so sulfur compounds exist in the water column for extended periods until they are removed through biological uptake or sedimentation.

Earlier in the post, I discussed the natural and anthropogenic cycles. These are the naturally occurring or human-caused ways that sulfur moves through the different reservoirs. Anthropogenically speaking, coal releases large amounts of sulfur, as does industry, agriculture, and urban development.

Sulfur Cycle

The picture above shows how sulfur is released into the atmosphere both naturally and by human activity. Sulfur can be deposited back into the biosphere by either dry deposition (accumulation of material) or wet deposition (precipitation). Through these forms of deposition, sulfur enters soils and water to be transferred by microorganisms or chemical reactions.

Brad

Potassium (K) Henson
Contains unread posts
Brodie Henson posted Dec 9, 2024 11:27 AMLast edited: Monday, December 9, 2024 12:10 PM EST
Hello everyone,

For this week&#x27;s discussion we have been tasked to conduct a further investigation into how the element potassium moves through the biogeochemical cycle. Below are a couple of standard Biogeochemical cycle diagrams that I will be utilizing for this discussion.

cycle

Mukherjee, S. (2023, February 2). Biogeochemical cycles: Definition, types, examples &amp; importance. Science Facts. https://www.sciencefacts.net/biogeochemical-cycles.html

Cycle

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

As you can see, I chose diagrams that did not contain very much water in the cycle. I chose this because potassium is not as abundant on earth or in the atmosphere as nitrogen and carbon. This is more of a sedimentary cycle. Let&#x27;s break down the diagrams. To begin with, potassium can be introduced directly into the soil via fertilizer or animal droppings. Plant residue can also place potassium directly into the soil. Once potassium is absorbed into the soil, decomposition begins via bacteria and fungi to break down the potassium for soil use. Process turns into a cycle when animals eat the plants and other organisms that have utilized the broken-down potassium. The cycle then goes back to the animal droppings. Some losses from the soil include run off for surface water on the earth&#x27;s surface. This may allow for a tad bit of potassium to be evaporated off of the earth&#x27;s surface and returned in the form of rain. This would be a very small amount, not nearly the percentage of carbon and/or nitrogen that would fall from the skies in the for of precipitation. Another loss of potassium for soil use could be caused from harvesting of crops. This is due to the churning of soil as well as nutrients being ripped out of the ground from farm equipment such as plows. These issues can be easily fixed by just adding more safe amounts of fertilizer as well as letting the soil rest for a set amount of time so nutrients can be organically replaced. 
I hope everyone has a great week and I look forward to hearing your responses!
-Brodie
References:
Admin. (2023, October 13). Biogeochemical cycles - a detailed overview. BYJUS. https://byjus.com/biology/biogeochemical-cycles/

Mukherjee, S. (2023, February 2). Biogeochemical cycles: Definition, types, examples &amp; importance. Science Facts. https://www.sciencefacts.net/biogeochemical-cycles.html

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

Phosphorous (P)-Craig Nalley
Contains unread posts
CRAIG NALLEY posted Dec 9, 2024 5:42 PM
Class, 

I chose to do my research on Phosphorous (P) and I discovered it is quite an important element to not only plants and soil but also is part of the building blocks for DNA and RNA. Looking at the cycle P it is mainly added to soil through organic and inorganic materials such as manure, fertilizers, and decomposing plants( Southwest Research and Outreach Center, n.d.). P is released in aerosol form from of volcanic eruptions which rain down ash and lava which carry P. It is then transported through the water which allows it to leach into the soil and be used by plants and animals. P is removed from the soil by the plants of the earth, and then as mentioned in the cycle above is replaced by those dead plants as they decompose which is one transport method of this nutrient (Khan academy, n.d.). It is also transported through the food chain as animals eat plants that contain P and as they themselves are consumed P can remain in organisms or be broken down into waste and deposited back into the environment.



Rock is where we will find the largest reservoirs of P and as the rocks weather and are transported by leaching of the element into the water and soils where they become available for use to plants and animals. Reservoirs of P are known to be at the ocean, it is transported from oceanic homes by a process called uplift and with anything geology it is very slow. The average phosphate residence time in oceanic sediment is 20,000-100,000 years (Khan Academy, n.d.). I found this great video that covers the P Cycle very well also. This video is a great and easy to understand about the whole process of the P cycle https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Wzo-uFS7LUA.

Human impacts on the P cycle has had some pretty bad consequences. We use it in our fertlilizer and detergents which is released back into the environment in overwhelming amounts. Like the video says these additions to the soil create an excess in the soil of P and then runoff occurs(Moomoo math, 2019). Which in turn can create harmful algae blooms which are also very harmful to the ecosystem(Moomoo math, 2019). 

Khan Academy. (n.d.). The Phosphorous cycle. Khan Academy. https://www.khanacademy.org/science/ap-college-environmental-science/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:the-living-world-ecosystems-and-biodiversity/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:biogeochemical-cycles/a/ap-es-the-phosphorus-cycle

MoomooMath and Science. (2019). The Phosphorous Cycle. YouTube. https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Wzo-uFS7LUA

Openstax, C. (2016). Phosphorous Cycle. Khan Academy. Retrieved December 9, 2024, from https://www.khanacademy.org/science/ap-college-environmental-science/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:the-living-world-ecosystems-and-biodiversity/x0b0e430a38ebd23f:biogeochemical-cycles/a/ap-es-the-phosphorus-cycle.

Sanspree- Copper (Cu)
Contains unread posts
Dustin Sanspree posted Dec 12, 2024 10:09 PM
Copper (Cu) is a chemical element that is defined as being a soft, malleable, and ductile metal that has not only high thermal conductivity but also high electrical conductivity. Copper acts as a catalytic metal and helps to activate senzymes in plants in order to deal with iron. Copper activates the senzymes in plants that are involved in lignin biosynthesis and is also a crucial element to the photosynthesis process. Photosynthesis is an essential part of plant life where the plant respiration and metabolization of carbs and proteins takes place allowing the plants to thrive. Copper gets released into the atmosphere and into soils when fossil fuels are combusted and then begins to cycle through animals who eat plants containing the copper that was received from the soil and the copper is then returned once again into the soil as fertilizer from the animal. Copper is an incredibly important element as animals must have copper for body health, pigmentation, bone and wool growth, healthy nerve fibers, and white blood cell functioning. Copper is essential in aerobic respiration and is found in the last protein oxidative phosphorylation in the cytochrome c oxidase. The cytochrome c oxidase is what binds the O2 between a copper and an iron and helps these two elements work together in disparate processes. If copper is not found in the soil then the plants that are grown in that soil will not thrive and will cause a deficiency in animals who in turn will not have the copper in their manure to return to the soil for the process to begin again. 

Copper cycle - Wikipedia

Magnesium (Mg) and Its Role in the Soil Cycle
George Steward posted Dec 12, 2024 12:13 PM•565 Words
Hi Class,

This week, I dove into the world of magnesium (Mg)—an element that often flies under the radar but holds the reins to life itself. It’s the heartbeat of plant health, an unsung hero in soil ecosystems, and even a quiet force for our own well-being. Let’s dig in.

A Day in the Life of Magnesium: Its Cycle and Role in Soils
Magnesium starts its journey locked in rocks like dolomite and serpentine. Over time, weathering sets it free, allowing it to mingle in soils. Plants eagerly absorb it through their roots, and it powers chlorophyll—the very molecule that lets plants turn sunlight into life. Once plants and animals pass on, magnesium circles back into the soil, completing the loop.

Soils act as both the playground and the vault for magnesium. They buffer its availability, holding it in reserve while microorganisms and plant roots orchestrate its next act. However, magnesium can become scarce in sandy or highly leached soils—a challenge we often solve with simple amendments like magnesium sulfate (Epsom salt) (Real Simple, 2024).

Why Plants and Soils Crave Magnesium
Magnesium doesn’t just pull its weight in photosynthesis; it’s also the backstage manager for plant enzymes, nutrient transport, and carbohydrate metabolism. Without enough magnesium, plants can’t make energy or transport sugars effectively. The result? Stunted growth and yellowed leaves—hardly a plant’s finest moment.

For soils, magnesium enhances cation exchange capacity (CEC), boosting their ability to hold and release nutrients. It also improves soil structure, making it easier for water to seep in and roots to breathe.

From Plants to People: The Epsom Salt Connection
Magnesium has a beautiful duality—it nourishes plants, and it helps us too. In agriculture, Epsom salt is a game-changer. It provides a quick, soluble source of magnesium to revive struggling plants and restore chlorophyll production.

But its benefits don’t stop there. Epsom salt baths are famous for soothing sore muscles and relaxing the mind, all thanks to magnesium’s knack for stabilizing cellular processes. After all, who doesn’t need a little magnesium-fueled recharge now and then?

Magnesium in Natural Springs: Saratoga Springs, NY
Saratoga Springs, NY, offers a glimpse into magnesium’s natural abundance. Known for its mineral-rich waters, springs like Hathorn Spring No. 3 contain high concentrations of magnesium, giving the water its distinctive taste and therapeutic qualities.

I visited Saratoga Springs in 2012, directly after completing the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling running obstacle race spanning marathon distance up, down, and around the ski slopes. After such an intense physical challenge, rejuvenation was high on my list, and the springs delivered. Seeing the geysers shoot magnesium-rich waters into the air was a spectacular view of this element’s journey through the Earth, linking geology, ecology, and well-being.

geyser
[SOURCE] A photo from my trip to Saratoga Springs.

Why Magnesium Matters
Magnesium might not be as flashy as nitrogen or phosphorus, but its impact is undeniable. It fuels plants, supports soil health, and even helps us recharge. By understanding its cycle and role, we can manage soils more thoughtfully and cultivate a healthier relationship with the ecosystems that sustain us.

Looking forward to hearing your thoughts—seeing which elements you’ve chosen to explore and how they all work together!

Best,
George

References
Real Simple. (2024). Is Epsom salt good for plants? Retrieved from https://www.realsimple.com/home-organizing/gardening/epsom-salt-for-plants
Saratoga Springs Visitor Information. (n.d.). Saratoga Springs Natural Mineral Waters. Retrieved from https://www.saratoga.com/mineral-waters/
OpenStax. (n.d.). Nutritional Requirements of Plants. In Biology 2e. Retrieved from https://openstax.org/books/biology-2e/pages/31-1-nutritional-requirements-of-plants

Iron (Fe) - Johnston
Contains unread posts
Grace Johnston posted Dec 11, 2024 5:47 PM
Good evening, 

The element that I chose for this week was Iron (Fe). Iron is the crucial building block of steel, nourishment for plants, and helps carry oxygen through the body. It is a brittle hard substance that is classified as a metal on the periodic table. Iron is the most abundant of all metals, however it corrodes rapidly from exposure to moist air and high temperatures. It is the most common element in the earth&#x27;s crust by weight, along with the thought of Iron making up the earth&#x27;s core. Iron is also abundant in the sun and stars. Plants need iron to produce chlorophyll.



Iron 



With the large role that Iron plays throughout the earth it&#x27;s no surprise that it&#x27;s so abundant. Iron appears in two states, ferric iron (Fe(III)) in the oxidized state which is difficult to dissolve in ambient pH, and ferrous iron (Fe(II)) in the reduced state which is easily soluble. Light is the driving force for the iron cycle in the natural environment because of its ability to act as an energy source for microbial metabolism.

Iron in soils

Iron is typically released into the soil and ocean through the weathering of rocks or through volcanic eruptions. Plants will absorb iron through their roots to create chlorophyl, the plants require sufficient iron to preform photosynthesis. Bacteria also release iron back into the soil when they decompose animal tissue.

-Grace

Hello class!

I hope everyone is doing well as we near the holidays with this cold weather! Unfortunately, I&#x27;ve been sick with a fever so it&#x27;s been rough but i&#x27;m excited to hear more about these elements.

I chose the element nickel (Ni) which is a micronutrient that can be released into soil and water systems from natural sources or anthropogenic activities. When there is an improper amount of soil erosion caused by over increasing crop yields, micronutrients are being removed and destroyed. Without them, the plants cannot grow to their full potential and become deficient. Nickel is a good micronutrient for soils that are peaty, sandy or low in organic matter. Low concentrations have been known to be a functional component for plants. However, too much nickel has been said to cause toxic effects to the soil and also to human health. Cancers and other diseases have been linked to nickel toxicity. &quot;Therefore, soil pollution by Ni could soon be a global environmental concern and a significant issue for environmental protection.&quot; (Lemaitre, 2022)

In my research, I found that the term redox refers to a process where electron availability is measured in the soil. It is also called oxidation-reduction and can be a key factor in determining if the soil is healthy or not based on the its carbon cycle. (Rinklebe, 2017)

Redox cycle

Nickel also effects water as much as it does soil, affecting all organisms that live there. Biochar is a solid form of organic waste material and has been used in research to remedy the contamination. (El-Naggar, 2021)

biochar

Nutrient Cycling - Lithium (Li)
Contains unread posts
Kyle West posted Dec 11, 2024 12:08 AM
Hello Class!

For this week&#x27;s discussion, I chose to research the nutrient cycling and global biogeochemical cycle of Lithium (Li). Lithium is an alkali metal with an atomic number of 3. I also chose this element because its the name of a song from one of my favorite bands, Evanescence, but that&#x27;s besides the point. 

Apologies ahead of time for such a lengthy post. I spent a lot of time ensuring I captured all aspects of this discussion in hopes to generate thoughtful conversations. Previous to this discussion, I had no idea how big of a role Lithium played within our environment. 

Global Biogeochemical Cycle of Lithium



Figure 01. The global movement of lithium through Earth&#x27;s systems, highlighting the yearly flow of lithium in billions of grams. The estimates provided are rounded to whole numbers for simplicity.

The Biogeochemical Cycle

Terrestrial Rock Weathering

Lithium in rocks range from 1-60 mg/kg and is found in pegmatites, clays, and evaporites. Weathering releases lithium, which rivers carry in water and sediment. Most come from silicate minerals, moving 55-94 billion grams yearly in water and 107-157 billion grams in sediment. Some lithium is trapped in estuaries, but most reach the ocean, showing how lithium moves through Earth’s systems.

Atmospheric Transport

Lithium gets into the air from volcanoes, sea spray, and dust. Volcanoes release 11 billion grams yearly, sea spray adds 26-52 billion grams, and dust adds 38 billion grams. Rain and dry air return 60-117 billion grams of lithium to the ground, mostly on land. Sea spray and dust are the main sources of lithium in the air, displaying how it moves between the sky and Earth.

Terrestrial Biogeochemistry

Lithium isn’t needed by plants but is found in their tissues at 0.05-8 mg/kg. Around 24 billion grams of lithium move through land ecosystems each year, mostly without active processes. Unlike potassium, which builds up in soil under plants, lithium doesn’t gather in desert soils, showcasing its little interaction with plants.

Ocean &amp; Lithium Balance

Lithium enters the ocean thru rivers, vents, and from the air, adding 126-175 billion grams yearly. It becomes extracted through sedimentation and other processes, removing 99-207 billion grams. Most lithium stays in ocean sediments, while some sinks into Earth&#x27;s crust. With a seawater concentration of 0.179 mg/L, lithium input and output are balanced, and it remains in the ocean for 1-1.6 million years.

Soils&#x27; Role

Soils are important for the movement of lithium. When rocks and minerals break down, lithium is released into the soil. It moves through rivers, dust, and rain but doesn&#x27;t build up in soil like other nutrients, such as potassium. Lithium mostly moves through soil by weathering and mineral changes, meaning it doesn&#x27;t stay in one place for long.

Human Impacts

The demand for lithium has grown because it is used in electric car batteries, glass, ceramics, and more. Its production has increased 15 times over since the 1990s, mostly from Chile, Australia, and other countries. Additionally, coal contains lithium, especially in China. Burning coal releases lithium into the air and fly ash, which can pollute water. The use of coal has increased the amount of lithium in the environment, and reused coal ash still poses risks to water. Furthermore, oil and gas production creates wastewater with lithium, especially in places like Texas and Arkansas. This wastewater can spill and pollute water, adding a lot of lithium to the environment. 

As you may have noticed by now, Lithium affects humans in many ways. It’s found in soil additives that can impact plants and soil. Water used in geothermal energy may contain lithium, affecting water quality. The use of groundwater can change where lithium is in nature. Lithium is also used in medicine for mental health. Recycling lithium helps protect the environment as more lithium is needed.

W5 Reading Materials

After reviewing the reading materials from Week 5, Chapter 5.9 Nutrient Cycling Links in Soil Systems from the book Fundamentals of Soil Ecology provide a significant correlation to this week&#x27;s discussion topic. In summary, Symbiotic fungi, like ectomycorrhizal fungi, help break down minerals and release nutrients such as nitrogen, phosphorus, and calcium. These fungi affect soil nutrient cycles, which can also influence the movement of lithium in the soil. This process is important for how lithium moves and is available in ecosystems over time, just like other nutrients.

This concludes my discussion on the Nutrient Cycling and Global Biogeochemical Cycle for the element, Lithium (Li). Feel free to further this conversation with any questions or insight. 

Respectfully,

Kyle

Reference

Schlesinger, W. H., Klein, E. M., Wang, Z., &amp; Vengosh, A. (2021). Global biogeochemical cycle of lithium. Global Biogeochemical Cycles, 35, e2021GB006999. https://doi.org/10.1029/2021GB006999

Beryllium (Be)
Contains unread posts
Lindsey Taylor York posted Dec 10, 2024 5:10 AM
Beryllium (Be)-
Beryllium is a lightweight, brittle metal with unique chemical and physical properties, used in various industrial applications. While it is less discussed in environmental contexts than elements like phosphorus or sulfur, beryllium&#x27;s geochemical behavior and interactions with soil and water make it a noteworthy part of Earth&#x27;s cycles. Below is an exploration of the beryllium cycle and its role in the environment.

The Beryllium Cycle-
Beryllium has a relatively simple geochemical cycle because it does not have a gaseous phase. It primarily moves through the lithosphere, soils, and hydrosphere, interacting minimally with the biosphere. The graphic below illustrates the cycle:



Lithosphere

Beryllium originates in Earth&#x27;s crust, primarily in minerals such as beryl (Be₃Al₂Si₆O₁₈) and bertrandite (Be₄Si₂O₇(OH)₂).
Weathering of these minerals releases beryllium into soils and water.
Soils

Beryllium in soils binds strongly to clay particles and organic matter, making it relatively immobile.
Its availability and behavior depend on soil pH, with acidic soils promoting beryllium solubility.
Due to its low biological demand, beryllium is not a significant nutrient for plants or animals.
Hydrosphere

In water, beryllium occurs in trace amounts as dissolved ions or particles adsorbed onto sediments.
It enters aquatic systems via erosion, weathering, or industrial runoff.
Biosphere

Beryllium does not play a known biological role and is toxic at higher concentrations.
Organisms exposed to elevated levels of beryllium, often from industrial sources, can experience harmful effects.
Human Activities

Mining and refining of beryllium for industrial uses, such as aerospace, electronics, and nuclear reactors, contribute to its environmental release.
Improper disposal of beryllium-containing waste can contaminate soil and water.
The Role of Soils in the Beryllium Cycle-
Soils act as a key reservoir and regulator of beryllium&#x27;s environmental mobility:

Adsorption: Beryllium ions strongly bind to soil particles, especially those rich in clay and organic matter, limiting leaching and transport.
pH Sensitivity: In acidic soils, beryllium becomes more soluble, increasing its potential for mobility and toxicity.
Erosion and Deposition: Soil erosion can transport beryllium to aquatic systems, where it may settle in sediments.
Importance of Understanding Beryllium in Ecosystems-
Environmental Monitoring

Although beryllium is not biologically essential, its industrial use necessitates monitoring to prevent contamination.
Toxicity Concerns

Chronic exposure to beryllium, particularly through inhalation, causes berylliosis, a serious lung disease.
Environmental accumulation in soils and sediments can pose risks to ecosystems near industrial sites.
Industrial and Environmental Balance

Responsible mining, refining, and disposal practices are essential to minimize beryllium&#x27;s environmental impact.
By understanding the beryllium cycle and its soil interactions, we can better manage its industrial use and mitigate potential environmental risks.

References

Agency for Toxic Substances and Disease Registry (ATSDR). (2002). Toxicological Profile for Beryllium.Retrieved December 10, 2024.
Alloway, B. J. (2012). Heavy Metals in Soils: Trace Metals and Metalloids in Soils and their Bioavailability.Springer.
Chinese Academy of Sciences. (2022, February 9). Geochemical behavior of beryllium in the Earth&#x27;s crust revealed.Retrieved December 10, 2024, from https://english.cas.cn/newsroom/research_news/earth/202202/t20220209_300626.shtml
U.S. Geological Survey. (n.d.). Beryllium - Statistics and Information. Retrieved December 10, 2024.
Thank you and have a great week!

-Lindsey

W6: Nutrient Cycling/ Magnesium (Mg). Mckenzie Baker
Contains unread posts
McKenzie Baker posted Dec 10, 2024 7:15 PMLast edited: Tuesday, December 10, 2024 7:17 PM EST
Hello Class,

Magnesium (Mg) is an important component of the Earths nutrient cycle and is especially necessary for the health of plants and soil. As an essential part of chlorophyll, it affects plant growth and the stability of ecosystems, playing a role in photosynthesis. It travels through the lithosphere, hydrosphere, biosphere, and atmosphere as part of the magnesium cycle. The weathering of rocks such as Olivine and Dolomite releases magnesium ions (Mg2+) into soils and water, which is the source of magnesium. Mg2+ is taken up by plants from the soil, where it promotes the synthesis of chlorophyll and the activation of enzymes. Too much magnesium seeps into the surface and groundwater, eventually making its way into oceans. It aids in marine processes such as coral calcification there. The cycle is completed when decomposition returns magnesium to the soil from the death of plants and animals. Magnesium is stored in soils and transported through them. The pH, organic content, and cation exchange capacity of the soil all affect its availability. In summary, magnesium is a key component of the nutrient cycle that promotes soil health, plant growth, and ecosystem stability. It’s careful management is necessary to preserve ecological balance and soil fertility. 



References:

Bray, L. (n.d.). Magnesium Statistics and Information | U.S. Geological Survey. Www.usgs.gov. https://www.usgs.gov/centers/national-minerals-information-center/magnesium-statistics-and-information

Kaiser, D. E., &amp; Rosen, C. J. (2011). Magnesium for crop production. Umn.edu. https://extension.umn.edu/micro-and-secondary-macronutrients/magnesium-crop-production

Marek Protasewicz. (2024, July 3). How magnesium level affects the growth and health of corals - Reef Pedia. Reef Pedia. https://reefpedia.org/en/how-magnesium-level-affects-the-growth-and-health-of-corals/

Calcium (Ca) and the Soil Cycle
Contains unread posts
RACHAEL CARAWAY posted Dec 9, 2024 8:33 PMLast edited: Monday, December 9, 2024 10:18 PM EST
 

A diagram of a structure of a rockDescription automatically generated with medium confidence

Calcium (Ca) and Its Role in the Soil Cycle

Calcium (Ca) is an essential element in soil ecosystems, playing a critical role in plant growth, soil structure, and the health of soil organisms. While often overshadowed by nutrients like nitrogen and phosphorus, calcium is vital for ecosystem stability and nutrient cycling.

Calcium moves through Earth&#x27;s systems in the calcium cycle. In the lithosphere, it’s stored in rocks like limestone and gypsum, which release calcium into the soil through weathering (Coleman et al., 2004). Plants absorb calcium from the soil and return it through decay (White &amp; Broadley, 2003). In the hydrosphere, calcium enters water systems, supporting marine organisms like mollusks (Gissel-Nielsen, 1987). In the atmosphere, while calcium itself doesn&#x27;t exist in the gaseous phase, calcium compounds like calcium carbonate buffer acid rain. This helps reduce soil acidification caused by pollutants, maintaining a more stable and neutral soil pH (Coleman et al., 2004).

A diagram of calcium cycleDescription automatically generated

In soil, calcium helps bind particles into aggregates, improving soil structure, aeration, and water infiltration, which supports plant root growth (Coleman et al., 2004). It also strengthens plant cell walls and aids in the transport of other nutrients (White &amp; Broadley, 2003). Calcium carbonate in the soil helps neutralize acidity, maintaining a healthy pH for microbial activity and nutrient availability (Gissel-Nielsen, 1987).

Calcium is also essential for various soil organisms. Microfauna, such as nematodes and protozoa, rely on calcium to maintain the structural stability of their cell membranes and support vital cellular processes (Tedersoo et al., 2014). These organisms feed on calcium-dependent bacteria and other microorganisms, which are critical to nutrient cycling and soil health. Mesofauna, like mites and springtails, use calcium to build strong exoskeletons, aiding in organic matter decomposition and nutrient cycling (Coleman et al., 2004). Macrofauna like earthworms and ants use calcium for their exoskeletons and skeletons, and earthworms help improve soil structure by passing calcium-rich material through their digestive systems (Tedersoo et al., 2014).

Calcium also supports the decomposition process. It neutralizes acids produced during the breakdown of organic material, speeding up decomposition (Tedersoo et al., 2014). Calcium-rich plant material decomposes faster, releasing nutrients like phosphorus into the soil, where they can be used by plants (White &amp; Broadley, 2003; Gissel-Nielsen, 1987).

Calcium is crucial for maintaining soil health and ecosystem stability. It supports soil fauna, aids decomposition, and ensures the availability of essential nutrients. Proper calcium management can help improve soil productivity and long-term ecosystem resilience.

Ray

 

Refrences:

Coleman, D. C., Callaham, M. A., &amp; Crossley, D. A. (2004). Fundamentals of soil ecology. Elsevier Academic Press.
Gissel-Nielsen, G. (1987). The role of calcium in the soil ecosystem. Journal of Soil Science, 38(1), 13-22.
Tedersoo, L., Bahram, M., Toots, M., et al. (2014). Global diversity and geography of soil fungi. Science, 346(6213), 1256688.
White, P. J., &amp; Broadley, M. R. (2003). Calcium in plants. Annals of Botany, 92(4), 487-511.

Silicon (Si) - Rei
Contains unread posts
reimond romero posted Dec 10, 2024 8:34 PM
Hi Class,

What do country music, our weekly readings, and elements have in common. The answer is silicon. In Somewhere on a Beach by Dierks Bentley, a favorite of mine, the artist writes of being on a beach after a dramatic heartbreak. Well, that said beach is made of small crystals and particles of silicon. When someone wiggles their toes in the sand, they are doing so in small rocks that made been made over millions of years. 

Silicon isn&#x27;t just local to the beaches around the world. It can be found in over a quarter of Earth&#x27;s soil. Silicon currently makes up 28% of mineral soil by weight (Rutgers, 2017). That means that in nearly every industry from mining, crop production, and ranching will deal with this element. In Fundamentals of soil ecology our readings cover the impact of soil fauna to soil health. Animals with varying degrees in soil presence, directly impact other species and possibly whole ecosystems. These readings made me reflect on the importance of microorganisms and how we as a society view them. It seems that just because these creatures are small in size, we feel their impact is minimal. But this is absolutely not true. These animals use silicon when it burrows into the Earth, but other creatures will actually consume and use Silicon in their physical make up. This type of being is called a silicifiers and they are mostly found in aquatic species like sponges (Frontiers, 2023).



References

LEAD (Pb)--Campbell WK 6
Contains unread posts
Scott Campbell posted Dec 11, 2024 5:20 AMLast edited: Wednesday, December 11, 2024 5:21 AM EST
Hello everyone, j

This week I chose to take a deeper dive into the world of Lead (Pb). Lead appears as a dull, silvery-grey soft metal and has a atomic number of 82. It has been used since ancient times to make pewter (a gray alloy of tin and lead) and paint dated more than 2000 years ago. Today, the primary use for Pb is car batteries, ammunitions, lifting weights, and metal sheeting. Lead compounds are also used to dampen noise and effectively absorb electromagnetic radiation. This makes it useful for x-ray machines, nuclear reactors, and particle accelerators. Pb is sourced from mining a mineral called galena, which than smelted down. Almost half of all refined lead is from recycling. With increasing the use of Pb and other metalloids, the human and environmental impacts raise concerns. Soil is the major repositories for the accumulation of the anthropogenically source metalloids, to include Pb. As seen in the diagram below, lead from building and widespread use enters the soil. Atmospheric deposition occurs, which is the process where lead particles settle onto the earth surface after being released form anthropogenic processes. Ultimately impacting human and environmental health.  



For humans, Lead is toxic and is know to cause cancer. Pb effects the human nervous, digestive, and reproductive systems. Steps have been made to ban and limit the uses of Pb to limit negative exposures. For example, it is no longer used in gasoline and residential paints.

Cobalt (Co) Zane Steele
Contains unread posts
Zane Steele posted Dec 10, 2024 2:39 PM
Hello Class,

For this week’s discussion I chose the element Cobalt (Co). 

Cobalt is a ferromagnetic metal widely used in heat-resistant and magnetic alloys. Biologically, it is a trace element vital for the nutrition of ruminants like cattle and sheep and plays an essential role in human red blood cell maturation through vitamin B12 (The Editors of Encyclopedia Britannica, 2024). Although cobalt constitutes only 0.001% of Earth&#x27;s crust, it is found in minerals such as cobaltite, linnaeite, skutterudite, and erythrite, as well as in soils, plants, animals, ocean waters, and deep-sea crusts. Important for marine life, the abundance and distribution along with other scarce nutrients can increase the growth of microscopic plants known as phytoplankton. The result of the phytoplankton can influence the carbon cycles in the ocean and atmosphere.

A diagram of a life cycleDescription automatically generated

 

This models is showing the processes involved in the oceanic biological pump explaining the cycle of Cobalt, Zinc, and Iron in the Atlantic Ocean. This imagine comes from the article, The Biogeochemistry of iron, zinc and cobalt in the Atlantic Ocean: the Atlantic Meridional Transect and UK GEOTRACES sections, written by N.J Wyatt.

Finding a diagram for Cobalt cycle has been challenging. Cobalt’s cycle primarily involves interactions in the lithosphere, hydrosphere, and biosphere. The distribution is greatly influenced by geochemical and biological processes. Cobalt can enter the ocean systems or hydrosphere by rivers, deposits from the atmosphere and hydrothermal vents. Phytoplankton can absorb and deposit the element cycling the element in the hydrosphere.

fig. 2.21 &gt; Many metal ions end up in the cobalt crusts as “hitchhikers”. In the water, the metal ions attach themselves to iron oxide-hydroxide and vernadite molecules, and are then deposited onto the porous surfaces with them. © maribus

This image explains how different metal ions find themselves in the cobalt crusts and then attach themselves to iron oxide-hydroxide molecules and then get redistributed into different porous surfaces.

Cobalt in the lithosphere

Cobalt can be found in the crusts of the ocean floor, the rock-hard metallic layers form on the flanks of submarine volcanoes or seamounts. The weathering and erosion of the crust releases the element into the soil. Different factors including PH, microbial activity regulate its bioavailability to plants and microorganism.  Cobalt is most abundant when found with other minerals that from when magma or lave cools. The soils play a pivotal role in either retaining cobalt or facilitating its mobility through leaching.

Cobalt in the Hydrosphere

As mentioned before, Cobalt is often found in oceanic crusts. Found at depths of 600-7000 meters, porous and trap other metal elements similar to a filter. Cobalt can be introduced into the hydrosphere by way of runoff, leaching, atmospheric deposits, hydrothermal vents and submarine volcanoes. Serving as a nutrient for some microorganism marine life, contributes to metabolic activities in areas with minimal oxygen,

Cobalt in the Biosphere

Cobalt is a key component for vitamin B12, which is essential for DNA synthesis, and the production of red blood cells in animals and humans. Cobalt is a needed element for nitrogen fixation.

Cobalt in Soils

In soils as mentioned before, cobalt aids in nitrogen fixation, supporting plant growth and soil fertility. Microbial communities utilize cobalt for reactions and metabolic processes. Vitamin B12 can improve the health of plants and animals. The element can help improve seed germination, and growth. When Cobalt binds to the soil and organic matter it can help regulate it bioavailability, altering the PH, and changing the mobility.

Cobalt and humans

The element is often mined for industrial uses including alloys, pigments, and batteries. Working involved in the mining of the element are at risk of respirator and skin irritation. Isotopes of cobalt are used for cancer therapy and equipment sterilization. Cobalt is used to make B12, red blood cells and aid in the production of DNA.

 

Cobalt plays a somewhat understated but significant role in the biogeochemical cycle. While it is not as abundant or widely discussed as other elements, cobalt is crucial in processes such as nitrogen fixation and as a component of vitamin B12. However, its movement through the environment is more complex due to its trace status and varying bioavailability, influenced by factors like soil chemistry and microbial processes. Industrial and agricultural activities also impact cobalt&#x27;s presence in ecosystems, with both beneficial and potentially harmful effects on human health and the environment.

---

Hi Class,  

This week, I dove into the fascinating world of chlorine (Cl)—an element that quietly yet powerfully shapes ecosystems, water systems, and even life itself. Let’s explore how chlorine cycles through the Earth, its role in the environment, and its unique interplay with magnesium.  

**A Day in the Life of Chlorine: Its Biogeochemical Cycle**  
Chlorine begins its journey in the lithosphere, stored in minerals and rock formations. Through weathering and volcanic activity, chlorine is released into the atmosphere as hydrogen chloride (HCl). From there, it cycles through the hydrosphere and biosphere, transforming into forms like chloride ions (Cl⁻) and organochlorines.  

In the atmosphere, chlorine compounds interact with sunlight, forming reactive species that influence ozone depletion. When deposited back to the Earth through precipitation or sea spray, chlorine enters soils and aquatic systems. Microorganisms, such as fungi and bacteria, play a key role in dechlorination and organochlorine breakdown, completing the cycle (ResearchGate, n.d.).  

Here’s a diagram that beautifully illustrates chlorine’s complex journey through natural and anthropogenic pathways:  

![Chlorine Biogeochemical Cycle](attachment://Selected-chlorine-compounds-their-origin-and-biotransformation-a-schematic-overview.png)  

**Why Chlorine Matters for Soil and Life**  
In soils, chlorine contributes to nutrient cycling and plant health, although it’s needed in trace amounts. Chloride ions improve osmotic balance, which helps plants regulate water uptake. Chlorine’s presence also supports soil structure by interacting with minerals and other elements, such as magnesium.  

Magnesium, for its part, plays a vital role in plant health through photosynthesis. Together, magnesium and chloride ions work synergistically to maintain the ionic balance in plants, enabling efficient nutrient transport and energy production. Magnesium in chlorophyll powers photosynthesis, while chloride ions facilitate the opening and closing of stomata, ensuring gas exchange and water regulation. This partnership highlights the interconnectedness of nutrient cycles.  

**Chlorine in Natural Waters: Saratoga Springs, NY**  
Saratoga Springs is a perfect case study for chlorine’s role in natural water systems. Its mineral-rich waters contain chloride ions alongside magnesium and other elements, contributing to the springs’ therapeutic properties. When I visited Saratoga Springs in 2012 after completing the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT, these waters provided much-needed rejuvenation. Seeing the interplay of minerals firsthand—chlorine, magnesium, and others—was a vivid reminder of nature’s finely tuned cycles.  

**Why Chlorine’s Cycle Deserves Attention**  
Chlorine’s influence extends from maintaining soil health to shaping atmospheric chemistry. Its interactions with magnesium and other nutrients emphasize the intricate web of life on Earth. By understanding and managing chlorine’s cycle thoughtfully, we can support healthier ecosystems and maintain a sustainable balance.  

Looking forward to hearing your thoughts—especially how other elements you’ve chosen intersect with this story!  

Best,  
George

Hi Class,  

This week, I found myself diving into the world of chlorine (Cl)—an element that often flies under the radar but has a hand in shaping ecosystems, water systems, and even life itself. Let’s unravel its story together.  

**Chlorine’s Journey: A Biogeochemical Cycle with Purpose**  

Chlorine’s journey begins in the Earth’s crust, locked inside minerals and rocks. Through natural processes like weathering and volcanic eruptions, it’s released into the atmosphere as hydrogen chloride (HCl). From there, chlorine dances through air, water, and soil in its various forms—chloride ions (Cl⁻), organochlorines, and more.  

In the atmosphere, sunlight works its magic, breaking down chlorine compounds into reactive forms that influence the ozone layer. Eventually, chlorine returns to Earth through precipitation, entering soils and aquatic systems. Microbes like bacteria and fungi step in here, transforming chlorine into forms that keep the cycle moving (ResearchGate, n.d.).  

Here’s a helpful image that maps out chlorine’s natural and human-influenced pathways. It’s amazing how interconnected all these processes are:  

![Chlorine Biogeochemical Cycle](attachment://Selected-chlorine-compounds-their-origin-and-biotransformation-a-schematic-overview.png)  

**Chlorine’s Impact on Soils and Ecosystems**  

In the soil, chlorine doesn’t just sit around. Chloride ions, in particular, play a critical role in helping plants regulate water uptake and maintain osmotic balance. And chlorine’s partnership with magnesium is especially fascinating.  

Magnesium, as you might know, powers photosynthesis by anchoring the chlorophyll molecule. Chloride ions team up with magnesium by helping plants control stomatal openings—those tiny pores that manage gas exchange and water loss. It’s a perfect example of how nature’s elements work together to sustain life.  

**Chlorine in the Healing Waters of Saratoga Springs, NY**  

If you’ve ever visited Saratoga Springs, you’ve seen this element at work. The mineral-rich waters, bursting with chloride ions and magnesium, are a testament to nature’s chemistry. I stopped by the springs in 2012 after tackling the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a marathon-length obstacle race up and down the slopes. After pushing my body to its limits, soaking in those springs was exactly what I needed to recover. It was a moment that connected me, quite literally, to the power of these natural cycles.  

**Why Chlorine’s Cycle Matters**  

Chlorine might not get as much attention as nitrogen or carbon, but it’s a behind-the-scenes player in keeping ecosystems balanced. It supports plant health, influences soil chemistry, and even shapes atmospheric processes. And when we see it in harmony with elements like magnesium, it’s a reminder of how intricately everything is connected.  

I’d love to hear your thoughts—whether it’s about chlorine or the elements you chose to explore this week. How do they overlap or intersect with chlorine’s cycle?  

Best,  
George

Hi Class,

This week, I found myself diving into magnesium (Mg)—an element I’ve long appreciated for its role in soil and plant health—only to pivot mid-research when I realized someone else had already claimed it. So, I switched gears and chose chlorine (Cl), which, at first glance, felt a little less exciting. But this change turned out to be a blessing in disguise. Exploring chlorine opened up a whole new perspective, especially when I started connecting it to magnesium and its interactions within ecosystems. Let’s break it down.

**Chlorine’s Journey Through Earth’s Systems**

Chlorine has a remarkable biogeochemical cycle, weaving its way through air, water, and soil. It starts locked away in minerals and rocks, only to be released through weathering, volcanic eruptions, or human activities like industrial processes. From there, it enters the atmosphere as hydrogen chloride (HCl), where sunlight helps transform it into reactive forms. These reactions have a global impact, from influencing the ozone layer to affecting soil and water chemistry when chlorine returns to Earth through precipitation. 

Once in the soil, chloride ions (Cl⁻) play a crucial role. They don’t just hang out—they support plant growth by helping with water regulation and nutrient absorption. Microbes also get involved, transforming chlorine into various compounds that keep the cycle moving. It’s a fascinating interplay, as seen in this diagram from ResearchGate that maps chlorine’s natural and human-influenced pathways:

![Chlorine Biogeochemical Cycle](attachment://Selected-chlorine-compounds-their-origin-and-biotransformation-a-schematic-overview.png)  

**Chlorine and Magnesium: A Natural Partnership**

Chlorine’s story gets even better when you bring magnesium into the mix. Chloride ions help plants manage water and gas exchange, opening and closing their stomata—those tiny pores on leaves that control breathing. Meanwhile, magnesium anchors chlorophyll molecules, powering photosynthesis. Together, these elements create a harmony that drives plant growth and sustains ecosystems. It’s a perfect example of nature’s interdependence.

**Saratoga Springs: A Personal Connection**

One of my favorite memories involving chloride ions comes from a visit to Saratoga Springs, NY, in 2012. I had just finished the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling marathon-length obstacle race that tested every ounce of my strength and endurance. After the race, I was completely drained, and soaking in the mineral-rich waters of the springs was exactly what my body needed. These waters, rich in chloride and magnesium, offered a kind of natural recovery that felt like a gift from the Earth itself. Standing by the geysers, watching mineral-laden water erupt into the air, I couldn’t help but think about how elements like chlorine and magnesium work behind the scenes to shape both our health and the planet’s ecosystems.

**Why Chlorine Matters**

Chlorine might not get the spotlight like nitrogen or carbon, but it’s an essential player in keeping our ecosystems balanced. From influencing atmospheric chemistry to supporting plant growth and soil health, its impact is undeniable. And when you consider its interplay with elements like magnesium, it’s clear that chlorine is a quiet but powerful force. Understanding these cycles isn’t just fascinating—it’s a reminder of how interconnected life on Earth really is.

What are your thoughts? Have you found interesting connections between your chosen element and others? I’d love to hear about it!

Best,  
George

Hi Class,

Sometimes life throws a curveball, and this week was one of those moments. I had my heart set on diving deeper into magnesium (Mg)—a favorite element of mine—when I realized someone had already claimed it. So, I pivoted to chlorine (Cl). At first, it felt like settling for second best, but as I peeled back the layers, I realized this was a chance to uncover fascinating connections between these two elements, especially their roles in nature’s grand design.

**Chlorine’s Journey: From Rocks to Ecosystems**

Chlorine is far more dynamic than I gave it credit for. Its story begins deep within Earth’s crust, locked inside minerals and rocks. Over time, weathering, volcanic eruptions, and human activity release it into the atmosphere, where it takes on reactive forms like hydrogen chloride (HCl). These forms influence everything from the ozone layer to precipitation chemistry. When it eventually falls back to Earth, chlorine enters the soil as chloride ions (Cl⁻), where it gets to work.

In soils, chloride is more than just a bystander. It helps regulate water movement in plants, assists with nutrient absorption, and even supports microbial processes. This humble ion becomes a bridge between the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and biosphere, quietly sustaining life while rarely stealing the spotlight. 

**The Chlorine-Magnesium Connection**

What really sparked my curiosity was how chlorine interacts with magnesium. Chloride ions help plants manage water regulation, enabling them to open and close their stomata—tiny leaf pores that control breathing and water loss. Meanwhile, magnesium is the anchor of chlorophyll molecules, the engines of photosynthesis. Together, these elements form a partnership that powers plant growth and keeps ecosystems thriving. It’s a reminder that even the most unassuming elements are part of a larger, interconnected web.

**A Personal Encounter with Chlorine’s Power**

My appreciation for chlorine deepened in 2012 when I visited Saratoga Springs, NY. It wasn’t just any trip—I had just completed the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT, a marathon-length obstacle race that left me completely drained. Those natural springs, rich in chloride and magnesium, felt like pure magic. Standing by the geysers, watching mineral-rich water erupt into the air, I couldn’t help but think about how these elements cycle through the Earth, linking geology, ecology, and our own well-being. It was rejuvenation in its purest form, and it left an impression I’ll never forget.

**Why Chlorine Deserves Attention**

Chlorine might not have the charisma of elements like carbon or nitrogen, but its contributions are undeniable. It’s a key player in soil and water systems, supports plant and microbial health, and even shapes atmospheric chemistry. When paired with magnesium, it highlights nature’s knack for collaboration. Diving into chlorine’s story reminded me how even the smallest pieces of the puzzle matter—and how understanding them can help us cultivate healthier ecosystems.

What are your thoughts? Have you noticed similar connections between elements in your own research? I’m excited to hear how the pieces come together in your posts.

Best,  
George

Howdy Class,
Life threw me several curveballs this week and this was just another one of them. I had my heart set on magnesium (Mg)—a favorite element of mine—when I realized someone had already claimed it. Chlorine’s Journey: From Rocks to Ecosystems
Chlorine is far more dynamic than I gave it credit for. I equate it to a caustic chemical cleaner that is not to be mixed with ammonia. However, its story begins deep within Earth’s crust, locked inside minerals and rocks. Over time, weathering, volcanic eruptions, and human activity release it into the atmosphere, taking on reactive forms like hydrogen chloride (HCl). These forms influence everything from the ozone layer to precipitation chemistry. When it eventually falls back to Earth, chlorine enters the soil as chloride ions (Cl⁻), where it gets to work.
In soils, chloride is more than just a bystander. It helps regulate water movement in plants, assists with nutrient absorption, and even supports microbial processes. This humble ion becomes a bridge between the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and biosphere, quietly sustaining life while rarely stealing the spotlight.
The Chlorine-Magnesium Connection

This is a prominent feature of my favorite trees that love to live in and breath fog. Meanwhile, magnesium is the anchor of chlorophyll molecules, the engines of photosynthesis. It’s a key player in soil and water systems, supports plant and soil microbial health, and even shapes atmospheric chemistry, especially in the fog belts in the company of sequoia groves. When paired with magnesium, it highlights nature’s knack for collaboration. Howdy Class,

Life threw me a few curveballs this week, and one of them hit my plans square on the nose. I had my heart set on magnesium (Mg)—a favorite element of mine—only to find it had already been claimed. So, I pivoted to chlorine (Cl). At first, it felt like settling for second best, but as I peeled back the layers of this elemental onion, I realized this was an opportunity to explore how these two elements interact in nature’s grand design. That curiosity is why I’m here.

### Chlorine’s Journey: From Rocks to Ecosystems  
When I think of chlorine, my mind immediately goes to harsh cleaning products and that “do not mix with ammonia” warning. But its story is much deeper. Chlorine begins its journey deep within Earth’s crust, locked inside minerals and rocks. Over time, weathering, volcanic eruptions, and human activity release it into the atmosphere, where it transforms into reactive forms like hydrogen chloride (HCl). These forms influence everything from the ozone layer to the chemistry of precipitation. When it falls back to Earth, chlorine re-enters the soil as chloride ions (Cl⁻), ready to support life.

In soils, chloride is more than just a bystander. It helps regulate water movement in plants, supports nutrient absorption, and even enhances microbial processes. This humble ion becomes a bridge between the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and biosphere, quietly sustaining life without demanding attention.

### The Chlorine-Magnesium Connection  
Chlorine’s interaction with magnesium is what truly sparked my curiosity. Chloride ions help plants regulate water through their stomata—those tiny leaf pores that control breathing and water loss. This process feels especially vital when I think about my favorite trees thriving in fog, breathing it in like lifeblood. Meanwhile, magnesium anchors chlorophyll molecules, powering photosynthesis. Together, these elements form a partnership that keeps ecosystems thriving, showing how even the smallest interactions can have a big impact.

### A Personal Encounter with Chlorine’s Power  
My appreciation for magnesium—and its unwitting accomplice, chlorine—deepened in 2012 during a visit to Saratoga Springs, NY. It wasn’t just any trip. I had just completed the Spartan Ultra Beast at Mt. Killington, VT—a grueling, marathon-length obstacle race that left me physically drained. Those natural springs, rich in chloride and magnesium, were like pure magic. Standing by the geysers, I felt an almost primal connection to the Earth—a reminder of how its cycles sustain us in ways we rarely stop to notice. Watching mineral-rich water erupt into the air was a vivid glimpse of chlorine’s journey through geology, ecology, and even human well-being. It was rejuvenation in its purest form.

### Why Chlorine Deserves Attention  
Chlorine might not have the allure of nitrogen or carbon, but its contributions are undeniable. It’s a key player in soil and water systems, supports plant and microbial health, and even shapes atmospheric chemistry. In fog belts where sequoias reign, it works quietly alongside magnesium to highlight nature’s remarkable sense of collaboration. Diving into chlorine’s story reminded me how even the unassuming elements are part of a larger, interconnected web. When we look closely, we see how every piece matters.

What do you guys think? Have you noticed similar connections between elements in your research? Thanks for allowing me to reengage.

Best,  
George

Zinc (Zn), Amanda Kerrick
Contains unread posts
Amanda Kerrick posted Dec 10, 2024 8:54 PMLast edited: Tuesday, December 10, 2024 9:36 PM EST
Zinc is a micronutrient for soil. A smaller amount of micronutrients are needed for soil than macronutrient. &quot;Micronutrients are generally concentrated in the vascular solution and cytoplasm of plants, so they are present in organic residues and released during the initial physical fragmentation of decomposing organic residues.&quot; (Whalen, J. 2021)  Zinc is primarily stored in the Earth&#x27;s crust, where it is found in various minerals. Through weathering and erosion, zinc is released from these minerals into the soil and water. Soils are a critical compartment in the zinc cycle, as they act as both a source and a sink for zinc. Zinc can be bound to soil particles, especially clay minerals and organic matter. Zinc can also be present in a soluble and bioavailable form, adsorbed to soil particles, which plants can take up. When plants are consumed by herbivores, or when decomposed in the soil, zinc is passed along the food chain. In the biosphere (or living organisms) zinc is absorbed by animals, where it plays similar metabolic roles. Zinc is also released back into the environment when organisms die and decompose. Zinc can be transported through the hydrosphere in water bodies, such as rivers, lakes, and oceans, via surface runoff, atmospheric deposition, and groundwater. Human activities significantly impact the global zinc cycle. Mining and smelting of zinc ores release large amounts of zinc into the environment. Agricultural practices, such as the use of fertilizers and animal manures, can alter the distribution of zinc in soils, increasing the amount and potentially harming the soil and the microbes in it. Zinc is a vital element for plant growth. Deficiencies in zinc can lead to stunted growth and impaired plant functions, while excess zinc in soils can be toxic to plants and microorganisms. 

Image

References: 

Natural. ZINC. International Zinc Association. (n.d.). https://www.zinc.org/natural/

Whalen, J., Ziadi, N., Schoenau, J., Paré, M. C., &amp; Burton, D. (2021, November 25). 1.7: Soil Nutrient cycling. Geosciences LibreTexts. https://geo.libretexts.org/Bookshelves/Soil_Science/Digging_into_Canadian_Soils%3A_An_Introduction_to_Soil_Science/01%3A_Digging_In/1.07%3A_Soil_Nutrient_Cycling

Wk 6 - Argon

Angel Delgado-leach posted Dec 11, 2024 10:34 PM
Good evening Class,

Sorry about this post, if something doesn&#x27;t sound right or there are a bunch of questions, please feel free to ask. I am recovering from surgery and I&#x27;ve noticed my typing errors are extremely high. This week I decided to choose my favorite-sounding element, Argon. It has always sounded like a medieval name for some well-known rogue warrior or something. Hope it gave you a little laugh. Here are the specifics about Argon and its Biogeochemical Cycle:

Global Biogeochemical Cycle of Argon
1. The Atmosphere:
Argon is the third most abundant gas in Earth&#x27;s atmosphere, making up about 0.93%, following nitrogen and oxygen. As a chemically inert noble gas, it does not readily form compounds with other elements, allowing it to cycle through the atmosphere in its gaseous form without undergoing significant changes. The primary source of Argon in the atmosphere is volcanic activity, which releases Argon and other gases trapped in the Earth’s mantle.
Once in the atmosphere, Argon is dispersed by wind currents and remains in the atmosphere for long periods. Its inert nature prevents it from reacting with other gases, meaning it does not have an exit pathway like other gases such as oxygen or carbon dioxide. Instead, Argon simply diffuses through the atmosphere, circulating globally without forming compounds.
2. The Lithosphere and Soils:
Argon is primarily found in the Earth’s crust and mantle, where it is trapped in minerals and rocks. Volcanic activity releases Argon into the atmosphere, but it can also be absorbed into the soil. Due to its inert nature, Argon is chemically stable and does not participate in soil-based biogeochemical processes. Instead, it may accumulate in small amounts in soil pore spaces, particularly in regions affected by volcanic eruptions.
Although Argon does not interact significantly with soil components, it can diffuse through the soil, especially near the surface where concentrations are higher due to volcanic emissions. However, its movement through the soil is limited compared to other elements involved in active biogeochemical cycles, as its gaseous state and chemical inertness make it relatively immobile within the soil.
3. The Hydrosphere:
Argon is present in trace amounts in bodies of water, entering the hydrosphere primarily through diffusion from the atmosphere. However, its solubility in water is relatively low compared to gases like oxygen or carbon dioxide, meaning that the concentration of Argon in water remains minimal. Once dissolved, Argon can be carried by ocean currents or move through bodies of water, but it does not play a significant role in aquatic processes like oxygen or carbon dioxide.
4. The Biosphere:
Argon plays a negligible role in biological processes, as it does not participate in essential functions like photosynthesis or respiration. While it is present in trace amounts in the air that organisms breathe, it does not chemically interact with biological systems. As a result, Argon does not cycle through the biosphere in the same way as elements like carbon, nitrogen, or phosphorus and has no direct impact on the biological functions of plants, animals, or microorganisms.
Summary of Argon’s Biogeochemical Cycle
Volcanic emissions release Argon from the Earth’s mantle into the atmosphere, where it remains as an inert gas.
Once in the atmosphere, Argon is dispersed by wind currents and remains chemically unchanged.
In the lithosphere and soils, Argon does not participate in biogeochemical reactions but may be found in trace amounts.
In the hydrosphere, Argon is weakly soluble in water and exists in minimal amounts.
In the biosphere, Argon is present in the air, but it does not play an active role in biological processes.
Argon, being a stable and non-reactive element, moves primarily through the atmosphere, with limited interactions in other Earth compartments. It is an example of an element that, while abundant, does not participate in the active biogeochemical cycles that drive life on Earth.

Breakdown

Bio response

- Angel

References

Sulfur (S) - Little
Contains unread posts
Bradley Little posted Dec 9, 2024 1:04 AMLast edited: Friday, December 13, 2024 4:41 PM EST
Class,

The sulfur cycle is the biogeochemical process that moves sulfur through the atmosphere, hydrosphere, and lithosphere. The attached photo illustrates how sulfur cycles through these stages.  Atmospheric sulfur can be deposited into the earth&#x27;s surface through precipitation or dry deposition. In this respect, sulfur can be absorbed into plants through the soil and subsequently absorbed into animals who eat the plants. When living organisms die, sulfur is returned to the soil via decomposition. This sulfur is generally broken down by microorganisms and released into the soil as sulfates. Sulfur is released back into the atmosphere through several different means. The decomposition of organic matter plays a significant role and can be influenced by humans through the burning of fossil fuels or even agricultural activities like tillage and fertilizer use. 

Soils play a role in this cycle through microbial activity and decomposition. Microorganisms transform sulfur and are involved in key processes like soil oxidation and reduction; this allows plants to absorb sulfur that infiltrates soil via precipitation. Furthermore, decomposing organic matter is stored in the soil prior to conversion, soil acting as a food bank for the microbial process. Soils act as a buffer in this process as well, regulating deposits from precipitation or decomposition by facilitating plant uptake and directly influencing leaching. Soil is a reservoir for nutrients, including sulfur, because soil receives, converts, and stores sulfur produced by organic matter and inorganic processes.

There are several reservoirs in the sulfur cycle. It is understood that sulfur exists in some form in the atmosphere, biosphere, and hydrosphere. In the atmosphere, sulfur exists as sulfur dioxide and aerosols. Sulfur in this reservoir generally originates from natural processes and the burning of fossil fuels. It has a residence time of several days, primarily depending on precipitation.

Sulfur residence times vary from day to day in the biosphere. Soils contain organic and inorganic sulfur, and microbes transform and cycle within the reservoir. A particularly interesting aspect of the biosphere is the storage of sulfur in rocks. Weathering and erosion release sulfur, either back into the biosphere or into the atmosphere via a chemical reaction, acid rain. The residence time in sedimentary rocks can be millions of years.

Oceans are significant reservoirs containing sulfate ions, with marine life playing a role in cycling. However, sulfur also exists in freshwater from leaching or falling precipitation containing dissolved sulfates. Sulfur can remain in the hydrosphere for long periods. Sulfur primarily exists as stable and soluble ions and does not undergo any significant chemical adaptations. A no-brainer is dilution. Oceans are vast, so sulfur compounds exist in the water column for extended periods until they are removed through biological uptake or sedimentation.

Earlier in the post, I discussed the natural and anthropogenic cycles. These are the naturally occurring or human-caused ways that sulfur moves through the different reservoirs. Anthropogenically speaking, coal releases large amounts of sulfur, as does industry, agriculture, and urban development.

Sulfur Cycle

The picture above shows how sulfur is released into the atmosphere both naturally and by human activity. Sulfur can be deposited back into the biosphere by either dry deposition (accumulation of material) or wet deposition (precipitation). Through these forms of deposition, sulfur enters soils and water to be transferred by microorganisms or chemical reactions.

Brad

References:

Coleman, D.C., Callaham, M.A., Crossely Jr., D.A. (2020). Decomposition and Nutrient Cycling. In: Fundamentals of Soil Ecology. Retrieved from https://ebookcentral.proquest.com/lib/apus/reader.action?docID=5124551&amp;ppg=182.

Zehnder, A.J.B., Zinder, S.H. (1980). The Sulfur Cycle. In: The Natural Environment and the Biogeochemical Cycles. The Handbook of Environmental Chemistry. Retrieved from https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-662-24940-6_6

Potassium (K) Henson
Contains unread posts
Brodie Henson posted Dec 9, 2024 11:27 AMLast edited: Monday, December 9, 2024 12:10 PM EST
Hello everyone,

For this week&#x27;s discussion we have been tasked to conduct a further investigation into how the element potassium moves through the biogeochemical cycle. Below are a couple of standard Biogeochemical cycle diagrams that I will be utilizing for this discussion.

cycle

Mukherjee, S. (2023, February 2). Biogeochemical cycles: Definition, types, examples &amp; importance. Science Facts. https://www.sciencefacts.net/biogeochemical-cycles.html

Cycle

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

As you can see, I chose diagrams that did not contain very much water in the cycle. I chose this because potassium is not as abundant on earth or in the atmosphere as nitrogen and carbon. This is more of a sedimentary cycle. Let&#x27;s break down the diagrams. To begin with, potassium can be introduced directly into the soil via fertilizer or animal droppings. Plant residue can also place potassium directly into the soil. Once potassium is absorbed into the soil, decomposition begins via bacteria and fungi to break down the potassium for soil use. Process turns into a cycle when animals eat the plants and other organisms that have utilized the broken-down potassium. The cycle then goes back to the animal droppings. Some losses from the soil include run off for surface water on the earth&#x27;s surface. This may allow for a tad bit of potassium to be evaporated off of the earth&#x27;s surface and returned in the form of rain. This would be a very small amount, not nearly the percentage of carbon and/or nitrogen that would fall from the skies in the for of precipitation. Another loss of potassium for soil use could be caused from harvesting of crops. This is due to the churning of soil as well as nutrients being ripped out of the ground from farm equipment such as plows. These issues can be easily fixed by just adding more safe amounts of fertilizer as well as letting the soil rest for a set amount of time so nutrients can be organically replaced. 
I hope everyone has a great week and I look forward to hearing your responses!
-Brodie
References:

Wegmeyer, H. (2024). New Resource: Potash Video &amp; Potassium Cycle poster. Nutrients for Life. https://nutrientsforlife.org/new-resource-potash-video-potassium-cycle-poster/

Phosphorous (P)-Craig Nalley
Contains unread posts
CRAIG NALLEY posted Dec 9, 2024 5:42 PM
Class, 

I chose to do my research on Phosphorous (P) and I discovered it is quite an important element to not only plants and soil but also is part of the building blocks for DNA and RNA. Looking at the cycle P it is mainly added to soil through organic and inorganic materials such as manure, fertilizers, and decomposing plants( Southwest Research and Outreach Center, n.d.). P is released in aerosol form from of volcanic eruptions which rain down ash and lava which carry P. It is then transported through the water which allows it to leach into the soil and be used by plants and animals. P is removed from the soil by the plants of the earth, and then as mentioned in the cycle above is replaced by those dead plants as they decompose which is one transport method of this nutrient (Khan academy, n.d.). It is also transported through the food chain as animals eat plants that contain P and as they themselves are consumed P can remain in organisms or be broken down into waste and deposited back into the environment.



Rock is where we will find the largest reservoirs of P and as the rocks weather and are transported by leaching of the element into the water and soils where they become available for use to plants and animals. Reservoirs of P are known to be at the ocean, it is transported from oceanic homes by a process called uplift and with anything geology it is very slow. The average phosphate residence time in oceanic sediment is 20,000-100,000 years (Khan Academy, n.d.). I found this great video that covers the P Cycle very well also. This video is a great and easy to understand about the whole process of the P cycle https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Wzo-uFS7LUA.

Human impacts on the P cycle has had some pretty bad consequences. We use it in our fertlilizer and detergents which is released back into the environment in overwhelming amounts. Like the video says these additions to the soil create an excess in the soil of P and then runoff occurs(Moomoo math, 2019). Which in turn can create harmful algae blooms which are also very harmful to the ecosystem(Moomoo math, 2019).

Sanspree- Copper (Cu)
Contains unread posts
Dustin Sanspree posted Dec 12, 2024 10:09 PM
Copper (Cu) is a chemical element that is defined as being a soft, malleable, and ductile metal that has not only high thermal conductivity but also high electrical conductivity. Copper acts as a catalytic metal and helps to activate senzymes in plants in order to deal with iron. Copper activates the senzymes in plants that are involved in lignin biosynthesis and is also a crucial element to the photosynthesis process. Photosynthesis is an essential part of plant life where the plant respiration and metabolization of carbs and proteins takes place allowing the plants to thrive. Copper gets released into the atmosphere and into soils when fossil fuels are combusted and then begins to cycle through animals who eat plants containing the copper that was received from the soil and the copper is then returned once again into the soil as fertilizer from the animal. Copper is an incredibly important element as animals must have copper for body health, pigmentation, bone and wool growth, healthy nerve fibers, and white blood cell functioning. Copper is essential in aerobic respiration and is found in the last protein oxidative phosphorylation in the cytochrome c oxidase. The cytochrome c oxidase is what binds the O2 between a copper and an iron and helps these two elements work together in disparate processes. If copper is not found in the soil then the plants that are grown in that soil will not thrive and will cause a deficiency in animals who in turn will not have the copper in their manure to return to the soil for the process to begin again.
